THE RED PAGE.
MARY GILMORE’S VERSES.
DAY gives her radiant face, whereon,
Love’s red shows ever love’s unrest,
But Night, her clinging arms and all
The wonder-whiteness of her breast.
THE moonlight flutters from the sky
To meet her at the door,
A little ghost, whose steps have passed
Across the creaking floor.
And rustling vines that lightly tap
Against the window-pane,
Throw shadows on the white-washed walls
To blot them out again.
The moonlight leads her as she goes
Across a narrow plain,
By all the old, familiar ways
That know her steps again.
And through the scrub it leads her on
And brings her to the creek,
But by the broken dam she stons
And seems as she would speak.
She moves her lips, but not a sound
Ripples the silent air ;
She wrings her little hands, ah, me 1
The sadness of despair !
While overhead the black duck’s wing
Cuts like a flash upon
The startled air, that scarcely shrinks
Ere he afar is gone.
And curlews wake, and wailing cry
Cur-lew 1 cur-lew ! cur-lew !
Till all the Bush, with nameless dread
Is pulsing through and through.
The moonlight leads her hack again
And leaves her at the door,
A little ghost whose steps have passed
Across the creaking floor.
HIS eyes looked into mine,—
O, look that made me wise !
I hid my own, although
My world lay in his eyes.
I turned away my face,
My breath came like a sob,
And to his heart my own
Gave answer, throb for throb.
I could not raise my eyes,
Lest he should read therein,
I trembled, woman-wise,
And woman-waked within.
&
SING sweet, little bird, sing sweet, sing sweet!
Throb little heart, and beat!
Swell little throat of the mellow note !
Sing sweet, O bird, sing sweet t
Love came to me yesternight, O bird,
Came with a kiss and a whispered word,
Crowned me (sing !) with a golden ring—
Sing to me, sing, O bird !
Love came to me yesternight (sing on !)
Dear were his eyes where the love-light shone,
Dear was his mouth in its burning drouth :
Sing, sing, O bird, sing on !
MY little love is sleeping
Under the orchard tree,
Awhile she has been weeping,
But now she dreams of me.
And round her mouth there hovers
A tender little smile,
Aud Love laughs at two lovers
Who had been cross awhile.
TIME waits not on our idle days,
The balance ever forward swings ;
Life goes impatient while we wait,
And takes again the hour He brings.
Love-red, and quick, he faces us—
Shall we turn back and bid him go
To wait till Time has paled his cheek
And set his bounding pulses slow?
Come then, Beloved, when the day
Sinks, splendid, to the watching West;
Time will not wait for us, then come , .
The hours shall die upon my breast,
YOU are there
An’ I am here ;
What’s it matter ?
Tell me, dear!
Side by side,
Or far away,
Love can ever
Find the way.
So I send
The Lovin’-Word,
Spoken soft, as
Though you heard.
Just a word
Across the sea,
Lightly whispered :
“You an’ me !”
Send it back,
O, sweet an’ true,
Just as whispered:
“Me an’you!”
MY hand in thine,
Thy hand in mine,
God gave me thee,
God made me thine.
Go not away,
O heart, my heart;
I have no soul
From thee apart.
I have no life
Thou dost not share,
No joy in hope,
No balm in prayer.
Stay with me, sweet—
The sad hours go ;
Stay' with me, sweet,
Nor leave me so.
Holds life, dear heart.
No more than this :
A look, a word,
A touch, a kiss ?...
Draw down the blind,
Shut out the day,
Bring tapers tall,
Fold hands, and pray.
DRAW down the blind,
The end is come ;
The eyes are wide,
The lips are dumb.
The loving kiss
No more shall thrill,
The loyal heart
At last is still.
Draw down the blind,
Shut out the sun ;
The journey ends
At half-begun.
LOVERS.
YOU ’RE a-wanting me
I’m a-wanting you,—
What’s us waiting for?
Life is wearin’ through.
What’s us waiting for,
Peaky-faced an’ sad ?
Life ain’t watchin’ round,
Hopin’ we 'll be glad.
Ti ne’s no good for us,
Life’s got nothin’ sweet:
Me a-mnpin’ here,
You across the street.
What should come between,
Partin’ of us two,
Y >u a-wantin’ me,
Me a-wantin’ you ?
Kiss me in the mouth,
Kiss me lovin’-fair,
What we 're waitin’ for
No one else ’ll care.
Kiss rne in the mouth,
Kiss me straight an’ true,
You a-wantin’ me,
Me a-wantin’ you.
SWEET-HEARTIN’
IT’S gettin’ hits o’ posies,
An’ feelin’ mighty good,
A-thrillin’ ’cause'she loves you,
An’ wonderin’ why she should.
An’ stopping sort of sudden
Because you ’re full of thought,
An’ quick with res’less feelings
That make life seem too short.
It’s feelin’ ’s if she’d loved you
Before the world was made,
An’ that she’ll keep on lovin’
Till all our debts are paid.
As if there’s nothing mattered,
As if all things were good,
As if the Lord was lookin’
An’ sorter understood.
It’s feelin’ kind an’ gentle
To ev’rvthing that’s weak,
An’ doin’ just such actions
As seem to nearly speak—
Such acts as women reckons
A man will always do
When he’s in love with someone,
An’ chat someone is you.
QUARRELIN'.
US is out, her an me,
Moonin’ round the square,
Tryin’ how to make, believe
As us does n’t care.
Us is out, her an’ me,
Just like fools,
Bustin’—achin’ fora word—
Obstinit as mules.
Us is out, her an' me,
Out—an’ all f’r what—
Things as ain’t the worth o’ that,
When you ’re love-forgot.
Us ain’t made to snap an’ snarl—
Us that’s one an’ two ;
Us ain’t made to jar an’ fret—
Us, that’s me an’ you.
Us ain’t made to go apart—
Kiss me lovin’-true—
Us that’s touchin’ mouth to mouth—
Us, that’s me an’ you.
MARRI’D.
IT 'S singin’ in an’ out
An’ round about the place,
Here an’ there, an’ up an’ down,
An’ feelin’ full o’ grace.
It’s rollin’ up your sleeves,
An’ whit’nin’ up the hearth,
An’ scrubbin’ out the floors,
An’ sweepin’ down the path ;
It’s bakin’ tarts an’ pies,
An’ shinin’ up the knives,
An’ feelin’ ’s if some days
Was worth a thousand lives.
It’s watchin’ out the door
An’ watchin’ by the gate,
An’ watchin’ down the road, —
An’ wond’rin’ why he’s late.
An’ feelin’ anxious like,
For fear there’s something wrong,
An’ wond’rin’ if he’s kep’,
An’ why he takes so long.
It’s cornin’ back inside,
An' sittin’ down a spell,
To sorter make believe
You ’re thinkin’ things is well;
It’s gettin’ up again,
An’ wand’rin’ in an’ out,
And feelin’ wistful like,
Not thinkin’ what about.
An’ flushin’ all at once,
An’ smilin’ jes’ so sweet,
An’ bein’ real proud
The house is nice an’ neat;
An’ feelin’ awful glad,
Like them that watched Silo’m—
An’ everything because
A man is cornin’ Home.
BABY.
KISSIN’, kissin’, kissin’,
Lordy ! ain’t he sweet
Rosy roun’ de mouf,
Rosy roun’ de feet!
Jes’ tuk out de water :
Fresher dan de rose ;
Kiss him little fis’s,
Kiss him little toes.
Ain’t no babby like him,
Couldn’t neber he—
Him dat all is mine,
Him dat's kissin’ me.
One Australian Girl .
MARY GILMORE’S verses, printed m
The Bulletin. The Worker (Q.), The Queens-
lander, The Clipper, and many other Australian
papers, have gained a wide popularity. Those
reprinted on this page exhibit her talent at its
best poetically speaking. In other examples, where she
strikes the note of social sympathy, she appeals to a
numerous class of readers with equal force and ettect.
Her work is often hasty—the rough embodiment of a
mood or an idea, quickly seized and crudely set
down. Yet sincerity and passion give the verse a rare
poignancy. Such a little homely gem as “ Marri d is
unique. The theme is commonplace ; even the vision is
commonplace ; but the power to express the vision at
that pitch and in that way is memorable.
There are other
MAR? GILMORE.
pieces in which a finer
phrase meets a finer
emotion. The emotion
is usually lyric a
momentary flash, a
natural cry, owing
little to “fundamental
brain-work.” ,It is the
eye, the imagination
of the Celt once more.
Mary Gilmore’s father
was. a Scottish High-
lander, “very High-
land ” ; and the char-
acter of her verse is
hereditary, attenu-
ated by admixture
and coloured often by
personal experience.
The work of such
a quick, alive, glanc-
ing, scintillating mind
is naturally full of unconscious echoes. Occasionally
one is reminded of the style or matter of Heine or
another. Mary Gilmore’s poetical value issues, in tact,
from instinct and temperament, and not from education,
though she has been well educated in the conventional
sense. The heat, the glow, the touch of phrase and
tingle of feeling that differentiate her work from mere
journal-verse—these she owes to her ancestry. Her work
is original in fibre.
Twice or thrice, as in “A Little Ghost,” it holds an
atmosphere of mystery, that indefinably haunting quality
which is given and received by no conscious artifice or
effort. At other times, M try Gilmore writes what might
be called epigrams of emotion —stanzas of four or eight
lines in which the words set themselves without premedi-
tation to the tune of feeling :
We did not think it, did we,
Looe had us in a net ?
Now w& may not remember
Nor yet may we forget.
A slight verse and simple ; yet there is something in the
saying that is noteworthy.
The story of Mary Gilmore’s life is remarkable.
She was born near Goulburn, N.S.W. Her father was a
Cameron, and her earliest and best-known verses appeared
over the initials “M.J.C.” Going to country schools at
Wagga and elsewhere, she became a pupil teacher
under N.S.W. Education Department at the age of
sixteen, after passing a brilliant examination. She
was given herchoice of appointment in Sydney or any
of half-a-dozen country towns, and chose Wagga as being
near her own people, and because she had gone to school
there. “I was only a gawky country girl dressed in a
home-made frock when I entered the school to begin
work, and when I went eagerly up to an old school-
fellow (she wore a watch, and her skirt was weighted
down with pennies instead of shot, and afterwards she mar-
ried a clergyman and wore glasses) she coldly gave me
the tips of her fingers and turned away to another girl,
whose dress had six pearl buttons where mine had one.”
A successful career as a teacher followed—at Silverton,
and later at Sydney—until Miss Cameron came within
the circle of the New Australia movement, and went to
Paraguay as a colonist.
“ I had a lot of fever in the colony ; and people said I
worked too hard. I was married there, and my child was
born in the neighbouring town. The nurse—an ex-
colonist—was drunk from before his birth till a week
after, and I did not write to the colony for help because I
did not care to expose her, thinking no one but I knew
she drank. Only for the kindness of a chance visitor I
would have died, and only for the nurse’s little girl of
eight 1 would have gone often all day, while in bed, with-
out food. A month before my child was born I went into
Villa Rica. When my child vvasitwo weeks old I returned
to the colony, and when he was sixteen days old I was at
the washtub—this because I knew every woman in the
colony had as much work to do as ever she could manage
without mine being added. I remember the longing I
had, when my child was born, toisend a wire to my hus-
band, but I was afraid of the expense—it would have
seemed like taking bread out of the children’s mouths,
and I sent only a letter. But I never think of that tele-
gram without a feeling of sadness. I was away and the
baby was come, and I had to wait for the chance of the
mail being sent for from the colony for my husband to
know.
“ I remember how we made a cot for the child. Will
split the timber—lapacho, a hard wood and a heavy—with
maul and wedges, then he trimmed down the splintered
sides with an adze, after which he split the pieces with
an old saw. He was weeks over the work—the wood was
so hard—then with a plane ancfa spokeshave he smoothed
all the surfaces. It was a bit out of plumb in some of its
legs, but it would take a beautiful polish. The idea of
the polish was a joy to us, but we had neither
the time nor the material to polish with, so the
joy never had a foundation in fact. And though
the cot was finished, 1 never put the, boy to bed
in it except by day. It seemed so cold-hearted to put
him away in the night, and we thought he might feel
lonely. ‘ Besides,’ his father said, ‘he is such a little
fellow.’ We made also a whatnot, but it was sawn out
with a crosscut—l was the architect, Will was the execu-
tive. It was a thing of beauty, actually had a bead on
its front edges, and wouldn’t go skew-wise if it stood
away from the wall. In fact, it could on occasion stand
alone ; but we kept it nailed to the wall for security and
safety’s sake.
“Some time after, we severed our connection
with the Gosme colony, and while Will was in Patagonia
I went into Villa Riea to live, renting the same
little house where my baby was born. The evenings were
pleasant, for it was May, and I used to sit in thg verandah
at sundown and even till nearly dusk. After a week of
this my landlord and his six daughters came one evening
to see me and remonstrate. ‘lt wasn’t safe,’ they gave
me to understand ; ‘ there were bandidos about.’ ‘ Would
I go indoors before sundown and fasten up the house ?'
And he hauled in the trunk of a small orange tree to add
to the security of .my door. This door opened in the
middle, had a holt top and bottom, and a double-lock in
the middle. He fastened the bolts, double locked the
door, and then jammed the sapling against the middle of
it. One of the daughters, to make things more secure,
brought in a long board ; this was propped in against the
upper part of the door. Some weeks afterwards, finding
the door rattle in a high wind, I examined the hinges.
There were no screws in them, only nails, old rusty nails,
andl about two to each hinge. I smiled. The thing was
so like Paraguay.
" After that I ceased to barricade the door in the
middle, and did it at the sides —not that it mattered
much, except as a precaution against it being blown in.
To this house used poor Larry Petrie to come after his
seventeen hours duty at the Railway Station. ‘ I.arry,
lurry, you foolish fellow,’ I used to say— 1 why don’t you
goto bed instead of coming up here?’' ‘Bed!’ he would
reply—‘ how can I sleep ? The carpenters work beside
me, and the lampmen are in and out all the time ; and so
ic’s a case of get drunk, or come and talk to yju,’ and he
would stay and talk till his nerves quietened, and then
tramp wearily hack to his little lamp-room for an hour’s
sleep. Twice he prevented a collision ; once he saved a
train from fire, and died at last with his ribs through his
lungs, his life given for that of the stationmaster’s child.
. J*
“ Will came up to Villa Rica from Patagonia,
intending we should all go down as soon as the summer
came. We went from Villa Rica to Sapucay (the Railway
works) and lived there two or three months. Then to
Buenos Aires for a few months. Here Will went shearing
in the Argentine—such an experience! In November
nearly three years ago we left for Gallegos, in South Pata-
gonia. From Gallegos the Falklands lie #ast 300 miles, so
you may' guess how cold it was. Magellan’s Strait is two
days’ ride south. On the way south an epidemic of
measles broke out; my little son took the complaint, and
inflammation of the lungs set in.
“There was no milk, water ran short; it was thick
and purple ; we were on a waterless coast in an old ship,
whose engines might blow up any time, whose condenser
only condensed enough for the first class, and used to
break out in leaks every second day. The Doctor suf-
fered from melancholia and loss of memory ; he was
kindness itself, and used invariably to pray over my little
one, but he dared not trust himself to do anything
beyond taking his temperature. When I would beg of him,
‘ Doctor, is there anything can be done?’ he would say,
‘ We can only wait, we can only pray ’—and sometimes,
‘He is too weak for anyone to do anything.’ We were 31
days on a voyage that should only have taken six or
seven. The Pacific boats do it in two-and-a-half. Yet
Billy lived.
“In Patagonia I was for a time governess to, I
think, the most awful girl that ever lived. She was six-
teen, looked eighteen, and called herself fourteen. She
knew everything ever known, and what I told her one
day she told me back in a day or two as entirely her own.
Among the items of her own peculiar knowledge was that
‘ she knew the woman who made all the lace for Queen
Victoria’s wedding.’ After tnree months governessing
I went from the Estancia to live in Gallegos. Here I was
advised to take up English teaching. I demurred on the
ground that I could not speak Spanish. 1 Oh, but you
are such a good English scholar,’ was the reply, ‘ you
should do well at it.’ It seemed a queer reply and a
queer reason for success, but there was a lot in it; so I
took a house and began teaching.
“My first pupil was Don Vicente Canb, of the Bank of
Argentina, nephew to a gentleman whose name I forget,
but who was one time Plenipo. for Argentina in London :
a fine scholar and a brilliant writer. Don Vicente lent
me his uncle’s translation of “ Henry V,” which was really
Shakespeare in another language. The first month of
teaching was dreadful. I felt such a fraud, fori hadn’t the
use of twenty words of the language, though with the dic-
tionary I could read fairly well. As the lesson hour came
round my knees used to shake with apprehension lest
Don Vicente wouldn’t come, and my stomach grew sick
in dread of the lesson should he come. Whichever way
it was, it was awful. Yet in a week Senor Cane brought
me other puDils, and the Deputy Governor sent me more.
Indeed, in a little while, I had to take a more convenient
house, and had as many pupils as I could manage. And
they were so kind, all of them. I got on splendidly with
my Spanish, and they were kind enough to say they did
well with the English—and indeed, after a while, I think
they did, for I tried hard enough. Moreover, they all
expressed their sorrow and disappointment when I told
them I was leaving. _ . .
“ Half the lessons I gave at my own house, the rest at
the houses of pupils, and many and many a day the wind
blew my little boy off his feet as we hurried along to a
lesson, or dashed me against a telephone post or a fence.
The wind is incessant. Except in the dead of winter, when
all is frozen, it never ceases. I have been half-an-hour
going 100 yards, and holding on to fences all the way,
and I have seen men—who wear no skirts—have to put
their hands to the ground to maintain their balance. My
little boy I couldn’t leave at home, as he was too young,
and a servant was out of the question. Only soldiers
wives were available, and they would only stay till they
had pilfered all they wanted ; in addition to which none
of them could cook, and all were dirty and immoral.
«**
“During the time I was in Gallegos, war was
threatened between Argentina and Chili. The disputed
territory was billy 60 miles away. It was a time of great
excitement. Soldiers drilled daily hack and front of my
house, beginning at 2 a.to. —the days were long and there
was little darkness at night ; the pigeons were taken out
and trained, and the wildest rumours prevailed. Ohilenos
were openly arrested in the streets and locked up, and l
was frankly to)d that unless .we had our passports Will
would be imprisoned. He was away and I was alone- he
was fifty miles off at Estancia Condor, initiating machine
shearing at the station. There was no direct mail to him,
and if I wanted to telephone or telegraph 1 would have
to do so via Chili, as the lines ran to and from runta
Arenas, and when Chili chose that we should get news of
the negotiations she opened the lines ; when she Qian D
choose she closed them. _ . , . ..
“ We had no Consul, but the English officials in the
Chilian English Bank swore to defend the bank and the
English women with their lives. There were afewro -
beries, and I slept every night with an axe, two or tnre
carving knives, and a siren whistle under my pi o ,
some of my pupils impressing upon me never to open my
door at night, should a knock come, till I knew who was
outside. Indeed, I was thought to be a phenomenal
woman to live alone and not be afraid. I wasn t atraia,
but I was mightily excited, and terribly anxious for tne
future. We had weeks of this, every few days bringing
news of mobilisation of troops first on one side anfl then
on the other. Our hope was that negotiations would go
on till snow fell in the Andes, when the passes—tneie
were only two —would be closed, and in the meantime
might get a man-of-war from the Falklands. Remem be ,
we were living amongst peoples only semi-eivihzed, an
of whom three parts were of Indian n „
never kept a revolver in the town, though 1 na
offered me. It is an awful thing to kill a man, and down
there unless vou kill it is useless shooting, and 1 w ° ul “"
dare to kill! My husband, too, was considered naa
because he did not carry a knive, not even when over
machines at Condor, where he had all the nation.
under him, and not one but was armed. , ...
“ For many reasons I liked living in Patagonia, *
cold was too severe—not the degree of cold, hut tn
combined with the wind. The wind gets in ever> •
I have been three days (on the edge of the Painpa) .. -,. .
to get the irons hot enough to iron, and burning „
coal all the time; and water dropped on the floor in»|)
kitchen froze at once. In the poorer houses the .
formed frost on the blankets, and I have h a d ic
eighth of an inch thick day and night on the msia,
bedroom window. As I sat governessing on the Estanma
my breath formed frost flowers on the scho ,
window, and the room stood at 32 degrees ana a
under. This with a kerosene-heater in the room.
“ After fourteen months of Patagonia we ie
Buenos Aires, and in March, 1902, left B.A. for
tralia.” -

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ballade of a Mystery.
So’omon, the king, long past and gone,
Said : * ‘ There are four things bewilder me:
The way of a snake on a path of stone ;
The way of a ship on the trackless sea;
Then the way of an eagle—these three
Put the lamp of the sage in the shade,
And the fourth (it is odd, you’ll agree)
Is the way of a man with a maid.”
When a glance has the power to shock,
And a thrill in a smile there be ;
While the sweep and the scent of a frock
Make one swim in sweet ecstasy;
When a tear dropped artfully
Makes the heart of the bravest afraid.
There’s a problem the dullest must see
In the way of a man with a maid.
But the ways of Jenny with Jock
Are a lock with a different key;
Think how Essex was sent to the block
Back in the seventeenth century,
And there’s many another than he
Who for longing and loving has paid;
And the noose is too often the fee
For the way of a man with a maid.
Love, O Love ! what we suffer for thee,
Yet we dote on the jilt and the jade,
And the spice of the risk is a plea
For the way of a man with a maid.
VIC.
D. BOUVERIE.
A Face in the Street.
Only a face in the street,
Haunting me many a day ;
Passed on the wave of the crowd
Out where the noises are loud,
Out where the hurrying feet
Pause not or stay.
Only a face in the street,
Such as the angels have had.
Fair like the gold of the corn,
Waved by the winds of the morn ;
Eyes that were wondrously sweet —
Proud and yet sad.
Only a face in the street.
Haunting me many a day ;
Passing and vanished and gone.
Swiftly the crowd hurried on.
O ily a face in the street—
Fair as the May.
VIC.
KATHLEEN WALKER.
Come and Have a Drink.
Life is long and most unhappy
With its constant woes and owes,
And the ancient Problem fattens
As it old and older grows.
There is little left to keep us
From the suicidal brink
But the welcome invitation,
“ Come along and have a drink.”
First comes bold young Miss Ambition
With the warm, seductive arms—
“ Up, brave youth, we go together ”
(Oh, the glory of her charms).
Years of fruitless adoration
Make you stand aside and think :
“Seems a case of breach of promise—
Better go and have a drink.”
Secondly, the Lady happens—
Usual lady, soul and eyes—
She, of course, will solve the Problem—
Fact, herself is life’s first prize.
Then the Problem yawns and stretches,
Tips an age-worn, crafty wink,
Says, “ The best solution current
Is to cut and have a drink.”
Last, a reckless-looking party
Hands his card in branded “ Chance,”
“I,” he says, “will beat this Problem ;
I’m the moulder of romance.”
Cheerfully you back his schemings—
Just as cheerfully they sink
Till the last, lean shilling’s vanished—
Not enough to have a drink.
No, the Problem is the victor—
It was issued out of Hell,
And the answer will be written
When they ring the Judgment bell.
Leave it to my lord the Devil,
Let him mould it link by link—
Shake the wrinkles from your forehead—
Time we had another drink.
M. L.
J. BARR.
When We No More Know.
Who can say what the end will be?
Who can guess how the game will go ?
Dust to dust at the last for me,
Earth to earth when I no more know.
O’er my grave, wherever it be,
The rains will fall and the winds will blow ;
What will it matter then to me
Where I’m laid, when I no more know ?
Men may say and think what they will—
I’ll be out of their ebb and flow;
I’ll have done with the good and ill—
Done for aye—when I no more know.
Then why reck of a taunt or two,
True to myself as I come and go ?
Life will carry me safely through,
And Death is sweet—when we no more know !
N.S.W.
R. CRAWFORD.

Tf)e Finger Post to Health
This is an Interesting Story
for Liver Sufferers.
The case of
Mr. G. HEATHCOTE.
(BY a special reporter.)
Since enlightened humanity requires that
the trials incidental to sickness should be
minimised wherever possible, and since by
kindliness and consideration much can be
done in that direction, a reporter called
upon Mr. George Heathcote, of Surry-
street, Waterloo, who willingly assented
to answer any questions.
“ Were you once a resident of Bunda-
berg, Queensland ? ” asked the pressman.
“Yes,” answered Mr. Heathcote. “I
was living there when it was necessary to
cross the bridge by boat instead of walking
over it, or, to be more explicit, during the
time of the great flood, and it was during
that exciting and disastrous period that my
blood was so impoverished through exces-
sively hard work for long hours at a stretch
that sores broke out on my hands and arms,
and my legs swelled terribly. Previous to
this I had suffered from fever and ague,
which left me very weak and shaky, and
for some weeks I noticed that my appetite
was falling off; but I put that down to
fatigue more than anything else, until other
symptoms cropped up to show me it was
not so, but my liver and kidneys which
were responsible.”
“ What reasor were there to enable you
to arrive at that conclusion ? ”
“ Any number of them, one of the most
unmistakable being a nasty pain which
caught me here (Mr. Heathcote indicated
the part affected by placing his hand on the
small of his back). My back ached so in-
tensely that my wife made flannel belts for
me to wear, thinking that they would afford
me some relief. If I was doing anything
which caused me to bend down, the pain on
straightening myself was something terrible,
while walking also provoked great distress
and inconvenience. Other proof that my
kidneys were in a bad way was also in
evidence, and as for the rest of my ail-
ments—well, I hardly know where to begin
first.”
“ Tell me a few of the most important—
those that are uppermost in your mind ? “
“ Oh ! I have not forgotten any of them,”
was the quick response, “especially the
heavy feelings in my chest, which made me
short-winded. After eating, a weighty sen-
sation appeared in that quarter, and before
I knew where I was my stomach began
swelling out with wind, which made me
very uncomfortable always, and at times
gave rise to sharp, cutting pains. The
sour gases gradually worked their way up,
and caused me to belch, after which, and
until I took a little more food, I felt
slightly easier, but the same unpleasantness
followed each meal, however trifling. When
making my morning preparations for work
I used to feel as if I would like to go back
to bed again, as I was entirely devoid of
energy, and had no desire to attempt any-
thing. This languidness was due, no doubt,
to the restlessness every night. I could go
to bed feeling so drowsy that my eyes
seemed swollen for the want of rest, yet I
found myself awake the greater part of the
night, and when I got up my limbs were
all aches. Nearly every day I was dis-
tracted with intense headaches? which ap-
peared to bring a mist before iny eyes, as
if my sight was failing me, and without any
warning my head became so giddy that I
had to take a rest till the sensation passed
away. Every month I was getting weaker
and more wretched, being continually be-
set with pains under my shoulder-blades,
which travelled right through to the heart,
and caused me considerable anxiety The
white coating on my tongue explained the
unhealthy condition of my stomach, too,
and what I wanted was a real good medicine
to clear away all disorders and impurities ;
but in those days I did not know as much
as I do now, or I would have been out of
my troubles very quickly. I went from
bad to worse, and had arrived at that
melancholy and desperate stage when the
least and most simple thing excites a feeling
of irritation, and I am sure you will admit
that such a life of misery is not worth en-
during. Had I gone on a little longer in
that sorry plight I question whether I
would have been as sound a man as I am
to-day, for I was fairly in the grip of my
complaint, when, owing to the recollection
of some conversations between a few of my
friends, I hit upon the idea of taking some
Clements Tonic. I took immediate action,
as I firmly believed that further delay
would have brought irretrievable disaster
to my constitution.”
“ Your good fortune came at a very ac-
ceptable time, I should imagine ’ ”
“ Yes ; and a very critical time, too, and
I regretted that I had not bought a few
bottles of Clements Tonic at the outset;
but we all have to buy our experience, you
know, although I intend doing all I can to
help other sufferers by letting them know
what Clements Tonic can do. Personally,
I can truthfully say that it generally re-
vived me from my lethargic state when I
had drank but a small quantity, and I found
myself rapidly improving. Clements Tonic
had a magical effect on my kidneys and
greatly relieved the pains about my back
and shoulders. This relief, coupled with
the abatement of my headaches, had the
effect of making me feel more restful at
nights, and the sound sleep I got through
using Clements Tonic did me an immense
amount of good. My slumber was not in-
terfered with by dreams, and when I awoke
at the usual time for getting up I arose with
alacrity and hastened to my morning meal,
after which I was ready for work. It was
a marvellous change to be full of energy
and cheerfulness again, but the invigorating
properties of Clements Tonic were so re-
markable that I could not have restrained
my feelings of joy had I wanted to. Wind
never formed in my stomach after Clements
Tonic got to work, neither did my chest
trouble me, while in the matter of palpita-
tion the same medicine was equal to the
prevailing requirements. There is no
denying the fact that Clements Tonic
was wonderfully effective as a blood
purifier, for the sores were gradually
effaced from my hands and arms, and the
cause of my legs swelling—whatever that
cause might have been—was entirely re-
moved. My illness had considerably
affected my nerves, which became shaky
whenever I was disturbed, but to my heart-
felt thankfulness I must say that Clements
Tonic was as effective on my nervous
system as it was on my other deranged
organs.'
“ What was your appetite like then ? "
“As it was soon after commencing on
Clements Tonic—very good, indeed, and it
has remained so ever since. But the
strengthening qualities of my newly-found
medicine surprised me, for although my
work was very heavy I always got through
it without feeling any ill effects from the
time Clements Tonic cured me, and I have
not once had the faintest return of any of
my old symptoms, in acknowledgment of
which I give you permission to publish my
story as it pleases you.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Georor Heathcotb, of Surry-street, Water-
loo, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of five
folios, and consecutively numbered from one
to live, and that it contains and is a true and faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic,
and also contains my full permission to publish in any
way my statements -which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment; and I make this
solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of
the Oaths Act, 1900.

Wore a Torture
BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
SULPHURIC 71GID.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to tlie Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free Belt, which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £4 for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained Worl<d
Wide fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen —In answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had my attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, I thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £1 and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt, with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
£4. I wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month. You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts.
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself in your
hands. I knew my case was very diffi-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a very
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Belt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has
Completely Cured Me, and strongly recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find are
suffering from the distressing malady
called “ Sciatica.”
I have not been wearing the Belt for the
past fortnight, but have passed it over to
my wife, who has received great benefit
for nervous troubles.
I remain, your grateful patient,
W. FOLEEY

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 1, 1903.
The Federal Capital.
It was in some measure unfortunate that the
Australian Federation was not cradled in time of
war or other great national stress. The easy and
comfortable conditions under which the union
came to birth—the only difficulty being the petty
and undignified one of soothing the squabbling
jealousies of various provincialists—deprived the
new nation of much that would have been valuable
in forming a character. Australia began federated
life too much under hothouse conditions. The
evil effects of this have shown themselves on
various occasions, but never so conspicu-
ously as in the discussion as to the location of
the Federal capital. From many sides comes a
clamor against a “bush capital,” and plaintive
arguments as to the “discomfort” and “incon-
venience” to legislators if the Federal Par-
liament sits anywhere outside Melbourne or
Sydney. In other quarters, whilst there is a
grudging acquiescence to the proposal that the
Legislature should move away from the fleshpots
of the great cities, there is an eager desire to veto
any proposed site which has real or fancied
hardships of climate or access. The time has
come to remark to those gentlemen of the
Federal Parliament who are desirous of
staying in one of our great cities, and to
those who are so querulously anxious that
a site should be ciiosen with prompt access by
Pullman car and with a soft and complaisant
climate, that the whole purpose of the Federation
is not to provide comfortable hotel quarters for
those who chance now to be its legislators. The
Bulletin wishes to say this firmly, if a little reluc-
tantly. This paper has never joined in that cheap
and nasty adulteration of Radicalism which seeks
the favor of the unthinking by belittling the repre-
sentatives of the people and attacking their so-
called “fat salaries ’ and ‘‘enormous perquisites.”
That is a Tory game to cripple the service of the
public so that the public may be the more easily
victimised. But, whilst Australia should give
the best that it can to its Parliament and
Ministers, concession must stop short of
keeping back the development of the nation
in the interests of the marble bath and the
civilised cocktail of the Legislature. So soon as
these things can be provided, consistently with
the national interests, they should be ; the legis-
lator is worthy of his bath and his cocktail. But
if the nation needs it, the Legislature should be
content with a bullock-dray capital. It must be
the nation’s interests first and those of the nation’s
s ervants second.
* * * *
It is ridiculous to hear, as one may hear, mem-
bers of the Federal Parliament arguing that the
Bombala site for the capital—the only truly
national site so far proposed—is impossible,
because, for a while, members would have to
travel some of the distance by coach, and
because the climate there is sometimes cold.
This is an old woman attitude. These “ dainty
affetuosos” who would sacrifice a nation’s in-
terests rather than submit to a little jolting or an
occasional sharp blast of wind, show themselves in
a wholly contemptible light. It is their misfortune
perhaps, rather than their fault, that such paltry
ideas should infect their minds. The daily papers,
With their draper souls, constantly preach paltri-
ness ini cowardice in national affairs, and there
has been in Australia of late no stirring deed to
rouse the mind from sluggishness. Federation
was born in peddling times. The very capital site
location was made matter of degraded huckstering
by George Reid. Eureka and the Anti-trans-
portation movement are far remote from these
days, and the spirit aroused by those great out-
bursts of national feeling has, to a great extent,
faded way. Else it would be impossible for an
Australian public man to balance against the
arguments in favor of a truly national Federal site
his personal liability to catch a cold in his nose.
• • * *
Ignoring the cold-in-the-nose issue, the con-
ditions which a locality must satisfy to make it
truly suitable for an Australian capital may be
briefly recapitulated as follows :
The area must be the sole property of the Federation,
and must be sufficiently large to secure for the Australian
people all the increment in land values which the build-
ing of the capital will give. An area of 100 square miles
would be inadequate even for the building of a city ; and
it would leave the larger share of land value increments
to the private landlords outside the area. Greater
Melbourne has an area of 250 square miles, and
that area by no means embraces all the land which
has largely increased in value by the mere building
of the city. If an area of 100 square miles, dedicated
tor a Federal city, were an exact square, and the city
were built in its exact middle, it would only be a distance
of five miles from the Federal G.P.O. to some private
landlord’s area. If, as it is more reasonable to suppose, this
exact mathematical regularity were not obtainable, it
might be only half a mile from the Federal city to the
outside area where the private landlord annexed the
profits.
The area must be of such a character as to permit of a
real city arising, with good agricultural lands contiguous,
with room and facilities for factories and arsenals. Either
an easy coal supply or water power must be available.
A bracing and sturdy climate is desirable. The Federal
area should be a nursery for the Federal soldiers and
Bailors and public servants.
A Federal port is absolutely essential. The present
humiliating tribute agreement with Britain cannot be
endured for ever. In time the Commonwealth must have
its own navy ; it will then want its own dockyards and
harbor. Further, the Federal capital should not be sur-
rounded on all sides by the area of any one State, and be
dependent solelv on that State for its means of communi-
cation.
Of the various sites under consideration there is
only one that meets all these requirements—the
Eden-Bombala area. In view of the necessity for
economy—the Commonwealth cannot very well
buy up Newcastle and the Hunter River valley—
it is probably the only site available in the whole
Commonwealth. And it answers to the natural
needs in a fashion almost absolutely complete.
An area can be secured stretching from an excel-
lent harbor on the coast towards fine, bracing high-
lands, where might be bred men who could beat
Maorilanders at football or steal cattle from a High-
lander of the 16th century. Railway communi-
cation with the great State capitals can be effected
without any enormous difficulty. There is a
splendid water-supply—the only really genuine
hall-marked, never-failing Australian river, the
Snowy, passes through the land, and water for
every requirement, from the generation of elec-
trical power for running factory machinery to the
dilution of the politician’s whisky, is available. It
is fitted, by reason of its cheap water power, to be
the greatest and most economical manufacturing
site in Australia—water is even cheaper than coal.
A harbor suitable for the naval and trade necessi-
ties of a Federal capital is in existence. There need
be no cramping of the area resumed, as 5000 square
miles, of mostly Crown lands, can be obtained.
On the Eden-Bombala site, in short, the Federa-
tion may found a city and a State which will
serve every national purpose, and wield a great
influence in breaking down those provincial
jealousies which are the chief obstacles to the pro-
gress of the Union.
• • • •
The question is a national and not a provincial
one. No member of the Australian Parliament
should consider New South Wales, Victorian, or
other State interests in coming to a decision.
Still less should he give a thought to his own per-
sonal predilections and weaknesses. The national
outlook is the only worthy one—an outlook not
confined to a generation, or even a century, but far-
seeing enough to take into consideration the great
possible future of this continent and of its Federal
Government. The question as to whether a little
bit of huckstering trade will go to, or be taken
away from, the merchants of Melbourne or Sydney
is one which cannot enter into the decision, with-
out treachery to Australia. The fear that a
national and economical site will necessarily be at
first a little remote from the big cities, a little
difficult of access, a little wanting in the higher
luxuries of civilisation, is one that can only in-
fluence the cowardly and the selfish ; the man
who would betray his country for the sake
of enjoying a little comfort on a velvet otto-
man is such a cheap traitor that he can’t
be conceded to have even the business acu-
men of a Judas. Members of the Australian
Parliament already make very great sacrifices in
attending to their duties, as they receive allow-
ances far too low to adequately compensate them
for their business losses. It would be very pitiful
if, after suffering serious inconveniences cheer-
fully, they should, on the deathbed of the first
Parliament, sacrifice the national interests in a
matter of the very highest importance for the sake
of an extra hour in a sleeping-car, in deference
to some provincial jealousy, or for any other
paltry personal or political reason. The Bulletin
in particular appeals to the Labor party to stand
to the national and patriotic attitude on this great
issue. That party has, so far, shown itself the
most consistently Federal and Australian of the
three organisations in the Parliament; it would
be a fitting crown to its work in the first Aus-
tralian Legislature if it were to stand unitedly for
the national, as opposed to the provincial, or the
personally selfish, view in the selection of the
national capital.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Civil Service Testimonial Cadge.
An estimable gentleman named Maynard is
retiring from a well-paid billet in N.S.W. Publio
Instruction Department, to spend his declining
years in reasonable affluence, and the usual
committee of highly placed busybodies has set to
work to practically blackmail the ill-paid sub-
ordinate employees of small sums to make up the
usual testimonial. The same old cadging circular
has gone out, but with extra-special impudence
the circular this time suggests the amount that
each employee is expected to give- so much from
the public servant with a salary of £150, and so
on according to a sliding scale. Many of the
people thus circularised never saw Mr. Maynard.
Some of them hardly heard of him in all their
lives. None of them, probably, reckons that
he or she is under any special obligations to him.
The writer of this has, at various times, when
some similar cadge was in progress, interviewed
civil servants—principally teachers and country
employees—and has found these to be very
common opinions on the subject:—
(1) That the habit of organising testimonials to depart-
ing high functionaries is sedulously kept up by high
functionaries who have not yet departed, so that the pre-
cedent may hold good when they leave the service in
their turn. In other words, the Jones who gets up a
testimonial to Smith to-day is credited with doing it for
the sordid purpose of getting his own testimonial to-
morrow. .....
(2) That the man or woman who doesn’t submit to this
petty tyranny and give his or her half-crown to the
rich boss, is marked down for punishment and petty
annoyance.
(3) That the chiefs of the service regard this process of
levying a tax on ill-paid subordinates, who can never hope
to get any testimonial themselves, as an unofficial means of
adding to their incomes, akin to the Chinese boss-man-
darin’s habit of exacting bribes from his inferior officials.
The service may be taking too harsh a view.of the
case, but that is the view a large section of it does
take. When a man who is in authority, or who is
supposed to represent someone in authority,
suggests to a poor subordinate that he should give
2s. 6d. to someone who has long been his cnief
boss, and who may still influence the course of
events, the subordinate naturally assumes that it
is safer to pay, however seriously he may feel the
loss of the 2s. 6d. He assumes that the influential
people who are running the cadge must want
2s. fid. very badly ; that they are liable to be dis-
appointed and infuriated if they don’t get it; that
if they are infuriated they may use their power to
do him injury. In his own mind he probably
regards them as mean blackmailers and not many
degrees higher than petty thieves, also as crawl-
some persons who are trying to earn somebody’s
gratitude by extracting petty sums from the poor
for his benefit; but, on the other hand, be con-
siders that, just as he parts his 2s. 6d. in order to
avoid offending them, they may also be in the
painful position of having to collect the 2s. 6d. in
order to avoid offending somebody else. And if
the view taken by the man who thus parts with one
of his scarce half-crowns is at all unfair, it can
only be said that tbe men who run the cadge
deserve to be thus viewed. They shouldn’t look
mean and grovelsome and mercenary and abject,
and then they wouldn’t be considered so. They
shouldn’t beg, and then they wouldn’t be regarded
as beggars. They shouldn’t cadge from people
they may be able to injure, and then they wouldn’t
be suspected of a tendency to injure those people
if the cadge isn’t responded to. And if they
haven’t sense enough to realise the situation and
cease cadging, Parliament ought to prohibit the
whole cadging system.

Senator De Largie’s “ Freetrade Tariff.”
Senator De Largie, of Westralia, has apparently
some curious ideas on fiscalism. He seriously
suggests that a fiscal referendum should be taken
on these four issues :
Protectionist tariff.
Freetrade tariff.
Protectionist revenue tariff.
Freetrade revenue tariff,
Three of these things are reasonably understand-
able, but tbe fourth is a contradiction in terms —
it is a live corpse, and a horse marine, and a tall
dwarf, and a pale European negro from Central
Africa. A Protectionist tariff, pure and simple,
is understandable. It means a tariff which is
designed solely to promote local industries ; and
when it achieves its purpose to the full, it will
yield no customs revenue at all, for the things
which can’t be produced here won’t be taxed, and
the things which can be produced here, and which
are taxed, won’t be imported. A Protectionist
revenue tariff is moderately understandable. It
presumably means a tariff in which Protectionist
duties are not so high as to stop imports, but
are only calculated to reduce them and to encourage
local production up to a certain point; also a tariff
which, in addition to its Protectionist duties,
includes some duties imposed for purely revenue
purposes on goods which can’t be locally produced.
A Freetrade revenue tariff is easy enough of com-
prehension. That implies a tariff in which certain
imports are taxed for revenue purposes with
exactly equal excise duties on similar articles of
local make, whereby the country secures revenue,
and at the same time enjoys all the alleged bless-
ings of free competition. If the excise duties are
not equal to the customs duties, then, to the
extent of the difference, tbe alleged curse of
Protection creeps in. But as for a Freetrade
tariff as distinguished from a Freetrade revenue
tariff there is no such thing—it is in the same
category as bears’ eggs and tbe famous Mrs.
Harris. Senator De Largie should _ try to
struggle out of bis state of mental confusion, and
explain what he is driving at.
A Chance for Reid.
The Sydney Importers’ papers have labored
heavily to prove that it was the duty of ex-Pre-
mier Barton to remain in politics and fight next
election, because G. H. Reid promised to oppose
him then at the Hunter. Apart from the facts
that it is pretty certain Reid would never have
made the promise but for his conviction that
Barton would not be in politics at next election,
and that he wouldn’t keep the promise if Barton
did remain in politics, there is something very
large and beautiful in the theory that the world
must stop short to wait George Reid’s conveni-
ence. The Importers’ party has certainly a rather
High opinion of the tonnage of its dancing clown,
but it is straining things a little to assume that
when Reid issues his comic challenge and waves
his dog in the air the other party must hang up
all other matters in order to meet him, or be for
ever disgraced. Anyhow, even if Barton is out
of the way, George need not despair. His quarrel
was not with Barton personally, but with tbe
head of the Protectionist Ministry. Alfred
Deakin is that head now, and there is nothing at
all to prevent Reid giving up his East Sydney
seat and fighting Premier Deakin next December
at Ballarat, which is almost as large a constituency
as tbe one Reid now represents. Of course, if he
didn’t really mean to oppose Barton, or if be only
meant to do it under the impression that Barton’s
seat was one he could capture without any
risk, lie will not attack Deakin ; but otherwise
there is every reason why he should. He pro-
fesses to want to fight the Protectionist Premier
of Australia, and Deakin is the man. And the
opportunity would be a glorious one to carry the
war into the enemy’s country, to snatch a seat
from the Protectionists, and to prove the reality
of that great Freetrade revival in Victoria, which
Reid has so often declared to exist. Also there
would be no question of deserting N.S.W. in any
material sense. Reid, of Ballarat, could see that
justice was done to N.S.W. quite as well as Reid
of East Sydney. In fact, N.S.W. would prac-
tically gain an extra member by the change.
The Labor Platform.
Few things are calculated to make any progressive
Australian feel sadder than the Federal and State
Labor platform as published regularly in the Labor
papers. It is such a fine platform at the beginning
and so miserably incompetent at the end. It
demands a legal minimum vvage, which is a good
thing—and which will raise the cost of production.
It asks for shorter hours which, when the wages
are not shortened to correspond, must also increase
the cost of production. ] t seeks for many things
which improve the condition of the people—and
m so doing raise the cost of production. And
certainly the cost of production should be raised—
in every case it should be raised high enough to
enable the work of production to pay decent
wages, allow decent hours, let the workman live
under decent conditions, and yet give the
employer a fair return. And with it all the
Labor party still states that it will sink the
fiscal issue, and allow itself to be represented by
Freetraders provided they accept its terms and
conditions. What is the good of a Freetrader
accepting any such terms and conditions? He
may vote for good wages, but, if he gets wages
fixed at a respectable figure by law, then, under
Freetrade, the foreign low-wage man gets the busi-
ness. He may help to get it enacted that the
Australian shall not work more than eight hours,
and then, under Freetrade, the foreigner who
works 12 hours gets the business. It is impossible
to"get short hours and good wages without raising
the cost of production—and then, under Freetrade,
the cheap foreign producer gets the business. If
tbe Labor programme is carried with Freetrade
attached thereto, it will bemuch worse for Australia
than if there had never been any Labor programme,
and the out-and-out Freetrade Labor member is a
worse enemy to Labor than Dobson, or Simon
Fraser, or Bruce Smith, or any of their kind.
Under a Dobson Freetrade regime the Australian
people, by dint of working long hours for low
wages, would still be able to live—in a kind of a
way. Under a Labor Freetrade regime they
would be prohibited from working long hours for
low wages, and they wouldn’t, through the com-
petition of cheap foreigners, get any work at all.
All the book platitudes in the world won’t alter
the fact that high wages and short hours mean an
increase in the cost of production, unless they are
counterbalanced by unusual and quite exceptional
skill, which the Australian certainly doesn’t
possess. The Labor platform is a good thing for
ensuring that what work there is to do in
Australia shall be done under fair circumstances.
But it does nothing to provide that there will be
work to do, under fair conditions or any other
conditions. At present Australia imports so
much and makes so little that there isn’t nearly
enough work for its people to do, and Freetrade
will increase the imports so that there will be less
work. Then there will remain—the platform,
which isn’t eatable nor wearable, which, without
the work to do, is only loose eloquence, theory,
language, ink, and well-meaning drivel.

The Victorian Factories Bill.
With loud professions that they desire to suppress
sweating, the Victorian employers are just now
engaged in a bitter fight against the new Factories
Bill. This paradoxical attitude only serves to
show what strange means some folk adopt in order
to prove their sincerity. They rank with that
genius in explanation who fired at a French Pre-
sident and explained that he only did it to draw
attention to anew method of stuffing flock mat-
tresses. The employers who hate sweating have
persistently opposed all suggested legislation for
its eradication. Before the advent of Federation
the Victorian Chamber of Sweaters used to
declare, “Give us any other means of putting
down sweating than the Factories Act and we
will support it.” Some of them and their press
heelers went so far as to advocate Arbitration in
preference to a Factories Act. The Federal Par-
liament came along with an Arbitration Bill,
and the employers rose in arms against it.
Now their own Irvine comes down with a
very much modified Factories Bill and the
employers are against it also. Apparently
the employers, when they say they are against
sweating, are telling one of those undiluted lies
sanctioned by the murky-faced goddess of “trade.”
It is the same kind of bald lie as is told by the
jam-maker who sells mangel-wurzels, turnips,
carrots, and vegetable marrows as “purest fruit
jams,” or by the meat-packer who sells twelve
ounces for a pound. “ Trade ” seems to be a sort
of hothouse in which the weeds of lying and
deceit flourish exceedingly. No lie, no meanness
is too colossal to bolster up a trade deal or to
hinder legislation designed to protect the public
from the tricks of employers. Although fac-
tories legislation has been on the statute-books of
Victoria for seven years or more, the Employers’
Federation sent a deputation to Chief Secretary
Murray to request that the present Bill should
expire in December, 1904, in order to give
the wildly novel idea a trial. In order
to have a free hand to exploit boy and
girl labor, the employers also asked that
no restrictions should be placed on the
number of boys and girls working in factories.
Here again the request was supported with an
unvarnished lie. The deputationists urged that
under the present system of fixing a proportion of
boys and girls to men and women in factories the
young people were excluded from learning trades.
When the employers had absolute freedom, before
Factories Acts were dreamed of, it is a notorious
fact that fewer boys and edrls were taught trades
than now. Boys and girls were then taken
on for a few months, without wages, by different
employers in turn, and then dismissed as
being unsuited. Further, the employers want
to exclude all men over 55 years of age and all
women over 45 years of age from the operation of
the Act. They want this done for the benefit of the
men and women themselves. The compassion of
these employers for the old and slow worker is stag-
gering. During the course of the deputation it was
strenuously denied that “ reputable
paid sweating wages. Assertions of that kind
recall the fact that it was said “reputable firms
did not diddle the customs revenue until it was
proved otherwise. As the employers could get no
satisfaction out of Murray, they put up Swin-
burne, the tall, lank advocate of freedom of
contract, to move a number of amendments
on the Bill in the Legislative Assembly, lo
the credit of a rather Tory House it has to be
said that the amendments w.ere rejected with
scorn. It is expected, however, that the sweaters
will have better luck in the House of 48 Rich
Landlords.

The Rump Ministry.
The method of reconstruction of the Federal
Cabinet augurs ill for its fate at the general
elections. “A Ministry of rehabilitated frag-
ments,” Josiah Symon has dubbed the recon-
structed Administration, and it deserves the sneer.
As an entity, it is 25 per cent, below the average
of ability of the Legislature, and includes men who
would he too much honored by a seat m a state
Cabinet. Yet Premier Deakin had an absolutely
free hand in making changes. When his prede-
cessor resigned, the whole Ministry
him ; and Alfred Deakin, having been sent
for,” was empowered to choose whomsoever he
listed. He might have arranged with Kingston to
return to the Cabinet, and there were available
such men as Isaacs, Higgins, Symon, and possibly
J. C. Watson and B. R. Wise (the last is credited


with an intention to enter the Australian Parlia-
ment). There was no need whatever to re-appoint
J.ohn Forrest, who is quite out of place in a
democratic combination; or Fysh, who is old and
harmless; or even Drake. By retaining these,
and adding Chapman, who never was strong
enough for even a State portfolio, and Playford,
who is better than Chapman but yet none too
good, Premier Deakin seems to have deliberately
sought for a Ministry of colorless political respec-
tability. Apparently he was guided to some
extent by the foolish old parochial idea that it
was necessary to share the portfolios out in
certain proportions among the States regard-
less of personal fitness, and it is a painful
commentary on the weakness of the N.S.W.
delegation that, outside the Labor party, there
was only one better man than Chapman who
could have been selected ; and that man — Ewing
—didn’t belong, in the strictest sense of the term,
to the Bill Lyne circle, or represent any place in
the vicinity of Lyne’s electorate. Certainly it
was impossible to offer a portfolio to Wilks,
or anybody of his calibre. All the same,
if the parochial policy of sharing port-
folios out geographically makes it necessary to
take in Chapman rather than Isaacs, and to
retain Fysh and Forrest and Drake ; and if the
doctrines of bell-topperdom makes it impossible
to take in Watson or Hughes ; and if the theory
of being moderate and colorless and affable and
trying to please everybody shuts out restless,
active men like Kingston who want to
do things—then it is difficult to see that
Deakin could have done better. Assuming
that what he wants is a restful Govern-
ment that is pretty well content with things as
they are, that is elderly and accustomed to go to
Government House, and that is nicely divided
among the States, then he has got what he wants.
If he is in search of a strong, progressive demo-
cratic Government, then he has found as bad a
sample as could well be imagined. It only
requires to get rid of George Turner to be all
mediocrity; Turner is the one landmark of
statesmanship left. The dominating force of the
Cabinet is now the big, loud, aggressively-Tory
John Forrest, and the steersman who dodges a
wav among the snags of personal animosity
is Bill Lyne. Possibly, in getting his
nonentities together, Premier Deakin intended
to wait for guidance until the general elections
were over. Then if the Tory cause is successful
at the polls, the Forrest influence will dominate
the Cabinet. If the democratic cause prevails,
Bill Lyne will be entrusted with the task
of winning back the Labor party, and the Ministry
will be as progressive as you please, and as its
limited knowledge of what constitutes progress
will admit. But a Ministry which has lost King-
ston and O’Connor, and which hasn’t lost Lyne
and Forrest and Drake, and which has found
Chapman, will probably not live to receive any
instructions at the polls except instructions to
resign.

The Federal High Court.
The Federal High Court, as constituted, commands
respect for its high ability, whilst imparting un-
easiness at the fact that it is all, practically, of
the one color as regards political convictions. The
last thing that a Supreme Court Judge should be
is a politician ; once he mounts the Bench his
partizan views should vanish and all things be
looked at in the sober and equable spirit of
impartiality. But it is inevitable that a man’s
training and associations should, to some extent,
tinge his mind, however much he may strive to
clear it of all bias. Whilst Australia can con-
fidently expect of the three judges appointed an
honest effort to be strictly fair in all things, it is
probable that in the decision of those high questions
of constitutional law which merge insensibly from
law into politics, their minds will be biassed
towards that Conservative and Imperialistic view
which they have followed all their lives. There is
no chance of any one of the three becoming a
Higinbotham, or following up that great
tradition of the English Bench which often made
the Judge the chief defender of popular liberty.
Griffith, Barton and O’Connor will dispense
good law, will administer even justice so far as they
can, but they will not, it may be safely predicted, in
laying down the early precedents which will make
Australian Common Law, ever fail to stretch, a
little towards the Tory view in local affairs,
towards the CHAMBERLAIN-Imperialistic view in
external affairs. In a Bench of five Australian
Judges, The Bulletin would welcome at least
two of them ; in a Bench of three, at least one.
It does not suggest the desirability of a palpably
Radical court as an alternative to this palpably
Tory and pro-English court. But, taking the
range of selection as including Griffith, Downer,
Barton, Higgins, O’Connor, Isaacs, Symon,
Wise and A. I. CLARKfive—or three —might have
been chosen who would have blended the different
constitutional law views of Australia. Griffith,
Barton and O’Connor are Tory and-pro-English;
Clark and Higgins are democratic and pro-
Australian ; Wise, democratic and pro-English ;
Symon, _ Tory and pro-Australian. A mixture of
these views would have secured a Bench embody-
ing the happy mean. The present occupants
represent the Conservative extreme. There is
one other point of criticism. It is stated in some
quarters that Chief Justice Griffith is to retain
some right to a future pension from the State of
Queensland. The Federal Parliament should
absolutely veto any such arrangement. If Mr.
Griffith has a promise of a future Queensland
State Pension—a promise which the Queensland
-State Parliament can at any time withdraw —
and a case, comes before him as a Judge in
which the interests of the State of Queensland
are in. conflict with the interests of the
Federation, what effect the fear of the loss of
his pension would have on him may be sur-
mised from two points of view—that of lofty
confidence, and that of suspicion. The suspicious
attitude would be the general public, if not the
correct, attitude. The Commonwealth must insist
on being sole paymaster, present and prospective,
of its Judges. In N.S.W. it is thought to be an
unwise thing to promote a District Court Judge
to the Supreme Court Bench, or to make a puisne
JAstice Chief Justice, on the principle that once
appointed to the Bench a man should have nothing
to nope for in the way of promotion, as well as
nothing to fear in the way of punishment, from the
Government. The loss of Griffith’s ability would
be great; but to surround the High Court with
an atmosphere of suspicion (which would be the
effect of his presence on the Bench whilst hoping
for a State pension) would be a much more serious
matter.
