The Drift of N.S.W. Assembly.
Whilst N.S.W. Assembly, hindered by factious
opposition and bogged by inept leadership,
wrangles and drivels, there is a great mass of work
waiting to be done. The business paper shows,
the following valuable reform measures, few of
which have any hope of passing this session:
Influx of Criminals Prevention Bill (a measure to pre-
vent the State being swamped by imported gaol-birds);
it wants only one stage to become an Act. There is
another Bill on the business paper to deal more effec-
tively with the habitual criminals of the State.
Forestry Bill, to put some method into the State’s deal-
ings with its timber resources.
Liquor Bill, an honest, if somewhat namby-pamby,
effort to deal with the scandals and anomalies of
the liquor traffic.
Two Water and Sewerage Bills, to allow of more effec-
tive administration of municipal works in city and
country for water supply and drainage.
Dairy Industry Bill, to impose some much-needed regu-
lation on the dairy-products export trade.
Gun License Bill—a measure which will do something
to check the sale of the pernicious pea-rifle and the
devouring revolver to the youthful and the maniacal.
Two Bills to deal with juvenile vice, and to rescue
from the streets and bad houses youngsters from whom
are recruited the criminal classes.
And half-a-score others of less importance, but
still of great importance. All these are hung up
whilst the House discusses endlessly and aimlessly
Billjim’s dirt money and the punt in the
electorate of Horseleech, M.L.A. A good deal
is the fault of the Opposition—an Opposition
which includes such freaks as Wood, Affleck,
Storey, Fitzpatrick, Levy, Jessep, and Dr.
Ross, who are physically—no truthful person can
add, mentally—capable of discussing any subject
at any length, and at any hour. Blame also
attaches to individual Ministers, some of whom
seem to revel in adding the maximum of ir-
relevancy to the minimum of courtesy in their
replies to the questions and speeches of opponents.
But the greatest censure must fall on the Govern-
ment as a body. It in no wise leads the House,
but allows the session to drift to its close without
any serious effort to do anything but fill in
time. And that’s a game which does not pay in
politics.
Maoriland’s Landless Policy.
“Kiwi” writes from God’s Country—and
Seddon’s
Now that Victoria appears to be about to embark on
the mad and potentially corrupt policy of acquiring
private estates for settlement, it may be well to point
out the effects of that system in this State. Anyone
who cares to analyse the facts must conclude that it is a
crass failure. To begin with, we imposed a land-tax
in 1891 —a very proper impost ; but a couple of
years later we started buying the very properties that
paid the most taxation, and hence, though the unearned
increment of the land of this State has advanced twenty
million pounds since then, our land tax revenue is prac-
tically stationary Meanwhile, we have not settled the
land problem by any means. The State tenants who have
been placed on the resumed areas are generally
more bigoted reactionaries than the people they
have replaced. They are growing bolder as they grow
stronger in numbers, and they are now clamoring
for the freehold, in which demand they are backed up by
the so-called Farmers’ Union. The richest squatters are
not more opposed to labor legislation than these
“cockies”; in fact, it is no exaggeration to say that
our landless policy is breeding reactionaries, who are
more dangerous, because more numerous, than the
squatters. Yet we still have many large estates. The
Parliamentary return referred to in Bulletin (13 8/’O3),
shows that there are 192 people who own each from
10,000 to 100,000 acres of land, and this coterie alone shares
among its members some four million acres. Our policy
of spending borrowed money on public works is having the
inevitable effect of enhancing the value of land. Hence
land is rapidly becoming dearer, and so we must pay
more for our purchases from year to year. To borrow
money for public works is bad enough ; but to borrow
again in order to buy our own lands at prices in-
flated by borrowing is the high-water mark of
absurdity. Meanwhile, the big squatter is feeling
more secure, for he has an army of “cockies” to fight
for him, and he doesn’t at all mind being bought out for
a good price. I have no doubt that if the Russell crowd
once got into office all the “ best” people would soon be
rid of their properties at handsome prices.. The one
effective remedy—a tax on land values —is, for the
present, lost sight of, and as an increasing number of
people become interested in the maintenance of the
present rotten system, some time will elapse before the
landless majority will realise how they are being cheated
in the name of a progressive land policy.
The Bulletin (3/9/’O3) pointed out something of
this in an article headed “Irvine’s Resumption
Scheme.” But “Kiwi” isn’t conclusive. The
new Maoriland farmers may be a bad lot, but if a
country waits before settling the people on the
land till it can get perfectly honest and unselfish
people to settle, and doesn’t start manufactures
till it can get angels to run them, or shipping till
it has evolved the perfect shipowner, it will wait
a long while. The Bulletin’s theory is this:
(1) The country should stop all borrowing, and pro-
vide its public works fund and a public debt sinking
fund (however small) out of taxation. Then it should
start buying back land, paying for it out of taxation.
Under these conditions it would not have to buy on the
inflated values created by a loan boom.
(2) It should lease the land in limited areas—not sell it.
(3) There should be a heavy graduated land-tax on
estates above the limit to hasten the process of disinte-
gration.
The idea of limiting the area would, of course,
arouse the bitter opposition of the class which
wants large estates. But it would have the sup-
port of the greater class which has no land, or
which holds areas below the limit, and which
wouldn’t want to see its chance of getting up to the
limit spoiled by the aggregation of large estates,
and a consequent land famine.
The Crown Defender—Once More.
The Victorian Letter Carriers’ Association lately
passed a resolution “that the Solicitor-General
be asked to frame legislation that, when a criminal
charge has been proved to be unfounded, the
expenses of the defence be paid by the Crown.”
Judging by recent events, the Victorian Solicitor-
General is much more likely to frame a picture of
the person who makes such a charge—especially
if she be a short-skirted damsel of nasty mind.
The resolution has, so far, met with only a stony
stare of incredulous surprise; notwithstanding
which, so long as that resolution is not given
effect to —so long, in fact, as a Government pays
a Crown Prosecutor and not a Crown Defender
—any Solicitor-General or other personage, or,
indeed, anyone at all, who prates about the
glories of British justice, the “palladium
of liberty, ” and so forth, is just talking bunkum.
Every time any Podsnap person gets on his
hindlegs and babbles of Magna Charta, “which
denies to no man justice and right,” somebody
should hit him heavily and promptly with a
brick. The blessed Law says every man is innocent
until he is proved guilty, but it takes no interest in
his ability or capacity to prove his’ innocence,
though it assists his accuser to prove him guilty to
the utmost of his power. Why the Law is afflicted
witli this crooked habit of thought not even itself
knows; at least, it has never given the slightest
sign of knowledge. Any person (even if neither an
adult nor an elector) may lay any charge—from
petty larceny to murder and sacrilege—against any
other. Until the evidence is heard, it is merely
one man’s word against another’s, yet the lop-
sided Law straightway runs one party into the
lock-up and takes no manner of thought whatever
about his innocence—it, in fact, so strongly
objects to the idea of his innocence that it even
puts all possible obstacles in his way when he
tries to prove it himself with such means as he
possesses ; while it spends its time and substance
without stint in helping the other person to prove
—not his innocence—but the first person’s guilt.
Why the Law should be so keen on convicting
someone passes comprehension. It isn’t because,
having built several costly gaols, it is desirable to
fill them, for there are large numbers of persons
whom it never dreams of imprisoning who would
look exceedingly well in gaol. It isn’t because
the conviction of an accused is profitable, for
prisoners—in Australia, at any rate —are a. very
costly luxury. It isn’t because of its innate
tendency to socialistic legislation—the Law
assisted the accuser and hampered or neglected
the accused long before socialistic legislation was
ever heard of. It can’t be because the Law deems
defending an accused highly immoral; some of
the most respected and esteemed “officers of the
court ” give nearly all their time and brains to.
defending accused persons, and the Law thinks so
mijch of these that it often makes Judges of them.
It seems to be only a habit, and it is a wasteful
and useless habit which brings the Law into con-
tempt, and throws dirt on the rabbit-skin-
bedecked robe of Justice.
An Australian “ Explorer.”
Australian exploration is as easy as shelling peas
nowadays. A Scotchman named Macdonald, who
passed through Sydney a few weeks ago, lately
came back again ; and Sydney Telegraph called
him “ an Australian explorer, ” and gave him half-
a-colurnn or so of space in which to set out the
things he found while he was away. The intrepid
adventurer, it seems, went as far as Cunnamulla,
and even a little beyond, and from there rode
down to Bourke. He didn’t wander much
outside the limit of the country that is
used for settlement, and must have been
within coo-ee of a homestead lessee’s camp
or a boundary-rider’s hut most of the time; and
certainly there are, at this moment, scores of
Australian prospectors who are ten times further
away from settlement. Yet for no other reason
than that the present person comes from England,
alleged Australian papers give this sort of
“exploration” columns of space, and brand the
man who wanders along the tracks that have
existed for at least a quarter of a century “an
Australian explorer.” And either this particular
explorer or the reporter who is alleged to have
interviewed him is a most shockingly igno-
rant person. “We found,” the explorer says,
or is alleged to say, “a great many gems
in the old beds of streams. They were sapphires
and topaz principally, as well as zircon. You
haven’t heard of that gem before, eh ? It is new.”
The zircon new ! An “explorer” comes all the
way from London to tell us that; and apparently
the Telegraph doesn’t know that Coghlan’s
“Seven Colonies” alludes to zircon and a number
of other almost valueless gems as being “ found
throughout Australasia.” It ranks with garnet
and such-like trash. And now, having found out
all these things, the explorer is “off to England to
get machinery to work deposits we have located ”
—Australia being a poor sort of place which, not
knowing what zircons are, couldn’t be expected
to be able to supply a 10-h.p. hauling engine.
Australian papers ought to hit this sort of bump-
tious person on the head ; instead, they encourage
him to be bumptious by advertising him.
+
The Daily Paper.
C. C. writes :
It is fairly safe to prophesy that when the new demo-
cratic daily paper arrives, the Herald-Telegraph com-
bination will lose thousands of subscribers who at present
have practically no option but to purchase one of those
calamitous mis-leaders of public opinion. A business
man must have a daily paper if only to read the market
reports, and the present writer feels deeply the hardship
of having 1 to give a penny to an organ calciilated' to in-
spire loathing and contempt;- There was a time when, by
. ignoring its leading articles, a decent man could read the
Telegraph without using profane language, but novC it is
not safe to let one’s eyes stray from the advertisements.
The present-day great Australian daily, not satisfied with
straight-out, impudent lying in its leading columns, now
distorts even the news of the day by (1) the suppression'
of awkward facts detrimental to its alleged prin-
ciples, (2) by most unfairly paraphrasing the speeches
and “ interviews ” of political opponents, (3) by cunningly
•worded innuendoes of the “ don’t duck him in the horse-
pond ” order, (4) by suggestive denials of “rumors”
having no existence—for instance, the stale trick of
announcing that “ Mr. Hardfist, the Labor candidate,
denies that he was ever the paid advocate of Anarchy and
free love.” For all these, and many other contemptible
dodges, the "leading daily” is anathema to those who
are compelled to “ support ” it because there is no choice.
The leading columns of a daily paper are recognised by
its readers as the space allotted, more orless, t o romance,
and can be “ passed ”■ as such ; but the news columns are
supposed to be a record of matters of fact, and subscribers
have a right to get the news of the day unadulterated
•with the paper’s peculiar policy. If the fact that John
Smith hanged himself at Booligal is wired to the daily
paper, we may be sad, glad, or indifferent, but we don’t
want that item, of news distorted in the attempt
to convey by innuendo that John Smith fell a victim to
the blighting influence of the Old Age Pensions Act, or
hanged himself owing to socialistic legislation. Those
who avoid that acrobatic portion of the paper have no
objection to the blighting influence of its leading columns,
hut, when we find its politics slopping all over the paper
and coloring the current news, we curse the tardy arrival
of that long-looked-for democratic daily, in which we may
relieve our pent-up feelings. It’s nearly time that we had
a daily paper to report fairly other politicians besides
M’Millan, Reid and co., who at present have a monopoly
of “ cheers,” “ loud applause,” and “ hear, hear.”

“Driver ** sends this surprising item :—-
There seems to be still a section of the community with
a vague idea that men in the service of the Victorian
Railway Commissioners work on the basis of an eight
hours day. The following programme of a day’s work for
an engine-driver (striker) may prove instructive :
On duty 6 a.m., North Melbourne engine sheds, clean,
oil, &c., engine. Time, f hour to 1 hour.
Take road to Flinders-street (owing to all other traffic
having precedence over an engine the time taken is
never under £ hour.)
Thus, after having worked for It hours, you couple on
to your train and draw it up to platform, and then your
pay starts. You are down to take a train to Oakleigh
and return ; time, 65 minutes. On return you are off
duty, and have to take your engine back to North Mel-
bourne, draw fires, clean, oil, etc.: another 11 hours.
So we have:
Time worked .. .. 4 hours 5 min.
Time paid for .. .. 1 hour 5 min.
With another broken shift in the afternoon, three more
hours lost time, and a trip to Oakleigh again at night, my
day consists of:
Time worked 17 hours
Time cleaning engines and taking same to
and from Flinders-st 9 hours
Time paid for 8 hours
On top of this we receive notification from Mr. Woodroffe
that all holidays earned by strikers prior to the strike are
forfeited.

More of N.S.W.’s Owe’Sullivan :
It is utterly impossible at present for the Government
to get money at a fair rate of interest. Every work along
the coast, and even the central railway station, and im-
provements in his own district, had suffered through
want of money. But he did not think this condition of
things would last long. Already there were indications
of a change for the better in London. The Government
would watch its opportunity and approach the money
market at the proper time, so as to be able to resume
operations on a number of works which were at present
suspended.
Same helpless old Owe’ with his off-hand assump-
tion that Australia can never pay its debts, can
never make even a drain except by incurring new
debts, and that when we are not incurring new
debts we must just sit down helplessly, and wait,
and abandon all progress, and begin sliding back
into the aboriginal stage. If N.S.W. could only
find a Ministry that had one progressive idea
apart from borrowing !

“B.” writes:—
A single morning of the Metropolitan Quarter Sessions
in Sydney afforded striking justification for the main
principle of B. R. Wise’s Habitual Criminals Bill. One
man convicted of breaking and entering had previously-
been sentenced to life imprisonment; another, found
guilty of stealing, had not been out of gaol for much more
than a month at a time during the last 25 years;
another was described as no sooner out of gaol
than in again ; another had started crime at 13 ‘
years of age, and till now had spent 18 years in gaol ;
another had spent 20 years in gaol; and the final one for
the morning was sent up for four years, the Judge
remarking, “You are no sooner discharged from gaol
than you are back again.” The problem of these re-
lapsing criminals insists on a solution. The Habitual
Criminals Bill can be made to sohe at least some of the
difficulty, and even a partial solution is better than the
present continuous drift.
♦-
The Act of 1890, which constituted the power of
the Melbourne and Metropolitan Board of Works,
contains a clause which provides that if any
householder fails to comply with the order of the
Board to connect his premises with the sewers,
“ he shall be liable to a penalty not exceeding £2O,
and also, after conviction, to a further penalty
not exceeding £5 for every day during which such
offence continues.” Yet, in spite of the fact that
the State Government has received innumerable
notices to sewer the public buildings, the matter
is continually overlooked —a course which even the
meanest householder would pursue at his peril.
This is Kyabramming with a vengeance. Not
only are the State buildings unconnected, but even
the old system of sanitation is neglected, and the
germ thrives like a fraudulent importer with no
Kingston about. The explanation blandly put
forward is that there is no money available for the
purpose—a plea which is summarily disallowed
even to the poorest working men. They have,
times out of number, found themselves compelled
to sell their little cottages, purchased from a small
income after years of thrift and self-denial, because
they were unable to meet the heavy expense laid
upon them in the interests of health by a Parlia-
ment which itself sits in an unsewered building.
Now, if the Metropolitan Board could only intro-
duce a Bill to give Irvine 12 months, or fine him
£IOO, on the lines of his own Coercion Act, some-
thing might possibly be done.

Significant that, after several years of drifting,
the M.L. Oppositionists have elected a leader.
Russell was the last formal leader, but he is too
tired to fight nowadays—l 3 years in Opposition,
without even a remote hope of office, would make
any aged politician tired. It was on his nomina-
tion that Massey, of Franklin, who has been
more or less Opposition whip since 1895, was
appointed. Massey, who is an Onehunga farmer
when he isn’t a politician, isn’t so chilly as
Russell. He starts out, too, with some distant
hopes. Besides Seddon’s awkward fix with
regard to the great whisky question and his
troubles with the Farmers’ Union, the old warrior
has had to announce a severe cutting down of
works expenditure, and that is making more
people “full up of Seddon” than anything else
he could do. Maoriland is a fairly advanced
country; but a lot of it isn’t advanced enough
to forgive the man who refuses to build a railway
for it.
Britannia’s Needs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Britannia needs no bulwarks —no tars with voices
deep,
No hairy sons of sea-dogs to guard her island-
steep;
Her trust is in the Lascar —the Dagoes make her
crew,
She leans her weary trident on her alien A.B.
true.
Britannia needs no Britons stout —she fills her
slimy slum
With every kind of wastrel cur that wolfs a
British crumb ;
Her yeomen wander over-sea to grow Canadian
corn,
And leave the peer and Polish Jew the land
where they were born.
Britannia needs no wheat-fields. Her beef is
raised afar,
Her mutton mostly grew beneath a far-off southern
star;
Her lands are falling fallow—her hillsides keep
the deer—
She loves the antlers passing well and hates the
grazing steer.
Britannia needs a noddle that will show the need
of men !
The need of keeping foreign trash within the
foreign den ;
She needs to base her Greatness on other grounds
than Greed,
And brag the less and do the more to save a
failing breed.
Britannia needs no Empire—she needs to keep her
Isle
A Home for sturdy freemen in a slavish world
and vile ;
If such Britannia’s passion were—l ween that
never more
The Earth should find her battling with the
freedom-loving Boer.
Britannia needs a yeomanry of sturdy men and
hale
To take the place of city weeds who cough at
every gale;
She needs to place a firmer faith in manly bosoms
bold,
And put no more her single trust in ledger-lore
and gold.
Britannia needs to cleanse of cant her crusted
British soul,
To give her poor an honest due and no dishonest
dole,
To let the heathen bow content before his wood
and stone
Until Britannia’s island boast no heathen of its
own!

Town Hall. ..: Ada Crossley Concerts.
♦
The Crossley concerts began with good
houses and worked up to crammed audiences. As
Ada is accepted as one of the best contraltos of
the planet, the wonder is that the Town Hall was
not crammed, from the first, through curiosity
alone. A voice that is one of the best
amongst a population of 1,300,000,000 (so far
as the census-taker has been able to count
them without being eaten while enumerating the
cannibals) ought to be heard —especially as it
first breathed over an area near our Dandenong
Ranges. It is a voice that has been variously
described as “golden,” “mellow,” “honey
colored,” “ dark in color,” and “ without a break.”
The over-soulers who can see the color of a voice
are few as. yet, but they are gathering numbers,
and by-ana-bye the singer who lifts a pale
green voice with a touch of crimson at the
edge will be quite common. Most Sydneysiders
are holding some of their admiration for Miss
Crossley in leash until they can hear her in
oratorio. When she breaks out in the stately,
sublime music of oratorio, the public will know
for the first time exactly how good that splendid
voice can be. In English songs Miss Crossley is
specially appreciated. She lias sung the pathetic
“Little Silver Ring,” the arch, plantation air,
“ Mighty Like a Rose” and the sprightly “ Love,
the Pedlar,” with all the qualities which their
various composers intended them to possess.
Added to her other gifts, she is a generous singer.
She doe's not make a bow take the place-of an
aria. Also she is good to'look at, tall and straight,
with a golden crown of gleaming hair.
a * * * *
The Crossley support is not ricketty.
Jacques Jacobs, a pupil of Joachim, is a violinist
who is never out of a first-class billet, even if he
is not valuable enough to have his right hand
insured for a vast sum. Percy Grainger is a-
pianist and coir poser who may one day be great
in bis art. He has no mannerisms, and produces
great effects in a singularly quiet fashion.
His interpretation of Cyril Scott’s “English
Waltz” has already caused a run on the music
shops for that composer’s pieces. Peterkin, the
basso, is reckoned on all sides “an artist,” and
bis sea-roller voiee never fails to win an encore.
Benno Scherek pianos for the contralto and Peter-
kin. Last of the Crossley concerts will be to-day’s
matinee.
* * * *
“ As You Like It,” without which no
Bacon-drama season is genuine, is the third of
Musgrove’s Shakspearori productions at Sydney
Royal. It at least equals, and at some points
surpasses, even “ Midsummer Night’s Dream ” in
the impressiveness of its mounting. There are
long and tiresome waits ; but the sets which show
the Forest of Arden form an entire gallery of
stage pictures of such rare beauty that the waiting
is atoned for. Some of the imported people
appear at their best in this production. Miss
Kerin, for instance, the Rosalind of the show, has
done nothing else so good in Sydney ; nor has Miss
Rignold, though her Viola in “Twelfth Night”
was excellent in patches. Explosiveness is Miss
Kerin’s besetting sin; no wonder her throat
suffers at times. But especially in the first act,
and later, in the scene in "which, disguised in
doublet and hose, she sets herself to cure
her Orlando of love, she is as winning as
one could , wish a Rosalind to be. Miss Rignold,
an actress of a severer type, always restrained,
often indeed not so much alive as the situation
warrants, nevertheless always graceful, drops
some of those mannerisms of speech which, espe-
cially in “Midsummer Night’s Dream,” often
made it difficult to follow her lines. With Nor-
man Partridge presenting Orlando as no one who
judged him by his previous work would have
thought him capable of doing, the three principal
parts are well filled. Yet the production will be
remembered for the pictures rather than for any-
thing else.
* * * *
Next to these comes Athol Forde’s Adam.
A fine old fellow is Adam, none. better in the
whole play ; and when Orlando leads him off,
with “Cheerily, Adam, cheerily,” the audience
feels for perhaps the only moment in the 3| hours
a twinge of emotion. On the other hand, W. H.
Denny’s work, as Touchstone, is mostly thrown
away ; the professional fool’s fooling is wearisome
nowadays. Kenningham, also, has poor oppor-
tunities. He had the fool’s part in “Twelfth
Night,” and that suited his comic-opera manner
much better than his present part of Amiens, the
singing gentleman who attends a banished duke.
Miss Milton also has the thankless part of the
poor-witted “country wench ” Audrey ; Sydney
is fortunate in having had the chance to see
her in something else. Alfred Bucklaw is the
melancholy Jacques who carries the burden of
some of the monumental speeches that dis-
tinguish what otherwise does not take high
rank among the master’s plays. His is a
well-considered performance; gentlemen who
recite “ The Seven Ages of Man ’’ should turn up
to see and hear how Bucklaw does it —an acid,
morose philosopher wouldn’t do it as it is generally
done by young men in evening clothes, who have
only read the lines in Somebody’s Reciter. And
the other people who don’t, want to learn how to
recite “ The Seven Ages of Man ” ought to turn <
up, if only to see the Forest of Arden as Hawes 3
Craven and the other artists have pictured it. a
*# * *
“Toreador,” at Her Majesty's (Sydney),
struts in triumph, although there is, up to date,
no gore on the sawdust. For the matter of that,
there is no sawdust. Spain is a perfect mine for
the comic operator, as well as for the grand
operator. The Spanish brave wears rosettes on his
shoes, and carries his dagger, and makes love—all
with the same kind of ferocious grace. Of course the
odor of garlic would discount the passion of his love
if it were sung too close to Donna Teresa. Fortu-
nately he usually sings it from the footpath,
whilst she leans out of an upper window. There
is a “Carlist agitator” in this opera. He is
played by Fred Young, and he aliases under
the name of Rinaldo. His get-up would hang
him at sight—if the Spanish Government did
the sighting before he got his artillery into good
position. He carries infernal machines about in a
sack and says “Hist” in blood-freezing tones.
Another minor character is that of" a band-
master which, played by W. Stafford, causes
a major quantity of laughter. He interrupts
the Dan O’Connor periods of the Governor
of Villaya with his rub-a-dub, and plays the
fool with a new kind of idiocy. Miss Florence
Young’s voice has lost nothing in popularity by
the public (interview) announcement by Miss
Crossley that she is better than anyone in the
same line in London. The “Toreador” will kill
his invisible bull to music for many weeks yet. j

That flickery picture thing which is
variously called the biograph, bioscope, wonder-
graph, shadowgraph, shudderscope and things
like that, is to turn up again at Queen’s Hall,
Pitt-street, Sydney, on Saturday. It is described
this time as American and electrical, and is called
the Yitagraph, and is supposed not to flicker. If
it doesn’t flicker it will be viewed with satisfaction.
* * *
“Mistakes Will Happen” goes better at
Sydney Palace now that the co. has settled
down to its stride, and some of the situations
which were good before are now extra-good.
When the nagging ladies are advised to moderate
their language “because there are gentlemen
present,” and when the dour matron shoos her
husband off the premises “because he annoys the
dog,” the laughter is now frequent and free, for
the points of these jokes have got into the public
mind, which was hardly the case on the first night.
A playwright who makes anew or nearly new joke
inflicts on the co. which produces his wares a great
deal of responsibility ; the public laugh rises much
more readily to old and familiar gags. When the
joke is quite new a gun ought to be fired immedi-
ately before it is uttered to fix the public mind on
it, and a flag ought to be waved immediately after
the joke.

M'Adoo’s Jubilee Singers are to open
their dark countenances in song at Sydney Town
Hall on Saturday, and will continue the process
till further notice. The Singers have always sung
something worth hearing in the past, and they
will doubtless do so again. The fact that they
can come into this country freely ought to demon-
strate to a lot of people, who have been led away
by the foolish brayings of Carruthers and co., that
the Immigration Restriction Act isn’t the insane
document those persons have brayed it to be.
** # #
The clawing of Pagel by his pet lion was
an unexpected addition to the programme of
Wirth’s circus that recent eventful evening. The
dog, which usually bustles round as a peacemaker,
sat still that night in a corner with a wistful look
in his eye; he seemed to know things were serious,
and apparently reckoned it might be better for
himself if he didn’t interfere. Leo became un-
usually violent, and when Pagel was lifting him
managed to spring away and claw the Hercules.
Pagel immediately dashed him against the iron
bars, and would have fallen upon him and torn him
up, had not the shrill voice of Mrs. Pagel induced
him to leave the enclosure. The huge, brawny,
fair-haired Teuton is not the slightest bit afraid of
the lion, and the animal is still half scared of him.
The lion is now three years old, and just beginning
to feel his strength, and apparently there are lots
of exciting arguments between him and his master
in sto.re yet. “ Dick Turpin’s Ride to York ”is a
Wild mix-up of men, in various colored coats and
pale-green moonlight, who rush about the ring and
ride in and out of the tents at full gallop. The
two-bright features of the “Ride” are when
Turpin takes Black Bess over a five-bar gate at
full gallop, and when that splendid mare falls
down in a dying condition and is carried out by
the company on a stretcher to the strain of the
“ Dead March in Saul.” But this writer prefers
the Haute Ecole act; there’s a lot more in that.
To-night, Thursday, two tall Hindus will wrestle
for the championship of White Australia.
* # #
Captain Starlight, whom Rolf Boldrewood,
his creator, killed, but whom Alfred Dampier
decided ought to have a chance to live out a purer
and nobler life, is drawing larger crowds to
Sydney Criterion than had been hoped for.
There is at present no thought of offering any
other attraction, and the fact that in seven nights
14,000 people, or thereabouts, have cheered the
picturesque protector of women and robber of
mail-coaches seems to be a pretty strong hint that
any sudden change would be a mistake.

j “ Valentine Day ” writes :
i “ 0.K.” mistakes the grounds of comparison which
should be between the cast of the original Rignoldian
production (IBS9-’9O) of “ Midsummer Night’s Dream ’
and that of the late Musgrovian production. The 1892
performance was a revival, and greatly inferior. In the
earlier cast, the Titania was Madge Seymour, pretty and
petite, who sang sweetly and danced beautifully,
and excelled in comedy moments. Writing from memory,
I imagine that Bessie Rignold (Dora’s sister) was
the Oberon, and she certainly made a fine figure and sang
the music excellently. Harry Jewett and Arthur Green-
away played the Athenian lovers; Kate Bishop and
Maribel Greenwood played Helena and Hermia. Bessie
Colville was the Puck, and by far and away the most limber
yet seen here. She was light of foot as a forest fawn,
and her exits were made with lightning rapidity—at the
end of a wire. The six rude mechanics were George
Rignold, Harry Leston, Stirling Whyte, Frank Ayrton,
Joe Tolano, and Frank Harcourt. I think that Charles
Hall played Egeus, and Hazlitt the Duke. Tilly Salenger
and Minna Phillips, to the best of my recollection, were
the singing fairies. In my opinion, taken player for
player, the Rignoldian cast, save in one or two instances,
was much the superior.
* * »
At Sydney Tivoli the only new turn this
week is that of the Powers Bros. They are
clever wire-rope acrobats; but the fashion for
circus-turns in music halls seems to be fading, and
they rouse no enthusiasm. Next Saturday Mar-
zella and her performing birds appear. This is
reported, on excellent authority, to have been a
boom and a blessing to Harry Rickards’ Mel-
bourne theatre; and doubtless it will prove the
same in Sydney.
** * *
“The Union Jack,” revived at Melb.
Royal on Saturday, has been one of Bland Holt’s
best business concerns for about 15 years. The
late George Gordon spread himself upon the
scenery of the first production; subsequently
Spong took a hand in the panorama; and now
the “picturesque illustrations” mighty fine
and large illustrations at that —are supplied
by John Brunton. Eleven scenes and a lot
of naval and military supers, are crowded
into a well-meaning melodrama. There are
no steam hammers or dynamite catastrophes
appertaining to this oft-told tale, nor does the
dialogue ever descend to the penny dreadful level,
but at times the conversation is rather dull and
prolonged. Love-scenes in melodrama are mostly
built to please the silliest part of the audience, and
the present case offers no exception to the rule.
Otherwise “Union Jack” commands respect.
Everything is strictly unoriginal, but the eternal
verities were considered by the authors when they
drew their long-bow together, and the one murder
in the piece befalls the principal villain at the
right time in a reasonable way. A neatly placed
deed of bloodshed has many charms.
* * * *
Two nice tall girls, blessed with £30,000
apiece by their defunct father, are pursued by
two bad men, and when one of these hard citizcms
has dug a hole in the other fellow’s chest all goes
well with the two good young men. They get the
£60,000 between them as a reward of merit. Also
a third girl is left at liberty to recover from her
base betrayal by the villain who has gone to
answer for his sins. Nobody seems to be looking
for work in this drama. The characters, without
exception, enjoy their three square meals a day ;
and Bland Holt, as an able-bodied seaman, gets
drunk whenever the spirit moves him. Mrs.
Bland on one occasion threatens to marry Brown,
the soldier-man, if her rolling mariner doesn’t
reform, but he vanquishes her scruples by climbing
up an apple-tree and pelting his rival with the
fruit thereof. Walter Baker scores the maximum
number of points as a hero; also, Miss Ross,
armed once again with her pocket-handkerchief,
is a sympathetic believer in Baker’s inn-o-cence.
JVliss Harrie Ireland supplies a happy perform-
ance of the other orphan sister, whose harmless
adorer is billed as “an army swell,” although he
doesn’t look it. Miss Pollock makes a jaunty
pretence of being Bland Holt’s mother on wash-
ing day; whilst Miss Rutledge walks feebly
through the part of the lost innocent who listened
to Albert Norman by the light of the moon.
Styan is artistic as a moderately evil baronet, and
Brightie Smith, sad to say, makes no appearance
whatever.

Ada Crossley, who had a pretty big Mel-
bourne reputation before she became a distin-
guished singer on a distant shore, is making her
first appearance at Melbourne Town Hall on
Saturday next, Oct. 3. Percy Grainger, the
golden-haired kiddie pianist of yore, returns to
the’scene of his juvenile achievements on the same
evening, a full-grown genius with a “Mr.” to his
name. The Ada Crossley concerts are fixed for
Oct. 3,6, 8, Id, and 12, and when those five dates
have been named further remarks would be super-
fluous. Everybody who knows what’s what in
musical entertainment will attend one or other of
the concerts. The wisest people will attend them
all.
*# * #
“Resurrection” is to run for another
week at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne), the demand
for that depressing Russian fable being fairly
brisk. Whether the great masses of feminine
public are most attracted by the charms of Julius
Knight or the horrors of the prison scene is a
debatable question. Julius Knight, formerly of
Ancient Rome, is an established favorite, and his
fur trimmiugs are much admired, but the prison
scene likewise has fascinations for the more
curious sek. Prisons are such free-and-easy
places—in Russia. All the convict ladies
picnic together under the supervision of
male warders who whack them with the knout
(off the stage) when they feel so disposed. Also
gentlemen visitors are permitted to drop in and
supply the drunkest lydy with unlimited liquor
money, what time her companions are at
prayers. There never'was so much gin and shriek
in any previous prison scene, therefore the present
opportunities for a moral lesson ought not to be
missed. They will terminate on October 9th, and
“Monsieur Beaucaire” is to take the stage on
Saturday, the 10th.

“Are You a Mason ? ” concludes its little
joke at Melbourne Princess’s this Friday, making
way for “Oh! What a Night!” The comedy
with the exclamatory title can’t flourish for more
than two or three weeks, apparently, owing to the
theatre having been engaged by the Caledonian
Society, and an amateur Mikado co. and other
intrepid organisations.

The Williamson co. made a brilliant finish
at Adelaide Royal with one night of “ The Chris-
tian,” Hall Caine’s own version of his gloomy story
being used. Before that the co. had given a short
trial to “If I were King,” which showed Athol-
wood at his best; Cuyler Hastings and Miss Lane
doing good work in parts not too well suited to
them. Also the grey charger seemed to be some-
what out of condition; but Edmund Gwenn
showed howagood man can make a small part stand
out. The mounting was excellent. The Adelaide
Royal stage is only moderate in size, and when
the houses of mediaeval Paris, and the Court party,
and the rabble all got on it at once there was_ not
much room to move about. In “Secret Service”
Hastings’American accent got a chance to hold
up its head at last; also Atholwood played a
nigger and Mabel Lane an old lady. William
Anderson is now back from Broken Hill with a
human ladder that descends from roofs and" robs
steam-hammers of their accustomed food, the
virtuous maiden. Mark Hambourg is giving
recitals at the Town Hall, but the Tivoli is closed
for the present.

The large figures in this society’s balance-sheets are a joke
or a tragedy—it depends upon the point of view.
Nominally, it has £226,430 of capital intact; it has
£212,022 ‘of fixed deposits at 2J per cent. ; it has (on
paper) £483,950 represented by loans on mortgage. Yet
these £483,950 of loans don’t earn enough to pav the
society’s way, notwithstanding that it gets £212,022 at 21
per cent. What the loans are worth, and what, therefore,
the alleged intact capital is worth, are consequently
things that make one sad to think about. Nevertheless,
some progress is being made, especially in the matter of
reducing the liabilities to depositor's, mortgagees, and
banks ; the total has moved in the last few years in this
way
1897 .. £383,689 1901 .. £301,177
1898 .. 338,966 1902 .. 288,630
1899 .. 322,551 1903 .. 271,700
1900 .. 813,838
In six years, therefore, these liabilities have been reduced
by £111,989, and in the same period the assets have been
reduced by £173,711 (paper value). If the liquidation
can proceed at the same rate, the balance of these
liabilities can be got rid of by sacrificing about £425,000
(paper value) of the assets ; and that would leave the
society with about £77,000 of its capital. Even that is
something a long way better than a few years ago seemed
at all possible.
* * * *
Victorian Chief Justice Madden is at work on a
brain-racking insurance case. Twenty-five years ago the
Australian Alliance Assurance Cos. paid a large amount for
shares in the Australasian Insurance Cos. The Alliance’s
fire branch for some years satisfied the liabilities arising
from the purchase of these shares, until the fire branch
became overdrawn ; and then, in 1884, a cheque for
£31,834 was drawn on the life branch and paid to the fire
branch. The Alliance’s life policy-holders (through
Joseph Webster) now want that £31,834 back, with
interest since 1884. Also, they allege that profits haven’t
come their way in the shape of bonuses as they should
have done —briefly, that the shareholders in the Alliance
have had in dividends a good deal of what ought to have
gone to the policy-holders in bonuses. The main dispute
is complicated by a subsidiary dispute as to what has been a
fair share of expenses for the life branch to carry: practi-
cally, the Alliance has left off taking new business (it has
only issued 46 new policies in the last two years, and that is
a good deal above the average), while its fire business has
increased ; yet the proportion of expenses hasn’t, it is
claimed, been readjusted. The defence is that everything
has been done for the best, and the Trusts Act and the
Act of Limitation and other solid masses of law are relied
upon ; also that the mere policy-holder isn’t entitled to
pry into the question of distributing the profits of a pro-
prietary concern—if anybody except the shareholder is to
do it, it is, not the policy-holder, but the man who is to
draw the money when the policy-holder dies. The Alliance
is not the only Australian assurance co. which has
practically gone out of the life business ; and though
most of these manage to pay very large dividends t)
shareholders, no bonuses seem to be reaching the
policy-holders. Possibly the latter have no legal claim ;
it all'depends upon the articles of association or deed of
settlement—in the case of the Alliance it is claimed that
the shareholders have a right to only 25 per cent, of the
profits, the policy-holders owning the rest. If, then, the
present case doesn’t settle policy-holders’ rights to
bonuses generally, it ought to set' them inquiring what

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wai gmui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral hiveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

Even after the awful warning that they had
last year, a number of Mt. Kembla miners have been
caught carrying matches and smoking material into the
mine. The'9s corpses which last year’s disaster produced
having failed to warn men off this sort of criminal foolish-
ness, a police court now tried a half-crown fine ! A fine
of 30 pence for a practice that is liable to make 95 corpses
is at the rate of rather over three corpses a penny.

their legal rights are in the special insurance concern
(especially any proprietary concern) in which they are
interested : whether, in short, they are to have a share
of the profits right to the end, or whether bonuses will
be paid only as long as the concern is seeking business,

this could not yet be written), because in that case the
position is said to be clearly defined by deed, and the
complication is simply the result of mixing up the two
main branches of insurance business.

1901 .. 12,255 6517 5933 11,450 1821
1902 .. 12,566 5019 6537 11,556 2831
1903 .. 12,684 4484 6706 11,190 4325
The shareholders’ paid capital is £4721, but £3500 of this
went to pay for the goodwill of a previous institution ;
part of this amount has been written off, and the item
now stands in the co.'s balance-sheet at £2OOO. Very
little information is given as to the company’s business ;
not even the total amount of assurance in force is stated.
A valuation is, however, promised as at the close of the
current year, and thereafter triennially. Even then, on
account of the extremely complex nature of most of the
policies, the position will not be clear unless the funds of
the industrial and the ordinary branches are separated.
In the industrial branch all sorts of frilled policies are
available ; and it isn’t advisable that the insurance fund
to meet the plain, ordinary policies should be mixed up
with the insurance fund for the frilled sort of policy.
Business, however, in either sort seems to be dull; prac-
tically no progress whatever has been made during the
past two or three years—except in the matter of expenses.
Premiums (which include payments under the bottle-
of-medicine, Sarah Gamp, and other frilled policies before-
mentioned) amounted to £12,021 in 1901; in 1903 they
amounted to £12,313. Meantime, however, expenses
have risen from £5933 to £6471, and the dividend (£235)
makes tho total of £6706 shown above.
This shows the awful waste of the industrial
assurance system—the system under which, instead of
saving up a quarter’s or half-year’s premium, the working
man encourages the canvasser to call round every
Monday for twopence or thereabouts. The People’s
Prudential has practically added nothing to its business
in the last two or three years; and a society doing
ordinary business would, in those circumstances, show
an expense-rate of £lO per cent, of its income. But an in-
dustrial co. has to spend so much time and shoe-leather
hunting up the twopences, and it has to spend so much
more time and leather hunting up people who will take
the place of other- people who won’t pay the twopences
any longer, that, in spite of the net progress being prac-
tically nil, the expense-rate is enormous, and is still
growing. Year by year since the company started the
rate has been as follows :
Expenses in Proportion to Income.
1897.. £44 1 8 percent. 1901.. £4B 8 3 per cent.
1898.. 39 16 3 „ 1902.. 52 0 5 „
1899.. 39 13 5 „ 1903.. 52 17 5 „
1900.. 41 0 8 „ V
In the last two years a dividend of 5 per cent, on capital
is included ; deducting that, the rate was £SO ss. lOd. for
1902 and £sl os. 4d. for 1903. In view of the fact that
the premium income is practically stationary, these
figures could hardly be less satisfactory. The outlook for
customers has seemed hitherto to be unpromising ; and
it looks more unpromising now than ever.

* * * *
Sold recently in Tasmania, for £6200, the old
steamer Kangaroo. The ancient puffer was one of
Hobart’s curiosities, it having run between that city and
Bellerive for the last 46 years. It was built at Hobart in
1855 for the carriage of stock, vehicles and passengers,
the hull being made l of TasmanSan blue gum and stringy
bark. The old battler is still in excellent condition ; even
the venerable and obsolete “ beam ” engine is sound, and
the piston-rod hasn’t worn one thirty-second part of an
inch. The present master, Capt. James S. Taylor, has
safely guided the old tub through calm and storm lor
41 years.

• * * *
Premier See told a questioner in N.S. W. Assembly
a few nights back that the State’s steam yacht Victoria
costs the country £B7B a year in insurance premiums.
The almost useless craft is supposed to be worth some-
thing over £25,000, so it ought to be covered by some
insurance. There seems to be no particular reason, how-
ever, why the Government should not create its own
insurance fund —no particular reason, anyhow, apart
from the fact that if it did start a fund the Government
would probably lay hold of it, and use it instead of
revenue the first time nobody was looking. Not that
an insurance fund is any more necessary than ah
ordinary sinking fund. In fact, from one point of
view a sinking fund is more necessary, because, while it
is at least possible that the Victoria will not founder or
be otherwise destroyed by accident, it is absolutely cer-
tain that both it and every other thing that is made of
wood, or stone, or iron, or putty will fall to pieces some
day, from old age and ordinary wear and tear ; so the
country that can’t see the need of a sinking fund to wipe
out the cost of the perishable concern which was paid for
with borrowed money can’t reasonably be expected to
see the need of an insurance fund

The reserves, with the current half-year’s appropriations,
stand : Insurance, £43,000; depreciation, £40,750 ; re-
placement, £7500.
The unspeakable Bent system of management is
■ working havoc with the Victorian railway revenue. Bent
knocked off trains wholesale, and. traffic took to other
channels. He saved money on repairs till the dilapidated
rolling stock couldn’t carry even what traffic was left,
and thousands of tons of goods that would bring in hand-
some freights lie waiting the trucks that never come.
For the first 2$ months of the present financial year the
revenue is £51,550 less than it was during the same
period in 1902 ; and, if the slump continues, the year’s
loss will be fully £250,000.

Editor Bulletin. —I see the Griffin chain-letter
is still going on. At a shop in the Imperial Arcade,
Sydney, you can purchase Griffin chain-letters for 3d. a
dozen or 2s. per 100. These are unopened. They contain
the stamps collected by American sympathisers, and an
assurance that “ the chain ” is unbroken. . It is pitiful to
read the warm expressions of goodwill in some of the
letters. I purchased a dozen, all sent from America, with
various dates in 1902. —S.

* * * *
The quinquennial report of the Queensland
Registrar of Friendly Societies shows that, in the northern
State as elsewhere, some of these poor men’s insurance
institutions still earn a miserable rate of interest on their
funds, the average for the whole of the societies being
£2 18s. per cent. Only one society, the Manchester Order
of Oddfellows in North Queensland, earns over 4 per
cent.; the Brisbane Foresters, numerically one of the
strongest, earns 1£ per cent. It is not at all surprising,
therefore, in view of this obviously bad business
management, that, according to actuarial calculations,
the societies will be unable to pay anything like the
amounts which, they have undertaken to pay. The
Foresters show up, on the whole, worst in this respect;
of the three “ districts ” one could only pay 14s. 6d. in
the £, even if its funds were earning 3 per cent., which
they aren’t; another could pay 15s. Id. (in this case the
funds are actually earning less than 1£ per cent.), and
another could pay 16s. There is only one society, and
that a small German concern, which has a surplus and is
likely to be able to pay all its members 20s. in the £ of
what it has undertaken to pay. In all other cases the
contributions must either be increased dr the benefits
reduced.
Maoriland friendly societies show a much better
result ;• yet in his last report the Government actuary
says there are still some managers who, “ through ignor-
ance, carelessness, or indifference, have never realised
the fact that the regular and judicious investment of the
funds at the highest, rate of interest obtainable, con-
sistent with perfect security, is a primary condition of
success in every friendly society.” The aggregate sick
and funeral funds, notwithstanding this, averaged a
return of £4 9s. 7d. per cent.; one district of Manchester
Oddfellows realised £6 3s. per cent. One of the officers of
the State Life Assurance Department has been set aside
during several years past to help put these societies on
a firm financial basis ; and he seems to be doing it.

The S.A. Gas Cos. differs from the Melbourne and Metro-
politan (Sydney) Gas cos. inasmuch as it covers practically
the whole field of gas-making in its State it supplies, as
well as the city and suburbs, Port Adelaide, Glenelg,
Port Pirie, Gawler, Kapunda, and Strathalbyn—every
fair-sized centre, that is, in S. Australia. All but £7700
of the reserves are used in the business ; the amount
mentioned is in S.A. State stock. The capital is
£250,000; and on this and the reserves, as at the com-
mencement of the year, the return was equal to £8 16s.
Bd. per cent. The co. is one of the very few S. Australian
concerns which don’t show for the past year a better
return than usual, and nothing in particular seems to
have been responsible for it—there has been a general
small decline in receipts and a general small increase in
expenses.
* • * *
Before the Westralian Government’s water
supply approached Kalgoorlie, that beautiful thing,
Private Enterprise, supplied the people with drinking
water at an average rate of about £3 15s. per 1000 gallons.
It would doubtless still be charging that if there were no
Government supply. The Government supply costs
about 6s. 6d. per 1000 gallons, and doesn’t pay at that,
and it is proposed to levy a rate and make other arrange-
ments, which will make the price something under £l.
Bub the people, who thought it quite natural that Private
Enterprise should charge £3 155., denounce the idea of
the State charging less than one-third of that as shame-
ful tyranny. Lands Minister Hopkins suggested, that, as
the hotelkeepers were making most of the row, they
might reduce the price of drinks from Is. to 6d. ; with
smaller profits, their municipal valuation wouldn’t be so
high, and then their water-rate, being based on the
valuation, would fall correspondingly. The publicans’
opinion of Hopkins is unpublishable.
* * * *
Metal prices are again making miners feel un-
easy. Copper has eased a further 30s. within 10 days,
and lead has had another drop. Silver alone has had a
spring ; but speculation seems to be at the bottom of
that, so it probably won’t last long. Almost the whole
of the recovery which took place all round early in the
year has now been lost, and prices are back to very near
the level pf this time last year. The following figures
show the recent movement:
' Sept., 1900. Sept. 1902. March, 1903. Sept., 1903.
Copper £73 0 0 £52'12 6 £64 0 6 £55 10 0
Tin 132 10 0 118 10 0 136 11 6 119 5 0
Lead 17 15 0 10 17 6 13 4 6 11 1 3
Spelter 19 5 0 19 0 0 ,22 18 0 21 0 0
Silver 2 51-16 1 11J 1 10. V 2 3 7-16
September, 1900, was about the height of the last metal
boom ; from that point there was a rapid fall till the end
of 1901, followed by a recovery which reached its best in
March, 1903. Lead has had the worst relapse. It was on
the price passing £l2los. that some of the smaller Broken
Hill mines resumed work ; and the present fall must
make their position very critical, notwithstanding the
improvement in silver. Lead-market reports have, never-
theless, been a trifle more cheerful than they were a while
back; one significant feature is that, while the imports
to Great Britain from the U.S. in the first half of 1902
amounted to 28,968 tons, they fell to 18,532 tons in
the first half of this year. This probably means that
the stocks accumulated in the U.S. during 1899-1900
are about used up. Copper stocks are also stated
to be again low. The effect of last year’s low prices is
shown by the accounts of the Calumet and Hecla,
America’s big producer. To April 30th, 1903, it turned
out in 12 months 42,216 tons refined copper, as against
42,462 tons the previous year ; but dividends were only
2,000,000, as against 4,000*000 dollars. As to spelter, on
which Brcken Hill builds much hope for the future, the
report of the Belgian Soci6t6 de la Vieille Montagne,
the largest spelter manufacturer in the world, is par-
ticularly interesting. After putting aside in 1902 a reserve
of 515,066 fr. (about £32,600) as required by law (Aus-
tralian-law hasn’t yet reached that degree of common-
sense), it made a divisible profit of 3,726,924 fr. (£149,000).
“ The production of zinc in Europe,” it says, “ showed
an increase of about 12,000 tons over 1901, but the con-
sumption appeared to be larger, and the price of the
metal was much better. The, improved situation was
due to the increasing demand for zinc for various pur-
poses. . . . The production (of ore) does not seem to have
increased in accordance with the demand.”
* * * *
The washing-up process of the unfortunate
Great Central Freehold copper mines at Mount Hope
(N.S.W.) came before the Melbourne law courts last week,
and was very lightly reported in the dailies. The co.
sued certain parties (of Chillagoe fame) for non-fulfilment
of contract to purchase shares. The defendants did not
limit their pleadings to legal grounds alone, but alleged
misrepresentations. This was a bold move, more especially
as the principal defendant was a director of the plaintiff
company at the time the contract, and alleged misrepre-
sentations, were made. The decision of the court, how-
ever, after a week’s trial, was a vindication of the Gt.C.F.
directors personally. A verdict for the full amount-
claimed, viz., £3333 16s. Bd., was given against Messrs.
Chapman, Higgins and F. P. Brett, the defendants.

wants something to liven it up, and I would not be sur-
prised if the Palmer supplied that something.

SOCIETY
POLITICAL ECONOMY.
HYMEN (to legal lady, spinster, Ac.): “Drop it!
What this country wants is more wives and
Toothers and fewer lawyers!”
A Perth (W.A.) Sunday paper, edited by
Tommy Walker, ex-M.L.A. of N.S.W., lately
published an alleged libel on Perth’s champion
litigant, who took on the legal feud with the light
of battle shining in his eye. The affair, of course,
started with a writ claiming damages for libel.
The paper’s lawyers then issued a summons for
security for costs, which was dismissed owing to
an informality. Paper’s statement of defence
becoming overdue, the plaintiff signed an inter-
locutory judgment, and applied to the court for
assessment of damages. Paper then applied for,
and secured, the setting aside of the judgment
with costs, and sent its bailiff to collect the costs.
Bailiff found nothing to seize but an assortment
of papers of no value to anyone but the plain-
tiff, who secured the permission of the Sheriff to
constitute himself bailee of them, and to use
them in the legal campaign meanwhile. Next
round commenced by the paper taking out another
summons for security for costs. Granted this time
by the Chief Justice. Plaintiff then appealed to
the Full Court against the order, and, after a
desperate encounter against eminent legal horse-
hair, induced the Court to set aside the order
(Chief Justice not dissenting). The paper’s law-
yer asked that the costs of the setting aside of the
interlocutory judgment might be set off against
the plaintiff’s costs, but the Court held that, hav-
ing elected to issue execution, and having seized
the valuable lot of unmarketable papers aforesaid,
the paper must be content therewith. Paper or-
dered to deliver defence within seven days. Next
round opened with the paper commenting on the
decision, and suggesting, that one of the Judges
was biassed. The plaintiff promptly moved for a
rule nisi for contempt. Refused, as his case was
not prejudiced; but next day the Attorney-
General moved for, and obtained, a rule. Tommy
Walker, on the return day, attended in person,
unaccompanied by legal shark, and explained that
he meant merely unconscious bias. He was let off
on payment of the costs of application. Many
more rounds are now in prospect.
* * * *
The up-to-date daily paper runs its serial
story on mutoscope lines; just as something
wicked is about to happen the curtain goes down.
Sydney D.T.’s shocker on a recent Thursday con-
cluded—
“ Your skin is white, is it not? You are proud of its
smoothness, its fairness, the pink flesh glowing beneath
the outer white. Well, since you are so proud of it,
’twould be a pity to keep so much of it hidden. And so,
your Highness,” she made a mock courtesy, “ Princess
Inez of Veragua, we propose that you shall be stripped,
so that all may admire, not only those here, but the men
of my establishment, the grooms, the lackeys, aye, even
the old Indians. 'Tis not often they have the oppor-
tunity to see pink and white.”
[(To be Continued.)
Of course, everybody who read that item bought
Friday’s issue to see all about the lady being
undressed, and found her jogging along through
the story with all her clothes on.
*.* * •
Are you a Mason 1 George Coppin is
appealing to the aproned citizens of Victoria for
the sum of £l2OO to endow another two “ homes ”
at the Melbourne Masonic Asylum, which, as he
explains, is practically a Freemasons’Old Colonists’
Association, the minimum age for an inmate being
50 years. The veteran’s circular is self-con-
gratulatory and interesting. He talks about his
own long connection with the craft and what he
has done for it. In March 1845 he was initiated
into the secret of the mystic gridiron; in 1871 a
committee of Brethren recognised his services
with a testimonial; subsequently he was elected
boss cockie of the Victoria Grand Lodge; and now
he states that when he is “ summoned to appear
before the Great Grand Master (they have made
the Almighty a Mason) to give an account of his
work, it will give him great happiness to feel that
the buildings for Masonic Old Colonists are
thoroughly protected by endowment against the
precarious and uncertain whims of casual sub-
scribers.” Good old Cop! A Bulletin paragraph
lately set him down as an evergreen of 90 winters,
but he’s not yet so ancient as that. He was born
April Bth, 1819.
* » * *
During the recent gales in Melbourne the
lightship off Gellibrand, which marks a dangerous
reef, broke from its moorings and drifted three miles
out towards St. Kilda, where it was eventually
captured. Apparently it hasn’t had any new
moorings for a long while, for fear of damaging
Irvine’s “surplus.” There has been a constant
agitation for a decent structure in the vicinity,
the materials for which lie close at hand, but
Bent’s Public Works Department won’t do any-
thing till a P. and O. steamer goes ashore some
night while the lightship is having “time-off.”
Bent invented the idea of time-off without salary,
by the way.
* * * *
Two recent Yarra drowning cases are
monuments to the parsimony of the Victorian
Government—and particularly of the Bent Public
Works Department. Like everything else under-
taken in Melbourne at the public expense, the
Yarra improvement scheme lies half finished, as
it will lie until Cohen springs another loan. But
there is no excuse for the authorities leaving two
large excavations, some 30ft. deep, with perpen-
dicular sides, and containing 13ft. of water, unpro-
tected. The place is a veritable death-trap for
those who don t know the Government’s habit of
digging holes and then leaving them till next loan.
A watchman was employed at night time for three
years, but was lately withdrawn, as Irvine wanted
his wages to help the “surplus.” There is no
fence—it has also gone into the surplus'; and
there have been two drownings in one week. Also
the mother of ,a drowned boy died of shock—
which makes three corpses in Irvine’s “surplus.”
Apparently nothing will be done till Bent himself
falls in.
• * * *
About the most tragic event connected
with the fall of the late Queensland Ministry was
the enforced abandonment of a gorgeous palace
car just completed for vice-regal and Ministerial
jaunts. Said car contained drawing room, bath-
room, lavatories, most elaborate fittings, lovely
panels—some “in the nude ” —painted at a cost of
£5O by a local artist, vice-regal coat-of-arms, &c.,
&c. Total cost about £3OO0 —a large sum to be
made good out of unfortunate swagmen’s poll-
taxes. And the Philp Government was kicked
out of office just as the car was completed.

He told his washup that the bruises on his wife’s
face arose solely “ through his kissing her when
he came home from Maoriland.” As the lady was
reported to be bruised all over, however, the
court looked on the explanation suspiciously, and
made it £lO or three months.

noble spirits (publicans) are shamefully misrepresented.
No honest observer can deny that the influence of bar-
maids tends to put down drink.

Habitues of Melbourne City Court had
the pleasure, the other day, of gazing on a very
brave man who took on what will probably turn
out a very large contract. A young woman, after
being fined £5 for her severalth offence of using
“ langwidge,” was charged with having insufficient
means, &e. Whereupon a hero stepped forward
and announced that, as he was very fond of the
lady, he proposed preparing a home in the suburbs
for her, and in that home he proposed to teach
and reform her. When she was completely re-
formed he intended to marry her and live an
idyllic life for ever after. The worldly-wise beak
smiled grimly, but allowed the defendant to go
away to be “reformed.” The man who took on
this huge contract was aged 23, and earned 30s. a
week, and it was shown that the lady, to block
the arresting constable, had undressed in the main
street. Thirty shillings a week won’t go far
towards “ reforming ” a lady of that reckless habit.
But the young man has a lot of excitement before
him.

THE NEW ORDER OF THINGS.
MRS. DE VERE JONES (who has advertised for a
parlor-maid): “ You have had the journey
out for nothing, I am sorry to say, as I am
already suited; however, as it is a long way
I'll pay your fare. Did you come by tram
or ’bus?”
APPLICANT (pertly): “Thank you, I just ran out
on me bike.”
A sweet spring-time advfc. in the “Missing
Friends” column of Melbourne Age the other
week was calculated to make more than one girl
happy. “ You are my love; every tree and shrub
as it blossoms tells how your boy loves you.”
Simply that and nothing more. No hint as to the
identity of the ad vertiser or of the girl. Although
he called himself a “boy,” he was possibly the
proverbial romantic man of forty suffering from
springicitis amoritis. Also, he was not impro-
bably a lunatic of no age in particular. There
are two or three polite lunatics knocking around
Melbourne just now, one of them being an alleged
Saviour, who wants to advertise his advent in the
dailies at 6s. per inch.
• • • •
“ G.H. ” ; “A man never knows what a
woman can do until she cries” (Bulletin, Aug. 20).
Doesn’t he? I remember a case where he
knew right enough. Smith (the man) had
lost his wife per elopement with a strange
gentleinan*who occupied a front pew in a
suburban church ; and Smith stood with
a stony jaw on the steps of the Divorce
Court, waiting till his case should be
shrieked for. The lost wife, a super-
abundant female published in a bright
scarlet binding, stood there too, and
publicly besought Smith to forgive her
and takeherhome. (N.B.— The strange
religious gentleman, having tired of
her, had abandoned her at Manly with
seven pence in coppers.) The more the
lady besought and flung her arms
round Smith’s neck the rockier Smith’s
countenance became. Finally, she
tried the last she-resource—tears, gal-
lons of them. Smith wiped her and
the moisture from his shirt-front, and
laughed—a hard, bitter laugh that
sounded like bad sandwiches. ‘‘Cry
and be hanged !” he said. “Twice I
was soft enough to let you howl your
way back. Once you wept in a blue
dress and a big hat with a red feather
in it, and next time you wept in a green
frock and a big hat with a white feather
in it. Now you can weep in these red
clothes till the court’s bogged if you like, but
I’m full up.”

THE SYMPATHISER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
During a recent Victorian newspaper controversy on
Old Age Pensions, a contributor, himself a highly-salaried
State official entitled to a pension, stated that he “ yielded
to none in tenderness and sympathy for the aged poor,”
but he violently objected to Old Age Pensions, all the
same.
“My heart is sore,” he said,
“ For all the agbd crones—
Who really should be dead ;
I grieve to hear their moans.
*' I yield to none in my
Anxiety to give
The poor who soon must die
A blessing while they live.
“ Pve prayers and tracts for those
Who totter down the street—
For prayers are more than clothes,
And tracts are more than meat.
“Some folks would money give—
But ah t the sinful waste ;
Like water through a sieve
It vanishes in haste.
“ For poor and agfed, then,
I sigh as all men should :
’Tis verra cheap, ye ken—
’Tis verra cheap and good.
'* On this I’ve built my plan
On this immortal fact:
However wasteful man,
He will not drink a tract.”

A Western telegram to the Sydney
papers:—
A solicitor was to-day suspended for six months
for having failed to properly account for money
held by him for a client. It was found that the
client was in a starving condition, and sick in the hos-
pital, and though he implored the solicitor for the money
he could not get it.
A solicitor gets six months’ holiday for not
accounting for the money of a sick person in a
hospital. And the clerk who doesn’t account for
the money of a healthy solicitor (healthy except
for over-feeding) in a club would easily get six
years’ gaol.

CURED ME OF A TEN YEARS’ COUGH.”*

Troubles.
“Insulting behavior” is such a large,
comprehensive term that it has even served as an
excuse for running in a woman of doubtful sanity
who entered Melbourne riding on the buffer of a
railway carriage. She had travelled 31 miles that
way. Presumably her conduct was held to be
derogatory to the locomotive, for it isn’t recorded
that she was otherwise than perfectly respectful
to everybody she met.
* * * *
Sydney magistrate Donaldson, who sent a
boy of good character to gaol for one month for
tossing pennies in the street, is the gentleman
who recently broke out on the barmaid question,
contending that a barmaid had less claim than
other women to protection from insult because
“sheknew what she was doing when she accepted
that occupation”—just as a policeman should have
no remedy for assault because he knew when he
took up his line of business that he must needs
arouse the animosity of bad characters.
THE RIGHT SIDE OF THE BARS.
CHIEF WARDER : “ What are you doing there?”
PAT: “Nothing, sir.”
CHIEF WARDER : “ Well, what are you looking
in here for? ”
PAT: “ Shure it’s better to be looking in than
looking out.”
FRENCH LEAVE.
MRS. THIRD WEDNESDAY: “Are you going to Mrs. Second Tuesday's funeral?”
MR. T. W.: “ Well, I did think of it.”
MRS. T. W.: “Do you know that the horrid woman never returned my last call ?”
Some old Sydney buildings are on the
market—those known as Wilshire Place, Brick-
field Hill. As far back as 1807 the original Wil-
shire (James) had a fine lease “on the road to the
brickfields,” the same now offered for sale. There
were two leases to the north of him and but one
to the south. East and west was bush, and
George-street was a cart track. Mr. Wilshire was
a tanner, his tannery being at the rear of the
buildings now about to be sold. He was the
second Mayor of Sydney, became a member of
the Legislative Council, and had a splendid monu-
ment over his remains in tho old Sandhills ceme-
tery. Not many Australian families retain a
century’s hold on their estates.

“ Ilex ” ; I have had the misfortune to
attend a Melbourne funeral. As the
dismal procession started, I
noticed an ex-
tremely seedy in-
dividual start
simultaneously in
the same direc-

tery was three

yet whenever I
looked out of the

was the w e t,.
shabby individual
keeping well
abreast of us.
The • puzzle was
solved when he
was observed at
the cemetery dis-
puting with a
dozen others their
claims to the
privilege of
“holding a
horse.” Whether
he obtained the
job, and, if so,
whether any re-
muneration was
forthcoming, I
know not; but on

the first pub. with
a dejected air, so
I concluded that
the local “com-

strong for the

* *
“Kati Kati” on
a large subject:
Some people seem
much concerned about
widows remarrying. Why
shouldn’t they? Because it
prevents the marriage of some
spinster? Not a bit of it. The
more marrying there is the more
there will be. Besides, the man who
marries a widow doesn’t want a wife to
love and cherish ; he wants one who will
love and cherish him and be a mother
to him. It is good for this sort of man to
meet a decent widow, and it is good for the widow.
She has acquired the habit of looking after a man, and
if she cannot get another she will have to get a parrot.
Surely no thinking woman, be she ever so unmarried,
would condemn another woman to keep a parrot.
* * * *
In a cavity of the masonry at the new
tower of the Central Railway Station was placed
a glass jar sealed, containing coins, documents and
newspapers of the day. “Which,” said Mr.
Owe’Sullivan, “will show our descendants 50
years hence what we are doing to-day.” Appa-
rently the Owe’S. expects his immense station to
be rooted up in 1953.
• * * *
The N.S.W. Supreme Court, by the mouth
of Justice Pring, has decided that pennies are not
instruments of gambling under the local Acts, yet
a Sydney stipendiary sent two lads to gaol for one
month each last week for gambling with pennies.
The Attorney-General released both lads, and
Carruthers has not yet tabled a motion of censure.
The following is an extract from a report
of the W.C.T.17. Conference recently held in
Adelaide: —
Committees on such topics as heredity and hygiene,
peace and arbitration, agricultural shows, purity, mercy,
and opium, and doing work among cabmen, sailors,
miners, shearers, household helpers, and numerous other
sections of the community, are connected with the insti-
tution, and are booked on the agenda for the presentation
of reports. The scope of the union is as comprehensive
as life itself. The principal influences at work are per-
sonal example and the dissemination of temperance
literature.
The habit of tackling too many things at once is
here manifest. There are too many committees.
When Mrs. Brown, living a quiet, retired life in
Adelaide, finds herself on the committee for the
reformation of shearers, and also on the one for
urging the Great Powers of Europe to arbitrate
instead of going to war, and for imploring the
Venezuelans not to revolushe, there is always a
chance that some insufficiently-reformed shearer
will cus3 while Mrs. Brown is busy elsewhere.

Vic. Education Minister Sachse showed
his fool mind conspicuously the other day when he
remarked to a deputation :
For the teaching of babbling infants it did not seem to
him that any very high qualifications were necessary.
Up to eight years of age practically no qualification
should be required.
This is dead against all modern educational ideas,
and against common-sense too. When plants are
young they demand most care.

* * * *
N.S.W. Assembly should promptly quash
the proposal to lease Chief-Justice Darley’s
Katoomba house, Lilianfels, as a summer resi-
dence for the Governor-General at £OOO a year.
There is no reason at all why N.S.W. should
provide the G.-G. with a summer house; it
already maintains for him a mansion which is
utilised barely one month out of six. Further,
£6OO a year is far too high a price for Lilianfels,
which, it is rumored, has been under offer for a
sum which wouldn’t bring half of that in interest.
* * * *
A select committee of N.S.W. Anglican
Synod states, in effect, that the reason why the
Australian youth so rarely applies for holy orders
is that clerical pay is small, and no sufficient pro-
vision is made for old age and infirmity. In a
word, “No rum, no hallelujah.” Besides, the
bishoprics all go to imported men, except when
there is a bishopric of the damper-and-gunyah
variety on offer.
