The Presbyterian General Assembly Com-
mittee (Melbourne) has formed an idea of the kind
that is peculiar to such gatherings of the godly.
After thanking a Castlemaine parson for having
protested against the introduction of Labor
politics into a Sawbath discourse, the P.G.A.C.
“instructed its executive to consider whether
steps could be taken to prevent meetings of the
same character being held on Sundays.” Why
not petition Irvine and Bent to pass a Bill for the
Suppression of Socialistic Sermons on Sunday ?
A largely increased police force, armed with
revolvers, might carry the measure into effect by
shadowing all persons suspected of the intention
to preach Democracy on the Lord’s Day, and
shooting them at the first mention of the Factories
Act.
There are people in the world who are
never happy unless they’re converting somebody.
Some time ago certain enthusiasts in Melbourne
laid themselves out to bring the Jews round to
Christianity. A recent meeting, however, con-
vened by advertisement, inviting “Christians of
the Jewish race” to attend did not show en-
couraging results. The only persons present were
the two gentlemen who convened the meeting.
* * * *
N.S.W. Anglican Church had a Synod,
the other day in Sydney, and a religious paper
publishes with some pride a list of the occupations
of the lay members :
Eighteen solicitors, thirteen merchants, twelve graziers
and farmers, sixteen civil servants, one soldier, ten clerks
in Holy Orders, three journalists, seven masters of
schools, five barristers-at-law, nine accountants, seven
clerks, five doctors, three builders, one Senator, two
printers, two architects, one District Court Judge, three
tradesmen, one M.L.0., two custom-house agents, two
bank managers, two commercial travellers, three com-
mission agents, four storekeepers, three warehousemen,
four auctioneers, two orchardists, two estate agents, and
one each of various other professions, whilst twenty-five
persons are styled “ gentlemen.”
Not a carpenter or a fisherman or a laborer of any
kind ! It goes ill with a church which cannot
muster to its councils one of the horny-handed, in
a country, too, where the average intelligence of
the horny-handed is so high.
• * * *
The Horsham (Vic.) magistrates have
severely admonished and imprisoned for four
hours a lad of 13 who stole a postal note. He was
employed by a mail contractor, and had to ride 60
miles every day with the mails. The rest of hi 3
time he did odd jobs about his employer’s house.
And the wages were five shillings a week and
“found”—perhaps “lost” would be a better
word.
* * » *
There was begun in Sydney, t’other day,
what is termed in legal parlance “a court of
inquisition,” before Edward Scholes, barrister,
and a jury of 14. This is the first court of its kind
held in N.S.W. for over half a century. It was
convened to test the question of the legitimacy of
one Matilda Clines, deceased, to determine
whether or not certain property should be confis-
cated to the Crown or pass to the deceased’3
relatives. Holman, Labor M.L.A., made his first
appearance as a barrister in the case.
• * * *
Equity Judge Simpson decided in the case of
Blanche Tyson, in Sydney last week, that a woman
who obtains a divorce with alimony must continue
to receive alimony during her life out of her hus-
band’s estate even after the death of the husband.
“Till death us do part ” has been relied on by
many lawyers as the principle governing such
cases.
EVENING (UN) DRESS.
“I hear Miss Orlrite ‘came out ’ at the ball, the
other night.”
“I can quite believe it; I left early, and there
was a good deal of her out then.”
The other day an ancient person, who
described himself as a cabman, applied for
admittance to a Sydney hospital as a destitute
patient. He was in a dirty condition, but when
the attendants were about to prepare him for a
bath he objected vigorously. In undressing him
£834 in notes, cash, etc., was found ! How is it
that you rarely discover an unexpected rich man
who is not badly in need of a wash ? Perhaps tho
average miser’s wealth is simply the money ha
didn’t spend on soap.
A WET NIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A mother with her brood of three,
Her bundles and a gamp,
Stands waiting—ah Iso patiently—
Beneath a yellow lamp.
Near by a boy is crying Post
To those that pass him—see,
He scorns the rain, but makes the most
Of opportunity.
A crowded car with sodden roof
Crawls slowly up the track.
The driver in his waterproof,
A dripping blot of black.
The great Post Office clock looks down
On all the life below.
And hour by hour he tells the town
That even Time must go.
The gutter’s flooded, lamps are lit,
Tired feet no longer roam ;
The blind is drawn, and under it
I see the light of home.
M.L.
W. FRANCIS CHAMBERS.
THE SLEEPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He is not dead, though o’er his head
The netting ivy creepeth ;
Though upstart weeds blot out his deeds,
He is not dead, but sleepeth.
Ay 1 though he lies where mortal eyes
See naught but gloom and sorrow,
At Death’s strong gates, he only waits
The dawning of To-morroiv I
Vic.

THE VARNISHED BOARD*’
A WOMAN’S LETTER,
(Sydney, September 29, 1903.
Aly Dear Arini,—
• Crossley’s song season, opening last Thursday
in Sydney, has sped auspiciously so far; a full
-Town Hall and a full, glad welcome met the
singer on her return after nine years. Perhaps
the platform was purposely left unadorned in all
itscoldj stiff vvooden plainness that it might prove
the greater foil to the very decorative contralto’s
presence—a, long, slender, pale-blue presence
crowned with pale green leaves, a spring-like
picture. _ She stood smiling and bowing towards
three points of the compass with a deprecating,
so-good-of-you ” air, not usually associated with
ji star performer. After the audience had assured
if r enthusiastic hands that it remembered
she had been raised in Australia and that her
return in . the noon day of her splendor
was appreciated, Miss Crossley lifted up her
voice in the Italian aria “Caro mio ben”
—otherwise, “My Dearest One,” a century-
and-a-half-old melody with which we are
all fairly familiar, but not as Miss Crossley
sings it. It is a sweet, pleading, gentle entreaty
to some .beloved, heart to cease its cruelty. She
gave it its requisite measure of tears and tender
sentiment, but it did not give her pure, rich voice
the chance that it got later in “O that We Two
were Maying!” At the second concert her
singing of “When the Birds Go North' Again ! ”
mid (for a recall) “Rocked in the Cradle of the
Deep ” showed why she is so successful in
oratorio. I would like to hear her in “ Elijah.”
There is a reverential, vibrant quality in her
voice, that peculiarly fits it for the expres-
sion of the emotions of faith and hope and high
resolve.. Ada sings as if she said her prayers
every night, and never scamped them, no matter
how late the recalls kept her out of bed, and ais if
she thought in a reverential way. Everyone was
wishing that the programme held more songs set
in a majestic key. At the same time, hers is a
radiant voice, as was proved by her “ Sunshine and
Rain,” and Purcell’s “ Hark, the Echoing Air.”
No matter how sad her theme she never gives you
the dumps. '
. After her first number the platform' was a more
fitting background for the contralto. A proces-
sion of flower-laden ushers paced up, deposited the
offerings and returned for more—again and yet
again more. They trod the daffodil and carnation
path so often that benevolent Benno Scherek,
who helped to gather in the harvest of flowers and
stook them, was submerged in floral stars, baskets
and posies. One long gilt crook with a little
bunch of forget-me-nots and a tiny, worn, infant’s
shoe attracted the singer’s special attention.
At her second concert, Miss Crossley appeared
in a picturesquely crippled condition—a walking-
stick in one hand and Benno Scherek for a crutch
on the other side. One foot was in plaster-of-
Paris and a red woollen slipper. Skipping on the
morning of that day in a too large shoe, her ankle
turned and the ligaments were injured. Although
suffering somewhat Miss Crossley sang six songs
at a standing in the second part of the programme
and the loud applause that followed was meant
more as a tribute to her pluck and amiability
than as a demand for an encore number. .
The Crossley gowning runs to a long, straight,
clinging style. At her first concert she wore a
palo blue gauze silk, under an overdress of
glittering net, embroidered round the foot
and on the corsage with big sunflowers in
blue chenille with silver centres. On her second ap-
pearance she was all white and silver, and the skirt
of her govvn was heavily embroidered with raised
leaves and flowers. The third concert “creation”
was amber satin and cream lace. She does not jewel
much. A diamond chain and the Victorian order
usually adorn her. The last, presented by the late
Oueen, 13 fashioned of red enamel and diamonds
surmounted by the Imperial crown. It is worn
on a piece of purple ribbon, and is liable to be
given to a lady who manicures the King’s hands
as well as to the lady with one of the finest con-
tralto voices in the world. So, if I were Miss
Crossley, I wouldn’t be extra proud of it.
By way of a side light on the joys of being a
great singer—on one recent morning Miss Crossley
received 150 demands for signed photographs, and
this is not more than a fair day’s average. About
149 of the 150 pirates who boarded her in this
ruthless manner were complete strangers, and
they expected the singer to supply the photo-
graphs as well as the signatures j also, in many
cases, to stand the postage and pack the parcel
carefully, and address it legibly to Dingo Vale.
1 he distracted lady, who wants a little vest, states
that she can’t meet these demands*
Mrs. Mayoress Hughes (who of all our society
women shows the greatest ability to sit in a Big
Draught all through a Town Hall show without
turning blue), attended all the Crossley concerts.
On baturday night she was accompanied by the
violet-clad Monsignor Vay de Vaya; and on
Monday night, the most fashionable and crowded
of the series, the Bartons shared the Big Draught.
The enterprising American girls ! One oflier
has become, it is written, Empress of Corea. She
is the daughter of a Presbyterian missioner, and
was already in the harem under the name of
‘Om.” Her own name is Emily Brown. No
doubt the harem, with its dancing girls and sweet-
meats, was preferable to a Presbyterian home
where papa preached plain, flat-footed Hell!
Presently Corea s emperor may be seen dressed in
lifth Avenue clothes; with a bilk of exchange in
one pocket and treatises on grain elevators beef-
trusts, and hog-factories in another, and playing
baseball, or motoring. Australian girls don
seem to have any luck (although some of them
marry Chinamen). Their colored man seems to
be always of the penny plain variety. In England
there are 30 American women, holders of titles
acquired by marriage, and Germany has six
princesses and 19 countesses who are American
born. Italy, France and Russia have a good
snare. These marriages are a good advt. for
U.S.A. ; a proof of the bigness of the dowry
Uncle Sam can give his daughters, in exchange
for a title.
A friend of mine and his wife, who were house-
hunting recently in Hobart, were informed by a
female property owner with a long upper lip that,
after 24 hours’ deliberation, she was sorry to dis-
appoint the lady, but that she could not possibly
let her have the house because her husband looked
like an infidel. If you wear the restless expres-
sion of one who doesn’t believe in the Mercury
and Hobart’s sparkling Rivulet, then you are set
down as an infidel, and the Commonwealth’s small
kitten will have none of you.
Two ancient Sydney families were joined in the
“holy bonds ’’ last week, when Dr. P. Sydney
Jones, third son of Dr. P. Sydney Jones, of Strath-
field, and grandson of Mr. David Jones, M.L.C.,
was united to Miss Constance Myles, of Dingadee,
Burwood. The bride is granddaughter of Mr.
Myles, an old-time Commissioner of Police and
builder of Myles’ Buildings on the “ Robks,” the
big weather-beaten terrace on the sky-line.
A charwoman under regular engagement for
scrubbing purposes at a fashionable dancing
school in-Sydney, went last week as usual to fulfil
her duties. On arrival, however, she was informed
that her services were no longer required, the
scrubbing job having been given to a lady who
contracted to take payment in dancing lessons.
A Truck Act ought to be wide enough to get at
the academy which persuades its charwoman to
take out her wages in airy gambols on a large,
well-worn toe.
Jacques Jacobs, violinist of the Ada Crossleyco.,
is a short, broad, curly young man who does not
look mangel-wurzel fed. A man playing a fiddle
is always an unsightly object, and Yacks Yakobs
has even less of a pink lily aspect at the work
than other violinists I have seen. At the first
Crossley concert he got a flattering hearing,
and a laurel wreath, which he accepted as
if it had been a laundry bill. Local violin-
ists are saying that he has wonderful ex-
ecution and tone and technique—in fact,
that he has a lot of wonderful attributes, in-
cluding “soul.” “Soul” is a very indefinite
thing ; no two people agree as to what it is, and
those who say they know what it is have great
difficulty in describing it, and some influential
ironmongers have doubts as to whether there is
any such thing; but whatever it is, and assuming
there is such a thing, some people are quite sure
Jacobs has it.
A fashionable and political gathering helped to
make history at the laying of a corner-stone of
the new Sydney railway station last Satur-
day. Wet flags, an ocean of wet umbrellas,
dingy, bad-weather clothes, and mud, mud,
mild. Premier See spoke under an umbrella
which was held over him with tender care by a
politician who is doing his best to put the See
Government out. Mrs. O’Sullivan held a rain-
shade over her own .especial Minister, and her
daughters stood on a lower platform and made dis-
respectful remarks about foundation stones. Mr.
O’S. quoted some stirring lines from the
poet Campbell, who prophesied the building of
such structures as this railway station in these
parts, but who omitted to say if the Owed
Folks. at Home would supply the money. It was a
beautifully-ordered function, and no one ever saw
a man with vine-leaves in his hair so gracefully
and lightningly removed as was an offender who
interjected whilst the Premier was speaking.
Two big men in blue elasped him on either side,
and the shamrock shot forward as if he had been
dynamited horizontally.
Quite interesting to look over the names in the
Queen s House Music Society (started and con-
ducted by Percy Grainger, in London) and note
the. Australians. Mrs. Conway Gordon, the
beauty of late Richard Speight’s family, appears
among the contraltos. She lives with her aristo-
cratic husband’s family—for the husband himself
did-n t turn out quite an archangel. Mrs. Zebina
Lane is another of the contraltos, and amongst
the baritones are Rupert Bunny, the artist who
painted Melba’s portrait, and Henslow Orchard,
who conducts one of the Sydney musical societies.
Queens House is where the Rossettis lived—in
Clieyne Walk, Chelsea. It is now occupied by

Have you seen the tissue-paper hats ? Our girl
friends are making and wearing them on one and
the same morning—white, pink, or blue hats made
up of tissue-paper plaited, and wired. Their one
fault is that they are rather heavy. But they look
like chip straw.
Stated on good authority that a woman and two
children are regularly employed by N.S.W. tram-
way autocrats to traverse the different routes. The
woman generally tenders incorrect fares for the
children, and if they are accepted a report of the
occurrence goes into the Department, and. the
guard is called on for an “ explanation.”
The Royal. Sydney Golf Club is embarking on
an ambitious scheme to acquire some square acres
or land between Bondi and Rose Bay. At present
the club ha,s temporary possession of the paddocks,
and it realises that its occupancy will soon be
challenged by the spread of settlement. The new
tram extension has changed the howling waste of
a. few years back to the “ very desirable subdivi-
sjori of the real estate auction man. Already
the lives of the stray rooster and wandering
gander are in danger from the flying golf ball,
while the increasing holiday traffic along the roads
which run through the links is a positive nuisance
to the Royal golfer. So it is proposed to
boldly buy up the territory at a cost of £20,000.
It is the “thing” to belong to the “Royal
j-yoneyS’ _ and scores of persons who grudge
na i? n °^ er directions, cheerfully pay
be members, though they don’t know &
golf bunker from a coal bunker, and could not play
over the links to save their lives, and wouldn’t
know a golf ball if they saw three of it hanging
above a pawnbroker’s shop. The committee thinks
xt will find no difficulty in placing 20,000 £1 shares
among those anxious to be part of the swell con-
cern, and already one or two have asked for £lOOO
worth each.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent: —
“The Austral Salon has gained some public
interest through the recent demonstration of
handicrafts. In turn, the Commonwealth Exes.,
the State Exes., and the journeymen Exes, did a
patrol of the exhibition, the result being that the
crowd rolled up and the show ‘went.’ Once it
was believed that the Salon was the Mecca of
matrons too heavy for light amusements, but not
stodgy enough to remain at home and label
the jam, or darn worsted hose. But the recent
outbreak suggests that most Salonites can draw or
daub or point-lace, or invent a hen-coop, or
embroider a novel crib, or show a talent of one
kind or another.
“Mark Hambourg, so the story goes, loves the
Sydney girl more fondly than , her Melbourne
sister, whom he accuses of ‘ coldness.’ It chanced
while he was here that he went to a picnic in
company with a goodly band of maidens and
others. The day was sultry, .and the pleasure-
seekers were only halfway up the gully when they
.heard the babble of a mountain brooklet amid the
fern. The lion of the day flung himse.lf on the
ground, exclaiming, thirstily, ‘Now, who would
like to bring me a cup of water from the stream ? ’
No one answered, so, after a surprised pause,
Mark, struggling into a sitting position, announced
severely, ‘ Melbourne girls are slow in comparison
with those of Sydney. Now, had I begged a cup
of cold uiater from them, in one instant the ladies
of Sydney, fleeing to bring it, would have vanished
away—away —away ! ’
“At Mark’s Sydney hotel lie was wont, when
so disposed, to receive visitors, sunk back on a
couch, his eyes closed and his mind sealed, seem-
ingly. to all but thoughts of his music. Here, the
average caller was less complaisant, or ‘less sym-
pathetique—less intuitive.’ Did Mark button his
eyelids during a chat, Mrs. or Miss Melbourne
would not deliver her message in hushed tones
and steal away, leaving the dreamy seer to his
vision. Rather, she waited till he appeared to
wake, and then went on talking in businesslike
tones. If he drowsed a second time, she rose and
swept off, with an offended, tread of some size. ,
“Men, in general, credit women with petty
spite and paltriness. Sometimes ‘ large-minded-
ness ’ is as little at home Under waistcoats as
beneath frock-bodices. Observe the case of that
asylum superintendent, recently mulcted in
damages for alleged slander against his ex-
colleague (formerly matron of the same institu-
tion). Good friends to begin with, their mutual
esteem failed to stand the strain of official wear
and tear. Among other absurdities, it came out
in court that defendant had directed the hall-
porter to compile notes concerning all the
matron’s visitors and their doings. These Paul
Pry notes provided a merry 15 minutes for court
loungers. The menial didn’t know the names of
the visitors, so he described them by their hair
and the way their clothes dragged behind them.
“The encroachments of a fashionable foreign
costumiere upon the habitations of a little artistic
circle in Melbourne affords an interesting com-
mentary on the relative positions of mere Art and
Drapery. The lady started in a very small way
in one corner of a Collins-street building, the
remainder of which was devoted to artist’s studios,
Longstaff and others being located there. With
the rapid expansion of the costume business one
after another of the artists received notice to quit.
There are now only two left, and one of these,
sculptor Summers, will be leaving in a few weeks
in order to let the foreign lady put in thirty or
forty more girls to sew Cup costumes together.
The remaining man is away up at the top of the
building, and Madame is putting off to the very
last the necessity for climbing several flights of
stairs. But the last of the artistic fold can read
his doom through the skylight.
“Ada Crossley is to sell at the Australian
Church bazaar almost as soon as she arrives. She
always said that Dr. Strong’s church would be one
of the first places to receive her home-coming
homage.
“Fritz Mueller’s last appearance hereabouts,
before he makes for Vienna, drew a big audience
to Kew Recreation Hall. It was an interesting
little entertainment, because, besides gifted Fritz
himself, the programme included work by several
enthusiastic young musicians, students at Mar-
shall-Hall’s conservatorium. Fritz’s platform
demeanor is refreshingly boyish.
“The knee exercise of one or two sorrowing
Magdalenes in ‘ Resurrection ’ makes the truly
religious and un-Christian alike squirm. The
sackcloth of the true penitent is never visible, and
her tears are mostly spilt in secret. A recovered
soul praying before a Greek Church image
in the midst of the harridans of the prison
yard is a ludicrous incongruity. The. thing
is farcical. Likewise the final tableau where
the repentant sinners, Katusha and Dimitry,
.pose in their purification—the former on abject
knees before the crucifix, the latter with a
princely .hand halting in benediction on the
Magdalene’s head—is one that sends many away
smiling. A more natural figure of woe is the
tall, vacant-eyed woman who, with streaming
hair and hands locked overhead, moans in-
coherently for the child she has slaughtered.
“There are two military swells'in the city who
hold similar rank and surnames. It is awkward
—and also convenient. One is a bachelor, and
t’other a widower. Both are big figures in the
circle of Frill. Both are favorites. Every week
the wicked gossips engage one o» t’other to some
widowed matron or some fashionable maid. When
friends congratulate them, as friends do, .'half,
maliciously, the defence of each is : “ Oh, it’s the
other fellow ! ”
“A dear, fussy, old dame, who often exploited
the sentiment that money was the special prero-
gative of the vulgar, has just been bequeathed a
fine fat sum by a clerical relative. A wide circle'
of friends wonder how this wealth will be diffused.
Will the dear old soul find it incompatible with
her fixed ideas to take possession of the vulgar
hoard ?
The latest avenue for the female worker is a
scheme for turning out a dinner spree on an elastic
6 P r ' ce3 >. with promptness and despatch,
s u , s *'. ess rings up the feast-maker on the
phone, gives information relating to the prospec-

“ The order of Carlos 111., a Spanish decoration
bestowed upon Consul Henry Cave, adds a gew-
gaw to the gold-braided coat of that impressive-
looking person and sheds reflected glory on his
smart wife. The lady does much to make her
Armadale home more than a tea-and-twaddle
centre for her friends. It is an assembling place
for the soulful who struggle with German litera-
ture and its words of 362 letters. Music, mediaeval
armor, aboriginal tools of defence and curios,
are other features of the Cave house.

“ By the death of Mrs. J. M. Templeton the
poor lose a staunch friend, and the Baptist flock
one of the most shining of good examples.
“Touring Australians, visiting New York, are
impressed by the ‘American smile,’ with its
revelations of up-to-date dentistry. A diamond,
twinkling in a front tooth, is quite common ‘over
thar.’ Much newer are the colored artificial
teeth, displayed by Yank beauty-actresses. At a
hotel favored by theatricals, a simple stranger
from far Geelong watched a blonde belle
coquettishly contrast her blue-enamel biters with
snowy meringues, while at another table a bru-
nette charmer let observers see for themselves how
‘cunningly’ her dental-furniture of pink coral
suited the simultaneous flash of her gypsy eyes.
“A certain smart set is disappointed that
Representative Isaacs was, after all, left out of
the Federal judiciary. Not that it matters to
the set how well or how badly he would exercise
the powers of judgeship, but he is the husband of
a charming woman. Mrs. Isaacs is showy and
extremely clever. She is a linguist, a conver-
sationalist, a decided poseuse of a picturesque
order, an occasionally decorative frocker,' and
almost a wit. More than that, she is popular.
So that a large section of Society regrets that her
busy and ambitious spouse failed to be Benched.
‘ ‘ So far, Railway Commissioner Tait has loomed
largest as a Society figure. He is a front-ranker
to an obstinate degree. It’s neck or nothing. He
pilots his wife carefully, bestows her on the
smartest coterie in sight, and then himself sets
about falling into step with those males worth
while. As a railway reformer he is yet to arrive.
Up in his Spencer-street department there is a,
vague belief in him, and they say, ‘ Wait a while-
and he’ll get right there. ’ So far, he’s only got:,
right there in Society ranks.

tow through the tube department, trotted out the-

“The girl-golfer is sticking to her favorite:
sweet frilled hat, but the hardier dame shows a.
tendency to the cork helmet. This is a replica of
the style once associated in our minds with Indian
colonels, but popularised a little by middle-aged
family men during the last two or three hot
seasons. I saw some helmets modelled for female
heads at a Collins-street store, and hear there’s a
demand for them because of lightness, shade, and
comfort—by the golfers of. the downright sort.
Those who wear sweaters on damp match-days,
and leggings, are, likely enough, ready to don the
white helmet, no matter what severity and for-
midable lines it gives the countenance. The true
she-sport says, ‘ Hang appearances.’
“In furniture one notes symptoms of a re-
action against the present Arcadian or Watteau
style, with its flowery chintzes, all shepherdesses,
wreaths, and lovers’ knots. Dresden china colors
and curtains and cushion slips of fresh white
muslin will soon be back numbers. So much the
worse for. the owners of small rooms ! The new
furniture fabrics show more decided colors, varie-
ties of rose, brown and gold, recalling the bygone
craze for ‘art shades.’ The Durbar, no doubt,
has whetted the world’s appetite for color feasts.
In design, the new chintzes seem akin to the con-
ventional patterns of ‘aesthetic’ times. People
fresh from England renovate their rooms with big,
handsome, plain curtains, and large, square
cushions showing mediaeval tulips, or daffodils, or
scrolls trained up against their groundwork like
saints on stained glass. Very effective and decora-
tive, methinks, when employed in spacious apart-
ments.
Yours affectionately,

SPRING FEELING.
You know it! The languor and the weariness—the loss of appetite —the touch of indigestion —the
irritation of the skin. They all come with Spring.
All these ills are banished by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They enrich the blood—brace up the nerves—
banish indigestion and chase all Spring weariness.
Just as new sap strengthens the trees to stand the Summer heat—so Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
strengthen men, women and children with rich, red blood.
They’re just the tonic you need for this Spring.
“ Last Spring-1 had that wearv, fagged-out feeling,” says Miss Alma Jones, 75 Albion-st., Surry Hills, Sydney. “ I grew
weak and listless, and just trailed my legs after me. Horrible sick headaches made me heavy and drowsy, and I felt more
dead than alive. I had to fSrce mvself to eat, for the very sight of food often turned me sick. Sometimes dizziness actually
blinded me, and I went into a lead faint. At last I got so bad that I had to give up business. Although I took common
tonics and doctors’ medicines until I hated the sight of a bottle, I got no better. Then Mrs. Goodhead told me how much
good Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People did her. She made me get some at Pattinson’s, and they started actually to
make new blood for me. Everv day I felt better. I got new life and energy from every dose. Six boxes made my blood so
pure and rich that now I never get tired. To-day I am a strong, healthy-looking girl, without a trace of that weary Spring
feeling.”

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Cardinal Moran attained his 73rd birthday
recently.
Printed that Captain Walls, of Sydney, is
arranging to build a yacht in England to contest
the America Cup next year.
Australia learns that Senator Drake is a
lawyer by his appointment as Attorney-General.
Australia would never have guessed it otherwise.
“ Toby ” no more ; Australia’s changed the cast,
Given him the part long years he’s set his heart on.
The lawyer’s Hamlet he’s to play at last—
“ Toby" no more, but “Mr. Justice Barton."
Seems that the late Monsignor O’Hea
(Melb.) was pretty well off as things go in these
latter days. His estate is estimated at about
£20,000.
Parson Whale, who died in Brisbane lately,
preached for many years in a big, square, stone
edifice up on Spring Hill, popularly known as
Whale’s Tank.
Mrs. Marion MTnnes, recently dead at
Geelong (Vic.), was reputedly aged 101 J years.
Her brother died at the age of 101 years and seven
months, in Nova Scotia, in Jan., 1901.
Barrister Colon na-Close works hard to
grow a party for next elections. A quarter of a
century ago Mr. Close was a parson in Brisbane,
and has boxed the compass of most opinions since.
Scorer, Victorian railway strike leader,
reckons his home will have to go. There is
nothing coming in for him. He has six children
—the eldest about 15. They are thinking of going
on the “land.”
John Forrest has now Occupied three
portfolios in the Federal Cabinet —Post-office
(before Dickson’s death). Defence and Home
Affairs. All three jobs would have got on about
as well without him.
Rutledge, Phil p’s Attorney-General, struck
a streak of bad luck. He had hardly time to put
his toga off when, owing to Griffith’s elevation,
there was a vacant seat on the Queensland
Supreme Court Bench.
Truly ex-Premier Philp, of Queensland, is
a man of widespread unpopularity. When the
news of his downfall reached Menzies (W.A.) old
Queenslanders there sang, “Praise God from
Whom all blessings flow.”

that preach the Gospel of Peace and
bring glad tidings of good things.”—

crew remained on Eagle Island until rescued by a gun brig
sent from Buenos Ayres. Late in 1813 Hayes reached his
home at Mount Vernon. He tried to regain his social
position but failed. He lived many years and was buried
in the crypt under Christ Church, Cork. Miss Pike, as
proved at Hayes’ trial, was detained only a few hours, her
friends having stormed Mount Vernon and rescued her.
Joey Bell, the spick-and-span Queensland
Minister for Lands, is the eldest son of Joshua
Peter Bell, one of the Queensland pure merinos
of the early sixties. Joey recently married a
daughter of Senator Ferguson.
“ I don't wish to cause any heartburning,
but there was not any Prime Minister I met in
the Mother Country who could fill his seat as well
as I did —and I am still growing.”—Seddon, com-
paring the Maorilander and the Englander.
F. H. Bruford, the new Victorian Auditor-
General, originally in the Customs Department,
was first brought to the front by J. B. Patterson
when Minister ot Customs. He used to write the
statistical portions of J.B.’s speeches for him.
A bookmaker in one of the Australian
capitals, who, some years ago, owned a house
worth at least £5OOO in a fashionable suburb and
ran his own horses, was lately fined for making a
penny book with newsboys in a city right-o’-way.
The depressed-looking Drake, who only
got into the Federal Cabinet by the untimely
death of his predecessor, has now held three
different portfolios—as P.M.G., Minister of
Defence, and Attorney-General, all within two
months.
Miss Vida Goldstein, the pretty little lady
who is about to seek election in Victoria to the
Federal Senate, confesses that she is 32 years old.
“You see,” she remarked to an Adelaide inter-
viewer with a touch of coquetry, “although
people in Melbourne think I am only 25, one has
to be 30 before one is qualified to sit in the
.Senate.” Miss Goldstein is only a dress reformer
to the extent of keeping her skirts clear of the
pavement, but is not' a sufficiently' advanced
woman to have a pocket in her dress yet.
Years ago, when the present Justice Dash-
wood was in Adelaide, Kingston had a business
appointment with him, which he failed to keep.
The irate Dashwood, after waiting for an hour
or so in the private room at Kingston’s
office, indignantly scattered all his Briefs,
papers, etc., over the table, turned his
room upside-down, and, boiling with out-
raged dignity, left a message that King-
ston would “find a memo, for him on
the table.” Kingston stored up the
episode in his mind. A time came
when he had an appointment at Dash-
wood’s office, which Dashwood failed ;
to keep. Kingston emptied Dash-
wood’s coal-scuttle on the table, heaped
the furniture on top of it, strewed the
contents of the waste-paper basket on
the floor, and left a message that Dash-
wood would find a complete explana-
tion on the table. Thereafter, those
two unpunctual men always kept ap-
pointments with each other.
A long-overworked and brainy
enthusiast, who is now a member of
the Australian Parliament, once sought
in the course of his desperate struggle
for a crust to supply a Sydney suburb
with cheap mullet—a whole cartload
of them. But meat was cheap then,
and the weather was hot —anyhow, at sun-
down the future member found himself
with a cartload of fish that simply had to
be disposed of—if possible, without attract-
ing public attention. The legend closes
with the pathetic picture of a tired father
driving his cart up and down the suburban
by-roads late into the night, his little son in
the back of the cart dropping stale mullet at
judicious intervals, pausing to rest the horse
and throw out much mullet when they came to a
vacant allotment, and whipping him past the
council chambers, where no mullet was disposed
of.
N. J. Brown, Speaker of Tas. House of
Assembly, who died Sept. 22, was a native of
Hobart, born in 1838, so he was almost old enough
to be a tradition. In July, 1891, he succeeded
Thos. Reibey as Speaker. Reibey is still alive,
aged 82 years.
John See, E. W. O’Sullivan, and Dan
O’Connor made quite a picture at the laying of
the corner-stone at the new Sydney railway-
station. It was refreshing to see Dan holding
an umbrella over John while the latter was read-
ing railway statistics.
A prominent Queensland politician will
probably “ go up King-street” very shortly unless
something unforeseen happens. The Jews and
Christians have been pecking at this unfor-
tunate for years, the result of too much confidence
in Mt. Morgans at £l6 10s.
Robert Ledger, a Benalla (Vic.) business
man, recently received a silver medal from the
Admiralty in recognition of services rendered
over 42 years ago as a sailor on H.M.S. Niger off
the M.L. coast. There is no unseemly haste or
flurry or jump about the Admiralty.
Edward Miles, master mariner, who got
the Braddon Ministry into such awful trouble
over his Strahan harbor contracts, and had to
drop out of Tasmanian politics, is announced as a
candidate for the Federal Senate. This is about
the most unkind thing that has yet been said
about the Senate.
Justice Barton has acted several times as
Supreme Court Judge on circuit in N.S.W. ; and
while doing that work his most striking character-
istics have been extreme deliberation and almost
painful slowness. But “the Bar” of country
towns always welcomed his appointment; even if
the session lasted only two or three days there
was “ a little dinner to the Bar ” at the end of it.
Chief Justice Griffith and Justice Barton
graduated M.’sA. at Sydney University in the
same year, 1870. Barton, however, went straight
through with his course, and took the academic
honor at 21; Griffith, though he was B.A. at 18,
let six years pass before he took the next step.
Griffith’s admission to the bar dates from 1807;
Barton’s from 1871. Justice R. E. O’Connor is
also a Sydney Uni-man.
Federal Justice O’Connor likes to keep
“in good nick,” and one of his pastimes is run-
ning. One morning recently he was doing a
sprint round Melbourne Parliament House grounds
minus hat and coat, when a new policeman on the
beat gave chase. O’Connor continued his pace,
but the “force’’ran him down and laid violent
paws upon him. Then a trembling brother of the
baton apologised, and was thanked for providing
a novel and exciting turn.
A certain new Southern high Parliamen-
tary official is the man who used to com-
pose his twaddlesome speeches as he lay abed
at night, and awaken his wife to listen
to the peroration. At about 2.30 a.m. she
would be called upon to express her drowsy
approval of his stereotyped rhodomontade,
beginning—“ And now, Mr. Speaker,” whilst the
orator sat up in his bed with one hand beating the
air and the other holding a glass of water from the
wash-stand bottle.

The Victorian House of Landlords has
elected old “Nick” Fitzgerald Chairman of Com-
mittees. Fitzgerald is a voluble Irishman, with
rheumy eyes and a wild, whirling style; at one
time he was a wealthy man. The Chair only carries
a salary of £4OO a year. As soon as the death of
Fred. Brown, the late chairman, was announced,
Fitzgerald’s friends •• Wised each other to the
effect that Nicholas wu*> • as well-to-do as
formerly. The chairmanship has been a sort of
refuge for the poorer members of the House for
many years past.
A Victorian railway inquiry board lately
acquitted one of the loco.-drivers—an ex-striker —
of the heinous charge of “refraining from con-
versing with his fireman. ” The driver in question
was James O’Connell, the plucky, ambitious
individual who put in a long succession of night
shifts on his engine that he might attend Univer-
sity lectures by day, and is now M.A. and B.Sc.
of Melbourne University. It should be a fair
thing now to haul up Bent before O’Connell on a
charge of “ refraining from conversing grammatic-
ally with anybody.”

Cowley, elected to the Speakership in
Bananaland Assembly, vice Morgan, the n«w

M.H.R. for Herbert (N.Q.) at coming Federal

Died at Ballarat, last week, nonagenarian
Dimsey (old Ballaratians are notorious for quaint
surnames), one of the originals in the .Band of
Hope G.M. Co., registered nearly 50 years ago.
Dimsey was the godfather of the mine, which
took its title from the Rechabite lodge he
happened to be ruling at the time. He was always
a staunch teetotaller and Methodist. Perhaps it
was because he ran a florist’s business that Dimsey
made a dying request for a very quiet funeral
with no flowers. As a dealer in comi>limentary
tributes to the dead he knew just how much they
were worth.
“ F.C.” : Evidently the strain of managing
a big company is severe. Clapp, boss-
director of the Melb. Tramway Co., smart Yankee
and one of the thinnest men who ever left a
land of lean men, looks very weary now. Of late
Jiis managership has been little more than nomi-
nal, just as is Sprigg’s secretaryship. The real
bossing and planning of the company is done by
a “plain brown man”—Horace A. Wilcox, who,
in the street, dresses like a storeman or caretaker.
A medium-sized, nondescript, unimportant-look-
ing individual, with reddish beard turning grey,
blue eyes and a slight stoop ; a godly man of the
Baptist persuasion.
Mr. John Bligh Suttor, cousin of F. 8.5.,
President of the Legislative Council, takes White-
ley’s place as N.S.W. Commercial Agent in the
Far North ; salary, £750 and liberal allowances.
In the preliminary sorting out of the candidates
Mr. Suttor’s claims were not supposed to amount
to much, yet as batch after batch was winnowed
he was found to survive. A whilom Sydney
commercial man, now an extensive speculator,
was favorite, but, owing to relationship, Premier
See vetoed him. Then came a commercial man
who had lived in China and Japan and talks a
number of languages. But at the last minute the
railway engineer—a very capable man in that line,
whose services to day labor even the Owe’Sullivan
hasn’t quite destroyed—slipped in.
It is a novelty to hear of a Minister of the
Crown throwing up his billet because of the
drudgery of signing his name to official docu-
ments. That seems to be the case with Lands
Minister W. P. Crick. It is true that in the
Lands Department the documents to be Ministeri-
ally initialled are exceptionally numerous. But it
should be possible to apply some mechanical
process to the work, instead of laboring over each
job with pen and ink. A few years ago visitors
to a N.S.W. Lands Minister were frequently
edified by the spectacle of an old gentleman
patiently and placidly signing document after
document, taken from a three-foot pile before
him. As he signed, about an inch of his tongue
protruded and followed the movements of the
pen. That Lands Minister delighted in the task.
He used to hurry over breakfast to get down to
the office table “to attach my signature.”

Died last week, Frederick Grew, the oldest
resident of West Maitland (N.S.W.), aged 96
years. He arrived at Sydney in 1830, and settled
at Wallis’s Plains (as West Maitland was then
known) in November of that year. The place was
named after Captain Wallis, of the 46th Ptegt.,
who was commandant at Newcastle in the ’teens.
Travelling by road from Sydney to Newcastle,
Mr. Crew said that he passed many gibbets, relics
of the convict system. The old gentleman told
weird tales of floggings and hangings, having a
clear recollection of seeing six men hanged for
“ sticking up ” a dray.

Inscription on a rough piece of wood which
purports to represent a tombstone out Arltunga
way :
Here lies the body of . If it doesn’t it ought
to, for being such a fool as to be taken in by a Dashed
Policeman.
The person whose name appears dug a grave
there, and buried a large stone to represent him-
self ; then read a funeral service, and fixed up the
inscription. He went to all this labor to relieve
his feelings because he took a policeman’s bad
cheque in payment for a horse. Seems a cir-
cuitous revenge, somehow.

“Ime Blode ” (who seems to be a fatally
attractive young man) writes:
That advt., by lady chastiser, alluded to in a recent
Bulletin, to wallop damsels 16 to 21, seems to fill a long-
felt want. My experience of ladies between those ages,
and especially round about 16, has convinced me that
judicious walloping and hard tucker are the things they
stand most in need of. Once had the misfortune to teach
a class of them, and, with absolutely no encouragement,
90 per cent, persisted in falling in love with me ; in hang-
ing around after lessons, and in occasional hysterical
outbursts if spoken to in a tone savoring of severity.
One, kept for a few minutes to explain her unladylike
deportment at an earlier hour, flung her arms round my
neck and sobbed out, “ You don’t understand.” My oath
I did, but I wasn’t taking anything in the way of hysterics
and got her out in double quick time. The presence in the
same city of a professional walloper, who might be called
in at any time to cane premature amativeness out of
them, would have saved me most of my hair.
“E.S.S.” : JRe carpet and rock snakes
(“Caber,” B. 17/9/’O3). H. J. M'Cooey gave the
following information in Bulletin of January 20,
1900:
Let me say that the generic distinctions between the
rock snake (Liasis childrenii, Krefft) and the carpet
snakes ( Morelia variegata, Gunther) are simply these : In
Liasis the lower labial shields are deeply pitted; in
Morelia the anterior upper labial shields are pitted. Also,
in M. variegata the entire dorsal surface is beautifully
variegated ; whereas L. childrenii is in general .color,
dark olive green. Again, M. variegata never attains a
greater length than 13ft., whereas L. childrenii may
attain to a length of 24ft. In re distinctions between the
carpet and the diamond snakes. There is in reality no
specific difference between M. variegata and M. spilotes.
They both work out the same and are anatomically
identical. M. spilotes is spotted all over; and appears to
non-experts to be a different and distinct species; but
mere .color-markings are absolutely worthless in the
determination of a species. In one word Morelia spilotes
is not a valid species. It is simply M. var. variegata.
Liasis childrenii is known as the Children’s Rock
Snake, and I don’t think there are many who
have seen a specimen over 12ft. —half of what
M‘Cooey says they attain to. According to Gun-
ther and others, the largest specimens of boas and
pythons seldom exceed 20ft. ; and Krefft adds :
“An Australian rock snake of 10 or lift, must be
considered a monster.’’ Also: “_A diamond or
carpet which measures above 10ft. in length may
be more than 20 years old. ” At that age they
have ceased to grow, though they may continue
to extend in girth. All the python id as are known
as rock snakes. Besides those mentioned, Queens-
land has the olive-green rock snake {(L. olivacea),
black-headed snake (8 or 9ft.), Gilbert’s rock snake
( Nardoa gilbertii), and one or two others. As to
distinction between carpet and diamond snakes,
it seems rather strange that the diamond is
found only along a limited strip of the east
coast of N.S.W. —not further north than the
Richmond River; while the carpet, snake
is found in almost every part of Australia, icith
the exception of the particular habitat of the diamond
snake. Krefft treats them as two distinct species,
and states that “there is reason to believe that
carpet snakes attain even larger growth than
diamond snakes.” According to the “Natural
History of Australia ” (which isn’t very reliable),
“the maximum recorded length of the carpet
snake (15 or 16 ft.) is considerably in advance of
that (lift.) of the other kind.” (N.B. —The snake
season commences in earnest in October.)

“ R.R,” : Some remarkable accidents. I
was cantering along, t’other day, when a.little
stick flew up and penetrated the horse’s side so
deeply that he died a few hours later. I once
saw a cow choked between the wall of a
house and a straining-post. She had evi-
dently, in an attempt to reach a few green
leaves alongside the building, slipped and
fallen as described. Riding past a
cocky’s place one day, my greyhound
“spotted” a pet kangaroo and gave
chase. The ’roo made for the pro-
tection of a “milker,” bumped
her over as my dog was closing
in, and all three fell in a heap.
The ’roo was killed by the impact,
and the dog was fatally impaled
on the cow’s horn. As I saw
Mrs. Cocky coming, I didn't wait
to see how the milker had fared.
Coffee-planter in Big Scrub,
overseering trench-digging by Hindu
gang, which, just then, was straining a
line: “Pull!” Nabob Khan (head
man) translates: “Kurtch!” “Pull—
pull hard ! ” N.K. : “Kurtch —kurtch-
ella ! ” P. : “Harder yet! Harder!”
N.K.: “Kurtch —Kurtchella ! Kurtch-
ella —Goorj-e-e-e ! ” But the day was
hot and the gang of a low grade, and
that planter’s soul leapt within him.
“ Pull, you blanketty dash asterisks.
Pull, you half-baked, dodgasted blanky
sons of niggerdom ! What were you
made for, you swarthy, sodden lumps of
blooming misery? Pull, you slummocky fag ends
of blanky creation ! ” Quoth Nabob Khan, in reply
to this lingual opulence: “I no understand how
to say that in my language.”

“Bon” announces the arrival in Mel-
bourne of one of the Owe’Sullivan’s oddities:
Having a political personage behind him he was
promptly pushed into a night-work billet in the Govern-
ment printing office. Arriving at work at 8 p.m., he was
supplied with a case and stick and told to lift “ copy."
“ ’Struth ! I aint a bloomin’ Sampson ; 'sides, where is
it?” “In the box, you idiot.” “Oh! thought yer
meant one of them South African joints.” Coiling his
legs round a stool he spent a full half-hour in gazing at
his case. Then approaching one of the comps, he asked,
“What game is that bloke in ther corner playin’?”
“ Dis-irig.” “Oh, that’s what they calls chuckin’ them
lead things into them funny little boxes.” Ten minutes
spent in emulation of the “ bloke in ther corner” pro-
duced a glorious state of “pye.” He next saw a couple
“ jeffing ” to settle the ownership of a piece of “ fat',”
and entered into the game with great zest. Nine o’clock
found him exhausted and inquiring what time knock-off
came. “Oh, about five.” “What! in the mornin’?”
“Yes.” “ ’Struth! this aint no place for me. I must
have me night’s rest. 1 Yer needn’t say nuffln’, but I’m a
butcher, and me night’s rest ” but just here he was
led out by a profane and perspiring printer, who returned
to his desk to pen a strongly-worded request to Authority,
asking if it knew the difference between a butcher and
a compositor, and if it didn’t would it kindly buy itself a
kangaroo to refer difficult questions to.

“ C.P.” : Some bush scientists do wonder-
fully good work considering their opportunities.
Near Alstonville, in the Big Scrub (N.S.W.),
lately, I dropped on H. R. Elvery, a self-taught
egg-collector. He has one of the best collections
of eggs in Australia, some of its features being
unique—notably pitters and rifle and regent birds’
eggs. His “clutches” of these are unsurpassed
in number.
* * * *
Once upon a time two Big Scrub cedar-
getters were on the spree in Ballina, and a travel-
ling jeweller induced them to buy a clock for the
camp, which boasted no timepiece of any sort.
Not till they got back did they discover that not
one of their dozen mates could tell the time. Nor
could the two clock-buyers.
“ C.H.R.” : Sketch in B. (9/7/’O3)
of lady and gentleman dining in economic
costume, reminds me somehow of a cleric
of my acquaintance who scared his family
and guests one morning by coming to
breakfast simply arrayed in his pants.
After a cheerful salutation all round, the
holy man took liis seat and asked a blessing
over the chops, while the guests nearly
carried the door off its hinges in their en-
deavors to escape, and Mrs. Parson fell
over the back of her chair in hysterics,
dragging over the breakfast service. The
groom and gardener wrapped their master
up in the tablecloth and carried him up-
stairs. Subsequently the rev. gentleman
appeared in private theatricals as a Spanish
nobleman. After the performance he went
home and went to bed with ’em all on, not
forgetting the tall boots and the spurs.
His wife thought the Russians had at-
tacked Tasmania and raised the street.
When the police broke in they found a
Spanish nobleman sitting in the shower-
bath, with a long feather drooping over one
eye, holding the Book of Jeremiah im-
paled on a dagger. He has since re-
covered, but Tasmania hasn’t.
* * * *
South Australia badly wants the old
age pension. Travel along any of the roads
or tracks of that distressful State and you
meet old men in twos and threes “ travel-
ling.” Yes, that’s the word, for they
themselves don’t know where they are tra-
velling to, simply looking for a doss and a
bit of tucker. Some of these poor old
fellows are of great age—over 70 —and
some can hardly toddle along. In the
travellers’ hut at Oollingrove station one
day recently there were three men each
over 66, two over 64, and one in his six-
tieth year. There is no possible hope of
these men getting work, but they keep
on moving because they might fall down
if they stopped. It is all utter misery, utter
hopelessness, utter waste of boots.

The story of Polly :
Fate decreed that Polly be reared “ on
the land” in the usual "settlement”
slab hut of two rooms, which, when
lighted at night, showed the family on-
goings through the chinks. Also, Polly’s
widowed mother and sister. Sis were
good-looking and sel-
fish ; she, herself,
being unselfish, cross
eyed, and uncom-
promisingly plain.
She was a working-
bee while her mother
and sister were of
the queen-bee varie-
ty. The two “boys”
of the family had
fallen into mischief
and on to the State,
so mother and Sis
did the best they
could on their
“ own," which was
not much, while
Polly was a long-
suffering “general”
in a family of 10.
Thus had Fate borne
a grudge against her
from the start.
Daniel Morton had
“ walked out ” with
Polly for a year or
two, and, at last,
June-twelve- months
was fixed for the
wedding. Now did
Polly, in her Bft.
square of servant’s-
room, dream dreams
of four-roomed sub-
urban cottages, in-
habited by a happy
Polly and Dan and—
baby, for babies
figured largely in
Polly’s dreams. Then
Fate tempted Polly
to take Dan to see
Mother and Sis.
Mother took to Dan
—so much so that,
ere that June-twelve-
months came round,
she became M rs.
Daniel Morton, and, when the occasion arose, Polly was
invited home to welcome a baby step-sister, which the
mother exhorted her to care for, and then died.
Nothing daunted was Polly, and while step-father Dan,
as he put it himself, “turned himself round” —which
process in his case took considerable time—she supported
the baby.
Likewise her heart was healed by the love of Thomas
Meehan. Five-feet-six of large-hearted Australian youth,
he also “ walked out” with Polly, who was getting rather
near thirty. But in an evil moment fate caused Sis to
fall in Polly’s way, and Polly, seeing how things were,
got a room and nursed her and her little stranger when
the hospital had done with them. This brought Sis into
the view of Tom.
Sis was a long time getting strong enough to go back
to work ; in fact she only got strong enough to take a
trip to town and come back Mrs. Tom Meehan. . But
Polly had made up her mind to outwit fate and claim at
least part of her dream ; so, shortly before Sis presented
Tom with a son, Polly presented him With a daughter.
Now askillionis added to the old slab hut, and Mr. and
Mrs. Tom Meehan and son, and Polly and Polly’s daughter,
with step-sister Morton, and Sis’s daughter, Gwendoline
Jones, live together in a happy, casual Australian fashion,
daily awaiting the return of step-father Dan, who has at
last “ turned himself round,” and is coming home from
the West to go back “ on the land.’
CHIEF JUSTICE GRIFFITH,
The first chief of the High Court of Australia.
Photo, by J. Hubert Newman, Sydney.
Near the end of last year two miners
sinking a shaft on White Cliffs opal field found a
colony of large black ants in the heart of a solid
rock, about 30ft. from the surface. The insects
died soon after exposure to the air. There was no
passage or fissure connecting the cell with the
outer world. The ants and a portion of their
prison walls have been sent to Adelaide Museum.
ON THE TRACK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I grope in my soul’s dim places,
In its chambers hung with black,
And peer till I find the faces
Of two on a sunlit track.
Oh, grey were the great bush spaces
That flung us our wild words back !
I laughed when I heard you sigh it—
“ Good-bye,” but no word I said,
The blood in my veins ran riot;
I stayed not your parting tread,
But watched till the dusk was quiet
And the fay sky-line was red.
When night, giving rest to the sleepers,
Hangs over the silent quay,
I list to the white surf reapers
With their scythes against the sea,
Till my soul has slain its keepers,
And the danger thoughts are free.
Day comes with its jar and wrangle—
I fret at the sunbeam’s glare ;
For what is their tinsel spangle
To the molten gold they were ? •
When you strove with the twisted tangle
Of the pine-cones in my hair ?
I yield to a dream entreating
Of a pathway known to two,
And shadowy branches meeting
Where the sunshine filters through—
But never a glance of greeting,
Or word as I pass by you 1
M.L.

“Boori”: Re “H.S.”par. (11/4/’3O).
The amount of solid food a blackfellow
can surround is as wonderful as his utter
disregard of its nature. Like the emu’s,
the nigger’s stomach refuses nothing but
broken bottles. Writer once saw a black-
fellow, quite a little fellow too, stow away
a bandicoot, entrails and all, which must
have weighed at least_ 81b. As he sat and
stuffed, I could see him, like Dickens’ tea-
party guest, “swelling wisibly before my
very eyes.” On my drawing his attention
to the fact that he was swallowing some
of the hair, he merely grinned from ear to
ear and said, “Oh, that all right. Mak’em
Jimmy’s binjie blenty warm inside.”
“H.S.’s” account of the aboriginals’ mas-
sage treatment for indigestion reminds me
of something. A Sydney doctor once told
me that while travelling in the South of
France he noticed in many of the peasants’
houses a great cannon ball weighing about
501 b. sewn up in flannel. On asking the
use of it he was told that when the good
man of the house suffered from indigestion
he lay flat on his back and the good woman
rolled the ball in circles all over his stomach
until relief came.
« * 1 * *
“ Cicada ” : The fruit bats, popu-
larly known as flying foxes, like all other
bats,carry theiryoungon their breasts; they
do not hibernate. In the early days there
was a very large “camp ” at Elizabeth Bay,
and at the present time there are a number
of well-known “flying fox camps” in the
county of Cumberland. This despised bat
makes a very good dish if skinned and
curried; and the writer when in Papua
had many a good meal of fox thus treated
when other supplies ran short. The true flying
fox is only found in the south of Japan, the Indian
and Malay region down to Australia, in a few
islands of the Pacific, and in Madagascar.
Probably no other creatures congregate in such
numbers. In a large fox camp the multitudes are
past all counting.

“ H.H.C.” writes:
It puzzles Tasmanians when they read about the hard-
ship of the poor N.S.W. cookies having to eat kangaroo,
wallaby and other native game. In Tasmania such fare
is only for the wealthy city folk, and whenever a consign-
ment of wallaby arrives in Hobart the fact is always well
advertised. At the auction rooms you will find the gour-
mets of the drainless city bidding spiritedly for hind-
quarters of scraggy marsupial, often blue-mouldy from
being “ hung” too long. One well-known Hobart identity
—a re-incarnation of the late Garden Honey of Sydney—
is often to be seen hawking a handful of wallaby remains
about when he isn’t on the Risdon racecourse inquiring
anxiously “ Who’ll’ave a good cigar?” Never saw him
actually make; a deal in either line, but I know a wallaby
cannot be earned around by the leg more than three
months even in Hobart. Stopping for a day at an hotel on
the Midlands I was surprised at having my breakfast
choice confined to “ fried rabbit or stewed parrots.” For
dinner the menu was curried rabbit and “ kangaroo
steamer,” and I was the only guest who didn’t beam upon
the “steamer” with joy. Waitress looked hurt when I
inquired if there was any cold dingo on the sideboard.
Escaped by the train before tea-time. In the carriage
with me was the corpse of a kangaroo-rat, neatly done up
in ferns, and a proud sportsman who beguiled the journey
with full particulars of how he “ stalked it ” for hours
before getting in the lucky shot. He said his people were
very fond of “ rat steamer."

REGAN (fervently); “Saints be praised, it’s no
worse! ”
An old German up on a Queensland river
ekes out his livelihood by fossicking for gold, but
he also keeps a few head of stock on the common.
He has a good stockyard near his hut, and runs in
any unbranded calves or “mickies” he comes
across in the ranges and brands them. T’other
day a man rode up. “ Hullo, Hans, I see you
have an unbranded heifer in the yard there. She
is not yours.” “Datvosall right, mine friendt,
I know dat, but she vill be mine as soon as dose
irons vos hot / ”
ARTHUR MORGAN,
Queensland’s new 18-stone Premier.
Photo, by Malhewson & Co., Brisbane.
Some religious sects not before discovered
turned up in the Westralian census papers. Also
some unusually truthful persons seem to
have sent in returns. In a front place
among these latter was a man who kept
a pub. and said his religion was
“£.s.d.” The most promising new
sect is that of the “ Goldfields Chris-
tian.” It is small yet —the founder
hasn’t had time to make any converts
up to date. The “ Goldfields Chris-
tian ” is understood to look coldly
upon the Coastal Baptist, - and to
wash irregularly. There is also one
Hoke and one Hylozoist wandering
over the face of the goldfields, though,
so far, neither of these has estab-
lished a tabernacle. What kind of
religion is a Hoke ?
“ Rus ” : A far-fcoo-frequent
item in the “country news” of the
Sydney dailies is the threshing-
machine accident, Hard, dangerous,
and ill-paid work “ following the
thresher.’’ Sixpence an hour
sounds (comparatively) fair enough,
but broken time —through bad
weather, shifts, etc.—reduces the
total earnings to less than the aver*
age weekly wage ; and microscopic
dust, smut, heat, and sun-glare on
the straw make the work extremely
disagreeable. “ Thresher men ” are
mostly youths who follow the mill far
more for “ the fun with the crowd ” than
for monetary returns, for, even in a season
of good weather and plenty of work, the
cheques at the wind-up are almost invariably
very light.
“ Kauri ” writes :
A peculiar insect has been found in the hot springs of
Lake Taupo (M.L.). It is about half the size of an ordi-
nary fly, but more slender in proportion, and black.
Builds natural incubators, and is not catalogued in
zoological works. Supposed by some theorists to play an
important part in building terraces. At Rotorua there
are numbers of living creatures always in hot water as it
were. Fishes swim in wateFaroqnd there hot enough to
cook them, but by some queer provision of nature the fish
are not cooked, but simply grow coarse and ugly.
Some of the girls who go to the hot lakes for a periodical
resurrection should consider these fish and the possibility
that the water may, in time, make a girl look the same
way.
* * • *
“T.J.L.”; Re food-adulteration. I once
saw a purblind Victorian dairyman take the can
of cream from the separator-room, instead of the
skim-milk, and empty it into the pig-trough.
The mistake was immediately discovered, and
most of the cream was recovered after a fierce
tussle with the swine. I was going to remon-
strate with the old man over the business, but
thought better of it when I saw how he handled
the pigs. The butter was on sale later in the
local store, but the trail of Dennis has been over
Mulvaney’s brand for me ever since.
“ R.N.L.” tells of how an aboriginal voted
in Victoria:
Medlar, as election time drew near, addressed me :
“ Of course, you’ll see that your blackboy records his
vote, Banks—and in the right way, too ! He has merely
to state that he is an aboriginal native residing in the
district.”
“I’m not going to the trouble of coaching him.”
“ Leave it to me,” said Medlar confidently. “ I’ll see
that he acquires a proper notion of his responsibilities.
I’ll take him in on polling day and see him vote.”
Early on election day he drove up and took Paddy
aboard. The township is 10 miles away, and Medlar made
the most of the opportunity. He began by telling Paddy
how pleased Queen Victoria would be when she heard
that he had helped to send Swingate (our candidate) to
Parliament. Paddy had heard of Queen Victoria, and had
witnessed the annual distribution of blankets to blacks in
the township on her birthday. Medlar went on to explain
how Paddy was to tell the “ big-fellow boss alonga court-
house” tliat he wished to vote for Swingate, carefully
impressing the name upon the boy’s memory. Paddy
absorbed instruction slowly, but whep the tin roofs of
Westaway showed through the heat-haze, Medlar’s pains
seemed not to have been entirely wasted. Medlar, with
Paddy, entered the polling-booth, and Medlar said : “ This
black claims a vote as an aboriginal native.”
“ Well,” said the returning-officer, “ ’tis the first time
such an application has come widin me experience. Let
me see.” He turned up his form of instruction.
“ ’Tis unregular," he said at length. “ But I’ll chance
it.”
“ He can neither read nor write,” said Medlar.
“ I’m insthructed to assist voters in such cases. Who
d’ye wish to vote for, me boy ? ”
“Queen Bictoria,” said Paddy.
“Hivinsi Who’s been beguiling ye?"
Medlar returned to interpose. “He wishes to vote
for ”
“ Hold your tongue, sir ! I allow no wan to interfare
wid electors 1 Who d’ye want to vote for, me lad ?”
Paddy, laying a brown forefinger on the officer’s coat-
sleeve, smiled insinuatingly : “ You got im planket?”
And Swingate lost that vote.
SPLITTING RAILS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There isn’t much in bush work, save a “ collar” here and
there,
And a man is rather lucky if he strikes it pretty fair.
But a toiler doesn’t change his occupation till he must,
And that is why I’m sticking to the splitting for a crust ;
And by dint of solid labor for a dozen hours a day
In the best of blaekbutt timber I can nearly make it pay.
I’ve got to tie my “bo-yangs” at the bottoms of my
pants,
To guard against ground-leeches and marauding soldier
ants;
But seldom that precaution for protection aught avails,
And I’ve moments most exciting every day a-splitting
rails.
Then before I start to scarf it I must test each blessed
tree,
For some, although they’re hollow, look as solid as
can be.
I never pick the biggist, for, of course, they’re mostly
rough,
And the smallest ones are “ ringy,” and a lot not straight
enough;
So I fell a medium blaekbutt that is minus twist or flaw,
And I quarter from the small end every section that
I saw,
Split the quarters into fence-rails then, and trim ’em with
the axe
Till they’ve finest feather edges and an even width o’
backs,
And though some are spoilt by running out in “ over-
growths ’’ and “ wales ”
I raise the blooming record for a day at splitting rails.
But though by going willing still a living may be won,
The signs are getting clearer that this graft is nearly
done;
Ay, the grand old three-rail fences that they gloried in
of yore
Aren’t standing round the homesteads and selections any
more ;
And you’ll hear the wind a-moaning weird and melan-
choly strains
Through the “ wire-and-dropper ” fences that are slung
across the plains.
Ah, well 1 the graft was heavy, but I’ve stuck to it so
long
That I’ve grown to like it somehow, and I’ll miss it pretty
strong.
But it’s going, for the market’s mostly glutted ; and the
sales
Only leave starvation wages for the man that’s splitting
rails.
vio.
BEN SON.

KIDNEYS
THE HUMAN FILTERS.
Note the Following
Are They in Order?
1
On each side of the spine, in the small of the hack, is the position occupied by the Kidneys.
The Heart, through connecting arteries, sends down a continuous flow of blood to the Kidneys day and night;
the work goes on without cessation.
■■ >' > >
2
The function of the Kidneys is to extract from the blood about three pints of urine every twenty-four hours, and
are thus nothing more nor less than HXJdVJIA.3NT FILTERS.
Animal and mineral matter are contained in this urine, together with about
an ounce of urea, and ten or twelve grains in weight of Uric acid.
mmm
The great desideratum is for the filters, or Kidneys, to be in perfect order;
they must do their work completely, extracting all waste matter from the
blood. If not, the blood becomes contaminated, causing many serious
diseases and much suffering.
Healthy Kidneys mean Perfect Health.
4
The Kidneys must not be overtasked or neglected upon the first symptoms of
inflammation.
Inflammation means sick Kidneys, and sick Kidneys throw every organ out
of order.
m
Impurities and poisons are left in the blood, which give rise to Bright’s
Disease, Congestion, Diabetes, Inflammation of the Bladder, Gravel, Urinary
Troubles, Headaches, Fluttering of the Heart, Limb Aches, Dropsy, Bheumatism,
and the troublesome and well-known Backache. The urine will not flow in
the proper quantity, the patient becomes irritable, nervous, weak and languid,
and lapses into a restless, feverish condition, eventually becoming “ done up ”
entirely.
TEST
Everyone can, in a great measure, discover the condition of their Kidneys, by
the following simple test:
In the morning, place in a bottle some urine, allowing it to stand for twenty-
four hours ; if if then contains a sediment like brickdust, or if particles float
about in it, or it is unnatmal in color, milky and cloudy, the Kidneys are out of
order—they are sick, and immediate steps should be taken to repair these MOST
important organs.
The great indicator of diseased Kidneys is Backache.
