POLITICAL
POINTS
There’s talk of a select committee being
asked for to discover to what extent Sunday labor
is carried on in Sydney newspaper offices. The
idea has a great deal to recommend it.
Tasmanian Assembly passed without dis-
cussion, t’other night, a Bill extending the suffrage
to women —this for the fifth time. Presently, the
House of Dodery will, also without discussion, for
the fifth time pass the Bill out; and so the dance
goes on.
Brisbane Courier, which has been de-
nouncing the Barton Government as the puppet of
the Labor party, is , now supporting the Morgan
Administration, which depends entirely upon the
local Labor party for its existence. Circumstances
alter papers.
Bent and Irvine have now instructed the
overworked country stationmaster and policeman
to constitute themselves honorary labor-agents for
the Victorian farmer. Why these sturdy beggars
■houldn’t advertise their wants in the local paper,
just as the manufacturer does, Heaven only
knows.
John Haynes, or whoever likes to move
in the matter, should, instead of again thrusting
the old contentious abolition of death-penalty
Bill at N.S.W. Parliament, move a Bill of a
couple of clauses allowing the jury to qualify its
verdict of “ guilty ” by
ad d i n g “ without capital
punishment.” Such a
Bill would probably
encounter little oppo-
sition in Parlia-
ment, and would
get as near to the
abolition of the
death penalty as
seems possible
at present.
MR. REID AND HIS PARTY.
“Speaking at. Parramatta Mr, Reid said he would
not issue any manifesto until he had fully con-
sulted the party he led, because it was no use having a
manifesto at variance with the principles of the party.”—
Daily Paper.
MR. REID : “Hanged if I know which way THIS
dog wants to go, so it’s not much use sooling
him on .”
The disorganisation of Vic. Education
Department is evidenced by the fact that it lias
among its servants one pupil-teacher of 26 years’
standing, and several of 18 and 19 years. The
“pay ” of these sweated unfortunates never rises
beyond £4B a year. And yet the excessive cost of
education is a favorite subject out Kyabram way.
G. H. Reid at Paddington :
If there was one mistake a public man ought to avoid it
was that of exaggerating the merits of himself or his col-
leagues before they had been subjected to the test of
experience.
Perhaps George had in his mind’s eye a certain
politician who once upon a time announced him-
self as the “ Chap to Save the Country ” —and left
the country unsaved.
Quinn and Meagher both figure on N.S.W.
Parliamentary business paper as authors of motions
for, practically, the abolition of the Legislative
Council. One or other should be able to force a
debate on the subject, if it is possible to drive the
disorganised Legislative wreck into any kind of
definite action, and the debate should be carried
to a division, so that the country will possess a list
of names for and against.
Premier See, in answer to a question the
other night, assured N.S.W. Assembly that legis-
lation in regard to life assurance companies would
be considered by the Government when returns
now being collected were received from the State
statistician. Which, from John See, simply means
nothing. Mr. See is prepared to consider, or is
considering, a score of useful reforms, but never
does anything to advance one of them. His Par-
liament is a cat-prowl which does little but con-
sider motions for adjournment and swop perso-
nalities.
The Melbourne daily papers are wailing,
oh, so bitterly, because the Labor organisation is
exacting a pledge from its candidates in the
coming Federal elections. They have pointed out
in the deepest sorrow and humiliation how shock-
ing it is to send a man into Parliament bound by
a pledge, and ask what is to become of our noble
political institutions if such a thing is permitted ?
Yet it is just 12 months since the same writers
were proving what a noble thing it was to send
men into Parliament pledged to the Kyabram
programme.
One prominent defect of Yic. Factories
Bill is the absence of any provision dealing with
the employment of Chinese in the furniture trade.
Labor-Minister Murray told a deputation of
manufacturers, who asked that the Bill should
provide for the regulation of Chinese labor and
the stamping of Chinese-made articles, that he had
tried to have such a proviso inserted, but was
blocked by his colleagues.
The Australian Parliament has decided to
select the Federal capital site by open voting.
The proposal for a secret ballot, as originally in
the resolutions, is credited to Lyne (everything
objectionable gets round to Lyne’s door somehow).
Lyne has two possible sites in his electorate, and
he naturally wouldn’t want Albury to know if his
coterie voted for Tumut, or Tumut to know if the
same crowd voted for Albury.
The Victorian Government is earning
additional obloquy by its tardiness in paying the
compensation due to retired railway employees.
Compensation comprises nine months’ pay, which
is specially appropriated, and can, therefore, be
paid without a Parliamentary vote. Many of the
men have been out of the service for months and
are suffering real hardship while unable to collect
the money due to them by a mean Government.
Bent has just introduced into the Vic-
torian Assembly a bill to compel cow and pig
keepers to provide properly paved sheds, cow-
yards, or styes for their animals. But the Kyabram
cow-keeper is not to be interfered with too much.
The Bill only applies to cities, towns, and
boroughs, or to shires that ask for it. Shires that
feel so inclined can remain as filthy as before, and
the Kyabram class has a great weakness for being
filthy.
In Victoria the mad rush for land still
continues. For 105 blocks on the Lake Buloke
common over 4500 applications were received. A
few inferior blocks in Ararat drew 310 applica-
tions. Present land boards recall the land-lottery
days of Colao and Camperdown, when the best
land in the State was swindled away. The odds
against getting a block are so great that Tatter-
sail’s sweep is a better investment wherein to
sink £l.
George Reid has still all his old dishonest
notions of finance. During the financial debate
last week he said—
The House, in’a fit of virtue, said : “We will have no
public works constructed out of loan money. They must
come out of revenue.” That is an absolutely unsound
and ridiculous position. It is a cruelly wrong attitude to
take.
The cruel wrong of not leaving quite all the bill to
our children always rouses the borrowing patriot
to fury.
George Reid’s party seems anxious to lose
him. It is proposed, now that ex-Premier Barton
has taken a judicial position, Reid should transfer
his electoral challenge to Lyne, and contest the
Hume seat. The party’s desire that Reid should
abandon his comparatively safe billet, and go
where he has a fair chance to be licked, may yet
lead it to suggest that he should fight George
Turner at St. Kilda or pursue Kingston to his
lair at Adelaide.
If Federal Chief Justice Griffith gets a
pension for his services in Queensland this
is what will happen : Supposing he decides in
favour of Queensland in any case between that
State and the Commonwealth it will be said that
he did it for fear of having his pension stopped.
Supposing he decides against Queensland it will
be said that he did it for fear he should be
suspected of being influenced by the pension
question if he didn’t. This world is full of base
suspicions.
N.S.W. Opposition leader Carrutliers in a
Sydney Telegraph interview on the Federal
Cabinet changes:
An interesting point crops up in this connection,
whether the other Ministers, who still hold office, will
resign, and go up for re-election. If so, Mr. Lyne will be
sure to be opposed.
Evidently Joseph is not sufficiently familiar with
the Federal Constitution to know that it is not
necessary, even for the new Ministers, to go up
for re-election.
A protest has been made in N.S.W.
Assembly against the further desecration of
Sydney harbor by the erection of a naval gaol on
Garden Island, and Premier See promises to make
representations to the naval authorities on the
subject. The matter might engage, too, the
attention of the Commonwealth Government,
which has been very kind to the Admiralty lately,
and might claim as some sort of a return that
Sydney be spared this indignity.
A neat backhander for Joseph Carruthers,
who complains of Premier See treacherously de-
laying for two years his hustings’ promise to deal
with reduction of members. B. R. Wise speaks :
Those who criticise the delay forget that in 1896 Mr.
Reid, with whom Mr. Carruthers was then associated,
dissolved Parliament on what he and his newspaper sup-
porters declared to be the simple issue of Upper House
reform. During the three years that Mr. Reid remained
in power not a single step beyond the printing of the Bill
was ever taken in this direction.
Ex-Premier Barton gave a striking proof
of honesty in the matter of the High Court
appointments. In a rash moment he said he had
no intention whatever of taking the Chief Justice-
ship. He was probably very sorry afterwards,
but he stuck to his word and didn’t Yes-No. He
gave the coveted position to Griffith, who had far
fewer claims upon it, and, for the sake of his
promise, only appointed himself to a subordinate
judgeship under Griffith. It is difficult to re-
member another such case in Australian politics.
Westralia is slowly realising what a fool
it made of itself when it adopted the land-grant
railway system. Already it is finding that, as a
result of that system, it will soon have to buy
land for the purposes of close settlement, and now
it is actually subsidising a steam service between
Fremantle and Geraldton, in order to compel the
Midland Railway Co. to moderate its demands.
It gave land and rights away wholesale, and so
created one huge monopolist; and now it has to
pay another company to break that monopoly
down.
Apropos the proposal to
spend £20,000 of loan money on
a gaol at Bega. N.S.W. has a
gaol at Glen Times which cost
£20,000, and has never con-
tained a prisoner; an empty
gaol at Trial Bay ; another at
Silverton ; a new penitentiary
capable of containing several
hundred prisoners is being
built at Long Bay; and, with
a decreasing gaol population,
there are unoccupied cells con-
tinually in every gaol and lock-up in
the State. Industrial prison at Trial Bay
having been closed, it would be interesting to
learn something about the experiment started in
Maoriland last year to occupy the manageable
prisoners at re-forestation —a work sadly neg-
lected in Australia.
Says a Sydney paper :
Mr. Kingston’s huge political personality drifting about
the House is a constant source of danger.
Some night there will probably be a bump as the
natural consequence, and Turner will. come
hurriedly on deck, with his surplus in his hand, to
report that the vessel is filling rapidly, and Deakin
will speak eloquently over the side for four hours
to the object of collision, and urge that there
should be no personal ill-feeling.
Dr. Maloney recently brought forward in
Victorian Assembly his seventh Women’s Fran-
chise Bill. Premier Irvine said he had no hope of
getting a statutory majority, as the Upper House
had cast woman’s franchise out of the Constitution
Reform Bill, and no fresh legislation on the
subject would be possible until that Bill had
received the royal assent. The want of that
royal assent is going to be a handy peg to hang
many things on.
Premier John See (John the Considerer)
has promised to consider the demand that the
trading coupon shall be abolished in N.S.W: by
Act of Parliament. Victoria, Maoriland and
Tasmania have wiped out the parasite ; a Coupon
Abolition Bill passed S.A. Assembly but.was lost
in Jenkins’ Upper House by one vote ; and
Queensland has a Bill before Parliament. And
John See is going to “consider ” the matter. It
is difficult to remember what John isn’t consider-
ing just now.
In a Riverina town lives a Frenchman
aged about 80. He was naturalised in Victoria 40
years ago, and has lived nearly 30 years in N.S.W.
No one ever explained to him that he had to be
naturalised six times to become a full-blown
Briton throughout the Commonwealth, and
N.S.W. accepted him as a citizen and a voter
without any;question. But when he applied for
the old age pension N.S.W. repudiated him as an
alien. It was quite legal—but when is that
Commonwealth Old Age Pension coming along ?
The Bulletin receives a circular, the
work of one Lindon Biddulph, in which L.B.
seeks to point out that the only possible place for
a Federal capital is at Lyndhurst, on the N.S.W.
western line, and presumably somewhere about
Mr. Biddulph’s back door. The most interesting
item, however, is a footnote which begins thus :
N.B. —I have not considered Alburv or Bombala, as
these places are situated on or near the Victorian
The off-hand way in which a certain arrogant,
parish-pump party in N.S.W. assumes that every-
thing Victorian must necessarily be infected, and
that for a place to be even adjacent to Victoria
disqualifies it as a possible capital, is calculated to
promote a whole lot of good feeling.
The pestiferous, cadging farmer again.
The blessed Koroit (Vic.) Waterworks
Trust,, which in 1899 had £2047 of its
£5047 liabilities and .£127 of its £227
accrued interest wiped off the slate,
coolly asked Water Supply Minister
Cameron for‘the loan of £BOO the other
day, as its machinery, bought with money
borrowed' from the State and never repaid, was
worn out and it wanted to buy more. And,
instead of kicking these impudent beggars
downstairs, Cameron promised to consider
the request—that is to say, he would see
his boss, Irvine. Isn’t there one single
gang of farmers in the whole State
honest enough to pay interest on the /
very trifling amount debited to
themfor their waterworks and also
to make provision for wear and tear
of machinery ?
It is getting on for ten years
since Private Enterprise applied
for, and was granted, the right
to build the Tasmanian Great Western Railway,
and the line hasn’t yet been commenced. The
people who got the concession never seriously
attempted to build a line; their whole visible
purpose was to sell the “concession” at an
enormous price to some rich but unfortunate fools
and retire with the profits. The “concession”
has been renewed again and again, and it has been
hawked round England for years in a vain search
for the right kind of fool, and is now being
hawked round America for the_ same purpose—
and even yet Tasmania hasn’t quite raised the
courage to give Private Enterprise definite notice
to quit, and end the whole disreputable business.
The King has, so far, refused assent to
the Victorian Constitution Reform Bill, on the
ground that cool, calm Equity lawyer Irvine
didn’t know enough law, or didn’t' take enough
trouble about his law, to see that the measure was
passed by the statutory majority required to
amend the Constitution. The publication in
England of Irvine’s crazy and utterly illegal
Strike Suppression Bill probably made the British
authorities keep a specially keen eye on his law.
The position is a bitter commentary on the Cool,
Calm One’s incompetence. All the results of his
negotiations with the House of Landlords have
gone to pieces, unless the messages he has sent to
England, praying that the measure may be allowed
to go through after all, should soften the heart of
Balfour, and make him decide not to tread too
hard on the crushed worm.
THEIR BUSY DAY.
Melbourne Age recently complained that there-
was a blank in the weather telegrams from Satur-
day till Tuesday. The above represents the sort of
weather that is hanging round to take us unawares
on Sunday night.
The refusal of the Senate to hold a joint,
sitting with the House of Representatives for the
purpose of selecting a Federal capital is under-
standable enough. The Senate, by itself, repre-
sents half, the legislative power, but if the two-
Houses sit and vote together it represents less
than one-third—36 votes out of 111. There are
certain circumstances under which a joint sitting
is ordered by law, but a voluntary joint sitting is
quite another thing. The Australian Govern
ment should have selected its own site and intro-
duced a Bill. Failing that it should have pro-
posed a ballot in each House, to see if, by any
luck, the two Houses selected the same site, and l
tried the joint sitting afterwards if necessary.
New South .Wales can’t complain of its
share of Federal honors and profits. Until now
it has had three representatives in the Australian
Ministry—on a population basis more than have-
been allotted to either Queensland or Victoria. It
has supplied the Federal Premier, the leader and
deputy-leader of the Opposition; it gets the-
Federal capital, and it annexes two out of the-
v three High Court Judgeships. Also the position
of Minister of Customs was raised from the fourth
in order to the second, so that N.S.W.’s Bill Lyne-
' might rank ahead of Victoria’s George Turner.
Arid even then S.M. Herald came opt with an
article setting forth that N.S.W' vould be-
grievously wronged unless the second Australian-
Premier came from that State as woll as the first.
WHY SOME MEN
GO TO CHURCH.
“ Wot , don’t Billy
yo jishin’ a Sun-
dye no more?”
“ Not fer a while
he lent the par-
son a quid, an’ has to take it out in pew rent."
At last the N.S.W. Government makes a
feeble move towards the long-promised referendum'
re the reduction of the size of Parliament. It is
proposed to give the public a chance to vote for
an Assembly of 125 members, as at present, or
100, or 90, or 80. The person who wants a House
of 60 or 70 members is to be muzzled. The person
who wants to reduce the numerous State politician
by abolishing the Upper House is to be gagged.
And a referendum which takes no account of the
Upper House—which only allows the people to
vote, within certain limits, for the reduction of
the House which tries to do something, and gives
them no opportunity to vote for the reduction of
the House which doesn’t even try to do anything
—is a pretence and a mockery.

Nowadays every man who is good enough
to command a decent billet elsewhere is getting
out of the Victorian Public Service. Recently a
man with about half-a-dozen high-class diplomas
in chemistry and mineralogy left for a well-paid
post in Africa. A surprised Government, trying
to find out why he left, discovered that it had
absent-mindedly cut down his pay to £1 a week.
It is an evidence of the great tiredness of
Christians when a person by the name of
Nathaniel Levi, M.L.C. has to introduce a Bill
into the "Victorian Legislative Council to prevent
Sunday trading in hotels. If Levi is not a Jew
he ought to issue a writ for libel against
his nose. Levi’s Bill makes it a crime for anyone
not a bona fide traveller to purchase liquor on the
Christian’s Sunday or during hours prohibited by
the principal Licensing Act. Further, it makes
it a crime to be found carrying liquor on a Sunday
or during prohibited hours. Tin's, it would seem,
might press heavily on persons going home “with
a skin full ” by the last trains. In addition any
person requesting a child under 15 years to obtain
or carry liquor from any licensed premises on
Sunday will be liable to a fine not exceeding £5.
Levi’s enemies say that these provisions are catch-
vote-concerns and that Levi is really after power
to transfer a license from one house to another.
At any rate his Bill makes elaborate provision
for removal of licenses—a thing which a holy
Government practically suppressed some dozen
years ago.

On September 12th a few Tasmanians
remembered that it was 100 years since Boweni
commenced his abortive attempt of settlement ati
Risdon, the cramped little place with no back to;'
it. Also the daily papers, including the Hobart,
Mercury, brought out centenary numbers, care-
fully expurgated of undue references to “the
System.” As the System was almost the only;
live or vigorous item of news 100 years ago, there ;
was mighty little to write about when it was left j
out. A really good centenary number, with all
the ancient flogging in it, would have been the
most completely alive publication Tasmania has
seen for a long while. In February next, 100
years after the founding of Hobart by Governor
Collins, the threatened centennial celebrations
will be held. There will be a religious service
and some children’s buns and lemonade. Also,
John Norton hopes to deliver a political address
in Hobart about that time.
“H.S.R.”: Be par in recent Bulletin
about the two men who helped Maffy-Bunkum to
defend Mafeking, and who, I should say by their
names, were Irish. It seems they didn’t get
paid. These two men were luckier than their
countryman, station-master Quinlan, whom Mafly-
Bunkum caused to be shot in the early part of the
scrimmage.
J. H. Carruthers, at Waverley (Sydney)
-bowling carnival, the other day,- let off. a. Wrtfc-
brous joke to , the effect that while :on a visit
to Japan, President John Young had pro-
posed to bring a team of Jap bowlers to Aus-
tralia, but, owing to Federal legislation regarding
aliens, the project had to be dropped. Which is
either the usual anti-White-Australia lie or else
evidence that Carruthers is a. crassly ignorant
politician. Federal immigration laws wouldn’t
have prevented any such Japs coming, provided
they didn’t intend to settle here. The Common-
wealth law expressly provides for such cases.
Maoriland’s new Judge—Chapman—was
senior counsel for the defence in the sensational
Hall poisoning case in ’87. Tom Hall is still
working out his life sentence in Auckland gaol.
At the laying of the corner-stone of the
new Sydney railway station (Telegraph report):
Referring to the credit of the State, Premier See said
that New South Wales would always meet its obligations
at their due date.
What beautiful bumptiousness! How does
Premier See know ? There is one occasion a little
way ahead when N.S.W. is pledged to repay
nearly £8,000,000 of debt in one lump. It will
meet that obligation if some one will lend it the
money ; if the loan is refused it will be bankrupt
unless its financial system reforms very much in
the interim. And there are a dozen similar
occasions ahead.

“ N.” : The first Pacific Cable Conference
.Sydney, 1877) recommended the San Francisco
route. Factious opposition was raised in London,
by Agent-General Yogel, of Maoriland, and
Agent-General Michie, of Victoria. They urged
that the Pacific was too deep. Sandford Fleming
had to fight the same objection, the stupid asser-
tion being made that the Pacific was 13 miles
deep.

The Minister for Education in the Vic. Crooked-Irvine
Ministry inquires into all cases of leave, and always
deducts pay. An outbreak of diphtheria occurred at one
school. School would have been shut and teacher would
have got full pay—but he was fool enough to catch the
disease from some youngster, and therefore lost his pay.
The following takes the cake : The Fijian authorities
vote £50 each year for a Victorian inspector to come
over and examine the schools. An inspector does this
on his holidays, and they take it turn about. This
year, one was sent as usual, and a Minister inquired into
the bill, which is paid through Vic. Education Depart-
ment, and refunded from Fiji. When asked to pass it, he
at first refused, but, after pressure, signed for £23, pass-
age-money. Balance was annexed by the Vic. revenue.
If the inspector prosecutes, he will be sure to be sacked,
go he must allow the Irvine Ministry to rob him of £27.
A Tasmanian politician tells this story : —
I had a motion on the paper which I considered of the
greatest importance, and by sounding my fellow members
I had come to the conclusion it would just squeeze
through. At the division old Winchester came over to
my side ; then he gave a sudden ejaculation, and went
back to his seat, and I was beaten by one vote. The old
man had promised me his vote, too, and I asked him
angrily why he left me. “My boy," he said, “ I’m very
sorry, but I’d left my spectacles on my seat, and Smith
was just going to sit on them. I ran to save them, and I
didn’t like to take them and come back again, lest Smith
should think I suspected him of an intention to steal
them." So the country was lost (for my motion would
have saved it, of course) all on account of old Winchester’s
spectacles. I’d rather have bought him an astronomical
telescope than have had that happen.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
S.M. Herald threatens to wrest the Yes-No
championship from George Reid. Granny’s account of
the A.J.C, Derby Day filled a whole page, and for three
following days the old lady spread herself over two
columns of description of fashion, beauty and racing. At
the.end of the meeting the same old lady fired off a lead-
ing article on the “ Evils of Gambling," using as a text a
Christian Endeavorer’s remark that the Randwick meeting
was a gathering of all the scoundrels of the Common-
wealth- The fact that it had done its best to boom the
said meeting of scoundrels was carefully ignored.
On the rise overlooking the Randwick course,
where. the local council allows the unregistered
“ bookie ” to lay odds of two to one in thrUmmers, a full-
blooded Chinaman has started shouting the odds in
“ welly good ” style on his own behalf.
•■ - •
Some queer doings were disclosed at an inquiry
held by the M.L. Trotting Association into the general
conduct of the Auckland T.C., one of the foremost clubs
in the State. As a result, a Hotspur note was forwarded
to the. club suggesting reconstruction, the payment of
outstanding debts, that the secretary shouldn’t have any
connection with the tote, that men who were in the for-
feit-list shouldn’t be allowed to act as stewards, and that
the handicapper should be distinct from the starter.
Otherwise, the club’s programmes won’t be passed in
future.
• • • • -• •
A big M.L. Mo t’other day laid a local pressman
£lOOO to a cigarette against chances of Benzoni in
principal event of Riccarton November meeting. It will
be something of a joke if the scribe has to collect, and his
prospects are by no means bad, for the nag is still alive
and shaping well. This recalls a happening in Sydney
some years back. A group at Tatts. Club was talking
trebles and one of the party named three of late Hon
Jas. White’s horses as possibilities. “Rot," remarked a
bystanding book, “ to show you fellows you can’t pick
. trebles I’ll lay a thousand to a bottle of wine against the
' trio named and we’ll,, have the wine now.” A thriving
’ Sydney auctioneer of the present time accepted the bet
and duly gathered in £lOOO on settling day after the
third race of the treble had been run.
• • • •
N.S.W. Parliamentary Gambling Committee
heard some straight talk from one-time punter, Mr.
Justin M’Sweeney. J. M‘S. was a big railway contractor
once, and now deals in timber. With a few paltry
thousands lying idle, he embarked upon racehorse-owning
.as a means of enjoyment and possible profit. The whole
lot of his seven o/ eight nags showed on the track that it
was in them to annex a Melbourne Cup, but somehow
each time they were “ after it ” the strong-armed jockey
or the doper or something got to work. Once he
backed his Arihi for £5OOO to win Maribyrnong
Stakes, and the “ good thing ” duly came off, but
subsequently his cake in every instance went to
dough. Between ’92 and ’97 he says he lost £30,000.
Mr. M‘Sweeney’s sizing-up of I. Moses’tribe isn’t flattering.
“They will hire people to kill a horse if every other
means of stiffening fails. They have been known to hire
jockeys to throw a horse, and they will do one of those
things to Mr. Crick if he has any chance of winning the
Cup with Ossian. They’ll never let him win £40,000 —he
hasn’t a hope.” Mr. M‘Sweeney said a great deal more,
most of which is useful reading for young men with turf
proclivities. ~

Maoriland always- did take itself verra seriously.
Nowadays it can hardly get its hat on. Lyttelton Times
of September 11 : “ Have we not just celebrated the
return to our shores of a football team which made our
Australian cousins seriously consider whether they were
made of exactly the same material as the Maorilanders ?
Do we not intend to officially welcome back to-night the
Arnsts, who from the extreme scratch mark disposed of
all the Australian champions in the greatest cycle road
race of the year ? Did not even our billiard representative
appropriate the’biggest billiard handicap that Australia
has ever promoted ? With Cruciform’s victory rounding
off this cycle of sporting successes it really does look as if
■in the colonial sporting world ‘ we are the people.’"
*■ * *
Again it is on the cards that the crack three-
year-old of Scobie’s stable may win the Derby and Cup
double. Emir, at time of writing, is apparently cured of
his leg trouble, and looks all the better in health for
his respite from hard work. A great colt, unless the
-trainer has made an almost impossible mistake in his
-estimate.
• • • •
Abundance, on his three-year-old record, is
-uncommonly well handicapped for the Melbourne Cup at
9.4. His score last season was five wins, including the
A.J.C. and V.R.C. Derbies ; four seconds, one third (Mel-
bourne Cup), and only two “nowheres,” one of these
being a meritorious failure in the Australian Oup race,
when the animal was off color, and the course was rain-
soddened. With another lOlbs. on his weight Abundance
would seem to have a Cup chance.
* * » *
The Victory and Vanity Fair, winner and
runner-up in last Melbourne Cup race, will not see the
post this year. The mare seems to have knocked herself
out by a mad bolting gallop she perpetrated on the track
a while ago, and The Victory is suffering from a bad
tendon, or something. The burly little horse was ex-
pected to improve rapidly with hard work, but his pace
has gone, for the present, if not for ever.
* * • •
Must not be forgotten by backers of the “ two
Belahs ” for Melbourne Derby and Cup that same man
(“ Mr J. East") owns Scottish King, though latter is
trained in Melbourne and former in Sydney. S.K. is
among the 6.7 section in both races and seems to have
more than an outside chance of winning.
* • • •
The owners of the Maoriland flier, Achilles,
have sufficient belief in their nag to run the colt
against either the local Cruciform or Ibex, or both
together, in a sweepstake of 500 sovs. each. Wellington
(M.L.) Racing Club promptly offered to arrange the
event, and add an additional 600 sovs. and a 100-guinea
cup to the sweepstakes. If the event comes off, punters
are likely to be treated to the unique spectacle of every
horse in the event going as if Sheol would swallow him
unless he comes in first. It’s a satisfaction to even drop
cash on a race like that.
• * , * *
Significant facts about Nothos : 1250g5. was
refused for him. A big commission was invested at card-
call before last Metrop., and a load of coin placed early on
the day of the race. His showing was decidedly good,
and he humps 61b. less in the Caulfield than in the
Metrop. If he doesn’t score in the Caulfield, his prospects
in the Melbourne Cup are not too blue ; he’s quite able to
see the journey out.

Despite two record gatherings, A.J.C. com-
mittee reports a loss of £3OO on recent meet, and yet
results are better than ever before for Spring. Same
period last year saw £10,700 added, and shortage £360.
This time added amount was £ll,OOO, with £l5O more for
salaries. Wet weather on closing day did the damage.
• * • *
Regrettable that Orzil-Kettledrum colt, Kilfera,
has not so far figured, and will not shape in any spring
turf events, and might not be seen out till autumn,
through kidney trouble. Presence of Kilfera in various
big things would have rendered them heaps more interest-
ing owing to the fact that he squelched Emir, Cherson,
Duke of Grafton and co., when gathering A.J.C. Cham-
pagne Stakes’ laurels.

Fame ? From a Tweed River exchange :
“ The three-year-old gelding, Dill Maeky, is now being
hacked about the local streets. The Cardinal is enjoying
a spell in luxuriant paddocks. They will probably meet
again at the T.R. J. Club meeting on Boxing Day.
Compare performances of Australian and Maori-
land champion mares—Wakeful and Cruciform. Wakeful’s
first race was at four years. In 38 starts she won 22 races,
was second in 11, and third in three. Prize money accu-
mulated, £14,700. Cruciform commenced operations at
two years, and started nine times. Next year she made
14 starts, last season four, and present year two. Her
record is 18 firsts, nine seconds and one third in 29
attempts ; and money gathered tots up £6OOO. Wakeful
was bought by present owner for 310 guineas. Mr. Stead
paid 325 guineas for Cruciform.

* • • •
Manner in which Billali is talked makes it
appear that Caulfield Cup is over, bar shouting, but
many good judges (among them V.A.T.O. handicapper)
aren’t taking as much stock in the nag’s Memsie Stakes

Billali is his brother Bobadil’s double.
* * * *
“Beaumont Smith” thus: “The racin’, or
rather, bettin’ spirut,” said Harum, “ is takin’ people by
the throat and chokin’ them more’n more every day.
’Taint sport now. Th’ sport’s killed ! Up at Bung Bung
it was sport. Every horse, from a draught to a three-
months’ foal, would take part in the races, and the best
’orse alwuz won ; that was if he stood up. Well, this isn’t
what I was goin’ to speak about. I was on the flat at th’
races on the ’oliday and 'ad a splendid place up agin the
fence and was watchin’ th’ horses prancin’ about on the
corse. It was a scorcher of a day, and presently a man a
couple of yards away from me give a groan an’ fainted.
No one took any notice of 'im, so I sez to the man next to
’im—a young feller who ’ad 'is ’air curled on ’is forred—
* Take ’im out o’ the crowd and get ’im some fresh hair
and water.’ ‘ Gam,’ he sez back. * D’yer take me fer a
sorft? Sum’n’ll snap me plice. I’ve got a bob on this
race, an’ I wouldn’t miss it fer all the faints in creashun.’
I fanned the man with me ’at and arsked four others to
take im out, but they all sez ‘ Take’m yerself.’ So I did,
and they promised to keep me place. But there’s no
’onor in bettin’ men. Next time I take a man, or a
woman either, what ’as fainted out of a crowd, I’ll be
.” A woman passed, and Harum, who respects
women, whispered the profane word in my ear.

Plain Tom, winner of the First Hunters’ Hur-
dles at Riccarton (M.L.), is trained by his owner, Miss
Button, who would willingly ride her horses in their
engagements if the authorities would allow her.
• * . * ■ •
“ A Man on the Course,” writing in Sydney
D. Telegraph (27/8/’O3) re totalisator, says : “ Actually,
the bookmaker flourishes in its company,” and, of all
places in the world, cites Maoriland as an instance. The
cold facts are, that when the totalisator was first insti-
tuted in that State the bookmakers found they could not
make a living, and an almost general exodus ensued.
The few who remained had to lay totalisator odds, and
did not consider the results satisfactory. Most of the
howling brotherhood came to Australia, and are here
still. Some few may have gone back, but not many. The
writer states that, for some time back, odds have been
laid on the New Zealand Cup, which is not run till
November. Of course, “ long-shots ” will be laid by
bookmakers, in betting clubs or elsewhere, but the booky
is a thin crop in Maoriland now all the same.

Three biggest winning trotting owners in M. L.
last season were: J. Pettie, £954 ; J. A. Buckland
(N.S.W.) £725 ; and J. Price, £419. Last-named amount
was won by one horse, champion Ribbonwood. If the
private match with' Fritz is included, however, another
£llOO must be added to his total, and places owner and
horse at the head of both winning lists. Otherwise
Buckland’s St. Simon tops the crowd with £431.

At recent Coolgardie and Boulder meetings the
ramping and roping would have staggered the strongest
“joint” from the pony courses. Total of disqualifica-
tions—one horse and party for one month Boulder
stewards took a race away from Perplex for alleged inter-
ference with second horse (Allander), and disqualified
Mat Harris for 12 months without hearing further
evidence than that of the protesting jockey, Trewartha
The W.A.T.C., on appeal, promptly removed Harris’ dis-
qualification, and suspended Trewartha for three months
for improper conduct. Allander is a Boulder horse, and
some Boulder “ big bugs” backed him for a stake in this
E articular race. These individuals come out all right, for
oth books and tote paid backers of Allander, on the
decision of the local stewards. Perplex gets the stake,
of course, but there is no hard-and-fast rule in Westralia
about bets following the stakes.

It has come: An Explosion-Stepfast colt has
been christened “WaimangU.”
• * * •
The walking craze has gripped Maoriland with
the tenacity of an octopus. On Thursday afternoons
spider-legged drapers’ assistants pit themselves against
bacon-vendors and the like. Arrayed in bathing costumes
and blushes, they expose their hairy legs to the populace.
“ The Walk ” (which is the only name yet applied to the
insanity) always starts in the centre of the town, proceeds
—with its attendant crowd—along the principal thorough-
fares, and finishes ditto. When finishing the competitors
invariably swing their arms madly and roll their eyes in
the most horrible manner, what time the crowd waves its
umbrella and yells. Even Capital has been seized by the
mania. The Fat Men of one street challenge those of
another, and the press raves over “ the promotion of the
element of sport.” In the race most of the competitors
fall down near the first pub It is a pathetic sight to see
a fat man prostrate on the roadside from palpitation of
the heart, through hurrying at three miles ah hour.
* * * »
“C.P.”: Had the job of “doing” some golf
championships recently. Didn’t know the game from a
bar of soap, and now that I do know something about it,
would certainly give the cake to the soap. The sturdy
athletes of both sexes who competed, as a rule resembled
nothing more than a consumptive sanatorium out for a
walk, though occasionally one ran to flesh —which was
about the only violent exercise he ever could have taken.
Not one seemed to strike the happy medium. Every one
of the male golfers assumed a Norfolk suit with a sweater
and plaid stockings, while the ladies wore curtailed
skirts, exposing also plaid hosiery. The less the
hosiery contained the shorter the skirt. The sweater
also prevailed. The principle of the game is somewhat
intricate—so say the people who play it. The competitor
places the ball on top of a miniature Himalaya, then
calls his boy, who is carrying a bag containing several
thousand sticks, says “Eena-deena-dina-do,” &c., &c., as
we all used to when small boys, and selects his
club. Removes his coat, swings the stick some
dozen times, then lets drive and knocks the ball
25 miles—or inches; that is if he’s a good player.
If he isn’t he hits the air or knocks down his
Himalaya. Having administered a severe blow to the inno-
cent ball, the expert hands the club back to the boy,
resumes his coat, and starts out on his arduous journey
to where the ball ought to be. But it isn’t. Looks two
hours for the ball. This seems to be the principal science
of the game. When the boy has discovered it first try,
the player again removes his coat, selects another club
by the same formula, gives the ball another swipe, and
repeats the former performance until he has gone right
round the earth and returned to the spot at which he
originally started. Golf undoubtedly proves that the
earth is round. The one who gets home with fewest
swipes is applauded and carried to the refreshment booth,
where he makes a speech and is afterwards presented
with a putty medal. Bowls is a better game than golf,
as you don’t have to wait so long ’tween drinks. I got
hold of a chap who used to say “haw ! ” and he told me
all about “ bogeys” and a chap having to “ lay a long put
on a keen green dead,” and another fellow being “ dead
square ” or “ halving the hole,” so I got on all right, and
worked up a good column. I’ve seen fights and football
matches —which perhaps are the same thing—but for real
excitement commend me to a golf championship.
* * * *
Kurupo Tareha, who won the amateur golf
championship of M.L., t’other day, to the unfeigned
delight of Maoridom, is a fine, robust specimen of colored
humanity, stands 6ft. in his socks, and wears a smile one-
third of his own length. Tareha is a native of Hawkes
Bay, and comes from the bluest of blue-blooded
stock. His father, Te Moanawanui (Henare Tomoana),
at one time sat in the House of Representatives.
Tareha formed one of the Maori contingent that
went to England at Jubilee time and shook a great
deal of ground performing hakas for the glorification of
Seddon and the edification of the nobility. Nickolaus,
the runner-up in the tournament, has a glowing future
before him at the game of two up and one to play. When
other kiddies were playing marbles he used to bunker the
domestic cat in St. Andrews (Scotland), and his records
show that he won a match at the age of six. He is now 19.
• * * •
The Victorian football season, just closed, is
probably the most brilliant since the old days, when
there were men with gigantic feet in the land and the
personal friend of Peter Burns or Billy Hannysee
possessed the freedom of the city. But one thing which
would lead to better football would be the captaining of
the team by a non-player. As what sort of incompetent
would the military world class Roberts or De Wet had
they conducted their campaign from a place in the front
rank, where they fired a rifle all day? Yet the football
captain is supposed to bear the heat and burden of the
combat and at the same time possess the cool,
hard head of the tactician. Under the captaincy of
Alec Dick, often said to be the best general that ever
managed a team, Essendon held either first or second
position for a long series of seasons. When Dick retired
from active play nobody thought of suggesting that he
should continue his captaincy as a spectator, keeping in
touch with his men by strolling casually about the ground
outside the boundary posts. Had they done so, Essendon
might be premier to-day instead of tailing in towards the
end of the list.
• • • •
It’s wise to have your life insured before taking
to football in Melbourne these times. Three matches on
the last day of the season resolved themselves into riots,
in which barrackers and players cracked one another’s
skulls like a 12th of July picnic party. In one case the
barracker-in-chief ordered his followers to “ Go for the
pickets ” 1 They accordingly stripped a neighboring
fence, and proceeded to “deal it out” with vigor. In
the struggle several heads were broken, and one player
was maimed for life. The battle-cry of these gentle lambs
was “kill the blankers.” The game as now played
is nothing but legalised assault and battery and premedi-
tated murder; athletes of the better class are giving it
up. The abolition of football by Act of Parliament may
yet have to be seriouslv considered. .
Immediately old man Duncan reached Dunedin
he burst up the “ happy family” fiction of the M.L. team
in Australia. Kiernan, he said, was no class ; Stead had
been unfairly treated, and North Islanders were grossly
favored in the selection of teams. In one act Duncan
became the most talked-of and most cursed man in all
Seddonland. The Aucklanders were particularly sore,
and when the Auckland crowd reached Otago they cut
their.ex-captain dead, apd promised Otago the premier
walloping of football history! One paper delicately
hinted that it would be wise of Duncan to sprain his toe
before the match. Duncan ignored the hint, but refused
to enter the Auckland dressing-room to toss up with
their captain, and sent one of his team instead. He sur-
vived the match, but sore at heart, for Auckland, after
being beaten by Taranaki, Wellington, and Southland,
got home on Otago by 11 points to nil.
* * * *
All things considered, Warner’s team will be
about as representative an All England Eleven as could
be picked, and if it is badly beaten in the five-games test
the result will only prove that among the multitude of
England’s front-rank cricketers there is little excellence
and a lot of inconsistency in form.
• * * •
S.A. Cricket Association, with a fall of over 100
in its membership (now 679), has nevertheless reduced its
debt from £3600 to £1761 in three years. The loss on
inter-State matches last season was £l5, and the English
team played for a suburban association, the Oval being
let in advance fora cycling meeting. S.A. now exchanges
with Victoria one-third of the gross takings in matches,
paying £57 and receiving £226 last year. N.S.W simply
recoups any loss sustained through playing in Sydney
(after deducting profit made in Adelaide), and its subsidy
on this account came to £96. The S.A.C.A wants to
spend £4OOO on an improved members’ stand, but can’t
see its way to do so yet. It claims that the Oval bicycle
track is “ the finest in Australia.”
Eternal squabble between Sydney Cricket
Ground Trust and N.S.W. Cricket Association will yet end
in a free fight. S.C.G. people want to scoop 6d. from
every grandstand fee in connection with English matches
(which works out 27 per cent, of the whole), Associatijn
to pay out of the balance all ground exes., &c. In ancient
Sydney, Albert Cricket Ground Co.’s attitude forced
setting apart of present ground, and now the Cricket
Association talks of trying to secure a site of its own.
* * * *
Finish of the Victorian 10 miles cross-country
championship saw what was probably the muddiest lot in
all Australia —their muddiness only being surpassed by
the misery of the shivering spectators —chasing J.
Stilwell (Essendon), the nearest to him at the business end
of the journey being Ferguson of East Melbourne. Stilwell,
in winning has placed a new record on the roster, this
being his third successive victory. Herbert, of Ballarat,
holder of record time (59min. ssec.), has also three
wins to his credit, but they were not secured jn
consecutive years. Winner’s time this year (60.37),
despite the fact that conditions favored stamina
rather than speed, is an improvement on both his previous
successes. The club championship also goes to Essendon,
which is this year suffering from a plethora of pennants,
it being the proud proprietor of the Cricket, Lacrosse,
and Harrier Championships. A feature of this year’s
meet was the presence of a team from “Tassy.” The
dozen from that brief island all completed the course—a
result achieved by no other competing team.
• * * •
Two-up school proprietors are jubilant over Mr.
Justice Pring’s (N.S.W.) ruling last week in a case of
“heading ’em”—that coins are not instruments of
gaming. What about the “ kip ” ? Writer knows one
school where they toss the pennies from the two fore
fingers of the right hand now and don’t bother whether
“ cops ” are about or not.
• * • *
Likely there’ll be no match for Australian
heavyweight bruising supremacy for some time to come.
Champion M’Coll’s shoulder, wrenched in fight with
Scanlan, three months ago, isn’t nearly right yet, and
doesn’t even show signs of mending.. Hard luck for
M'Coll, who has a rich harvest in Creedon, Felix and
James waiting for him.
• * * *
That Felix scrap should be Scanlan’s last fight
in Australia. No boxing club can afford to have a hot-
tempered person of such toothy tendencies about the
premises ; he is too good a peg for the Obadiah press to
hang vast sermons on.
* * * *
Scanlan-Felix heavyweight fight, at Sydney
National Sporting Club, saw the white favorite at 2 to 1.
At end of first round Scanlan was 3 to 1, which was
absurd, considering form and skill displayed. Scanlan’s
chance was only visible during the first three rounds, and
then because there was a feeling that the black had no
heart, and that tfie changing situation might supply an
opportunity for the white right to flatten out the son of
Ham. It was not till the sixth round, when Scanlan’s
comparative helplessness and utter inability to make use
of any chances became evident, that Felix showed his
best form, driving home his long left with symmetry and
confidence, and thenceforward he was superb.- The
black man behaved most fairly, and, in breaking,
never once tried any “ smart” work, but Scanlan rarely
missed a chance to get one home during the break or
while holding Felix fast. For nine rounds the black’s
clever left was never off the Yankee’s face, throat or
chest. The 15th round was furious, and Scanlan forced
Felix against the ropes, and, gripping his throat with the
left, bent over him. Then the colored man hurled him
off, and rushed across to the referee and showed what
looked very like the marks of the Yankee’s teeth on his
breast, and the fight and the coin went to Felix on the
foul amid a storm of cheers and hoots. The Yankee
left on Monday for his native land, where they burn alive
the impudent negro who dares to stand up against the
white man.
* * * *
If the Maoriland amateur pugs succeed in
downing this continent’s reps, after the manner of their
footballers, it will be due to brute force and crass ignor-
ance of when they are beaten. Heavyweight giant
Taylor and middleweight Griffin are sluggers pure and
simple, and the selection contests revealed nothing but
physical fitness and capacity for punishment. They don’t
box, they try to knock out.

The Fury of Justice A'Beckett.
The angry Justice A’Beckett got extra
angry at Ararat (Vio.), the other day. He
was trying a lad of 17 years of age for an alleged
attempted criminal assault on a girl of 15. The
evidence for the prosecution was that the offence
was committed by accused while three of his
mates stood guard. On the other side, the offence
was denied and it was shown that the young lady
had been guilty of previous improprieties. In
summing up to the jury Justice A’Beckett lashed
out like a wild horse and said, among other heated
remarks, that if the jury believed the girl’s evi-
dence there had been an assault of a most gross
character, and the conduct , of the ruffian who
assaulted the girl, and of the three ruffians who
looked on, was more like that of a horde of savages
than of members of a civilised community. The
jury did not believe the girl’s story, and promptly
brought in a verdict of not guilty. Then Mr.
A’Beckett lost all control of himself, and assum-
ing the alleged offence to be'proved he howled
like this :
Then I take it assaults on girls in this district are a
form of amusement not punishable by law, and that this
young man and his companions may benefit by the know-
ledge and let their desires grow riot at the expense of
girls, and that, however clear the evidence, an Ararat
jury will not convict. It is a disgrace to the community.
And, as usual, no juryman had the courage to tell
the cantankerous old man that his conduct in
bullying the jurymen for finding a verdict accord-
ing to their consciences, as they were sworn to do,
was a disgrace to the bench, and a blot upon the
judicial wig, and a smudge upon the long judicial
rags. For many years past there has been in
evidence all over Australia the evil-minded girl who
brings false charges against some man, and who,
when bowled out, cheerfully admits herself to be a
perjurer, and yet goes away unpunished. Among
dozens of such cases there hasn’t been one, so far
as the writer can recollect, in which the girl per-
jurer of this kind, even when she cheerfully
admitted herself a perjurer, has suffered in any
way from her crime. Judges and Attorneys-
.General conspire together to shield her,’ just
as if they liked that kind of crime. And just
because she knows, by reason of the shameful
fashion in which Judges and Attorneys-General
tolerate this especially foul species of per-
jury, that she can perjure herself with impunity,
this kind of girl is horribly frequent. And
because of her frequency, and because of the con-
spiracy to shield and encourage her, juries look
askance at the young woman of admittedly
dubious character who comes to the court with a
glib tale of how her pure young life was blighted.
But J udges, apparently, never grow any wiser.

SPECIALITIES
Sentenced at Perth recently to two years
for bigamy—George F. Mulqueeny, said to be
identical with Mulqueeny, a witness for the
Times before the Parnell Commission. Mul-
queeny, who was a man of many parts, told
Captain O’Shea he held proof that Parnell had
given Frank Byrne £100 to escape to America,
and the gallant captain introduced the informer
to Soames, manager of the Times. Mulqueeny, in
the box, declared that Soames gave him “£4a
week and money whenever he liked to call for it.”
He has never had such a good time since, and
when arrested in Perth was selling newspapers at
the post office.
Barton’s prolonged “think” before con-
senting to quit politics is said to have been
lubricated by draughts of milk and boiling water,
mixed in equal quantities. Nevertheless, we can
take it for granted that he would never have
arrived at any reasonable decision without the
illuminating help of D.C.L. Whisky. Most intel-
ligent decisions, whether in business or political
enterprise, or from the Bench, owe most of their
wisdom to D.C.L.—The Whusky of the Wise.*
S.M. Herald announces a death which
carries back to the very early days :
COX.—September 9, at her late residence, Flood-
street, Leichhardt, in her 71st year, Annie Mar-
garet, eldest and beloved daughter of the late John
and Sarah Cox, of St. John’s Wood, Regent’s Park,
London, grand-daughter of the late Francis Cox,
Circular Quay, Sydney, and only sister of Mr.
George Francis Cox and Mrs. M. Todhunter.
Francis Cox came out to N.S.W. about the year
1791 and set up a smithy on the shores of Sydney
Cove about the. spot where Harrison, Jones and
Devlin have their big warehouses now. In Sept.,
1806, he was one of 135 free settlers who presented
an address of welcome to Governor Bligh on his
arrival (afterwards the country presented him
mostly with bricks). This old pioneer had three
daughters, natives of Sydney. One married
William Charles Wentworth, a second Richard
Hill, M.L.C., and the third married George
Bloodsworth, an inn-keeper in Sussex-street.
“ Ku-Klux ” : I got a ticket for the gallery
of the dullest theatre in Melbourne, the other
day. I stayed 20 minutes, and during that time
two things struck me forcibly. One was the
monotonous regularity of Peacock’s “hear, hear,”
whenever Irvine spoke in a loud tone. The other
was the scarcely-veiled contempt with which
Irvine _ greeted the Kookaburra’s attenipts at
cordiality. Peacock isn’t an Opposition nowr;
he’s an echo.
W. D. Beazley, of Victoria, is the first
straight-out member of an Australian Labor
party to reach the Speaker’s chair, though for
three years J. H. Carin has been Deputy-
Speaker and Chairman of Committees in N.S.W.
Assembly. Probably, in fact, Beazley puts up a
world’s record. The first President of the Bul-
garian Sobranie was an elderly peasant who
couldn’t read, but it isn’t recorded that he was a
Labor member.
The Rev. N. M. Hennessy, of Sydney
(who seems to hold a brief for the Patman, who,
his Master declared, would have considerable
difficulty in entering the Kingdom of Heaven,
and who is anything but an admirer of the class
from which his alleged Master sprang) has been
exhorting men who make their wealth in N.S.W.
to enter its Parliament “and cleanse it, and bring
in a standard of manners, and a patriotic ambi-
tion that will make the bounder crowd, that
never understood the country’s good .....
drag themselves from the Chamber in which
they never should be found.” The gentle
preacher speaks rashly and in ignorance.
Had he been in N.S.W. during the bad old
days of unpaid politicians, when Parlia-
ment contained mostly men who had made or
were making money in the State, and if lie
had heard their language, listened to their wild
invitations to “come outside,” and heard one
honorable member cast dishonorable reflections on
the female ancestors of another honorable member,
he would have realised that the present “ bounder
crowd’’ are a set of quiet, refined gentlemen by
comparison with the people he wants to put in
their places.

Of N.S.W. members of first Federal Con-
vention, in 1890, William M'Millan politically
survives; of the second (1891), M‘MiHan and
Barton politically live, but Parkes, J. P. Abbott,
Dibbs, Jennings and W. H. Suttor are dead,
politically or otherwise; of the third convention
(1897), nine members are still in politics, Abbott
being the missing man.
“Chasselas” from South-western Victoria :
The Age sa.vs :—“ Under the Duffy Act of 1862, in the
Camperdown district, out of 699 selectors there were not
250 genuine cases ; 1,250,000 acres were alienated under
this grand Act, and 1,000,000 of them passed directly into
the hands of the big squatters. The land at the time was
worth more than four times the amount given for it, and
it paid squatters to give dummies a big consideration to
sell and clear out.” As the owners did not comply even
with the provisions of the loose Land Act under which
they obtained their titles, the State practically gave these
gentlemen of the old colonial school a present of its
richest land per medium of Duffy, and the jobbery
stinks like a tortoise to this day. Yet the Age
says nothing of a land-tax, but prefers to try to exact
something in the way of inconie-tax from the dairy
tenants who run their joyless existence for the landlord’s
benefit. Just now, to defeat Tom Mann and socialists,
and stop the exodus of population until times get better,
they are supporting Irvine and monkeying with the Com-
pulsory Resumption Land Act. Meanwhile, the big
estates are cutting up as fast as they can, on outrageous
terms, to establish a precedent. When the Government
arrives at its Resumption Act there will be little to
resume which Is not loaded up to thrice its value.

“ B.” : Met a ship’s captain just now who
told me he was off to the China coast with a local
charter. “ We might pick up a line or two from
the Chows to bring back. But if we don’t, we
won’t lose much. We will go into dock there and
be overhauled top and bottom. No seven shillings
a day over there. See ! ” A necessary part of
the Navigation Act will be to provide that all
Australian-registered ships be docked and re-
fitted in Australia, except in cases of emergency.
The shipping laws of most civilised countries have
such a provision.

Spruce Myth’s brutal method of stating
his economic principles does incalculable service
to the cause of progress and humanity. Those
unconsciously going his way are shocked when
Myth explains where such principles will land
them. Eulogising the law of supply and demand
in the House of Reps., the other day, he argued
that if a person desired a horse he bought it on
the very.cheapest terms, and, similarly, he ought
to get his work done for the very lowest wage
human beings will accept. The realisation that
in Spruce Myth’s school of thought a workman is
just a horse—something that works naked in
chains, and is flogged when it doesn’t go fast
enough, and is shot when it is worn out—was a
whole treatise on the Freetrade ideal of buying in
the cheapest market.

It was-only the other day that early closing was
denounced as “ a gross interference,” &c., and a
piece of “socialistic legislation”; yet to-day the
Victorian Shopkeepers’ and Traders’ Association
—which no one would accuse of being socialistic —
actually asks Chief Secretary Murray : (1) To
have all business places, including banks, public
offices, ,&c., .compulsorily closed on a weekly
half-holiday, this to apply to the country, as well
as the metropolis; and (2) that the police shall
take stringent action against all offenders, and
that provision be made fora minimum as well as a
maximum fine for breaches of the law. The demo-
cratic party leads the way in reform, while the
Fat Man denounces it for “driving capital out of
the country,” and so on. Then, when the reform
comes, the Fat Man finds it makes things better
instead of worse, and he approves of it cordially,
without retracting any of the lies, he uttered about
it or giving one word of credit to the pioneers of
the movement. Then the next suggestion of
reform finds him cursing with all the old activity.

Preferential Trade.
There have been remarks from various quarters,
during last month, on the subject of Preferential
Trade with Britain—mostly illustrative of the
foolish habit that public men in Australia have of
throwing unnecessary eloquence about in a reck-
less fashion. The Protection Union :
Great Britain’s greatest living statesman, Mr. Chamber-
lain, finding the ruin that free imports have brought to
British industry, calls on the colonies for help in a policy
of Preferential Trade. Protectionists answer that call
with a loyal and glad assent. Freetraders refuse to give
the Mother Country-any advantage over foreigners. Pro-
tectionists unanimously accept Mr. Chamberlain’s call
for Preferential and Reciprocal Trade, with a loyal love to
the Empire. Freetraders would help the foreigners to
ruin the Empire.
Ex-Premier Barton :
With regard to Preferential Trade, the Government is
of the belief that it can be carried out quite consistently
with the maintenance of our own tariff for the purposes
for which we framed it, while giving for purposes of
Imperial cohesion an advantage which no Freetrade
policy can give to our own kith and kin.
Opposition leader Reid :—
If this tariff is to remain as it is, I am going to tell the
people of Australia that, no matter what they do in Eng-
land, I shall make no bargain with the people of that
country. I shall ask them for nothing. But I shall ask
the people of Australia to take down half the wall which
shuts us in from the mother country. I would not mock
the people of England with anything less, and I leave it
to them to fight out their own battles as to what is good
for them. I know that it must be good for them for us
to do what I propose. It has been hard enough upon
them, when they have given us our land, our lives, and
our humanity, to find a wall erected against them. They
have always had power enough to smash these walls
down, but they have been too noble to attempt to use the
slightest indication of pressure. When we are dealing
with people like that, we should be honest and frank
with them, and tell them what we mean.
Chamberlain himself seems to be the only man
who continues to say nothing definite on the pre-
ferential trade or Protection issue which he has
raised ; and until he does say something definite
there is no call for anyone in Australia to become
excited and promise things. With all the elo-
quence and scream now being devoted to the
“proposals of Mr. Chamberlain ’’—which so far
he hasn’t proposed—Australian public men are
putting their country into the position of a young
woman who is eager to say on what terms she
will marry the man before she is asked.
If, as is a possible outcome of the present
situation in Britain, Chamberlain persuades
that country to move in the direction of
Protection, undoubtedly Australia should be glad
to talk reciprocity ; considerations of business
will urge in that direction. But, so far, no assur-
ances or promises are in order. The Protection
Union blatherskite about a “loyal and glad
assent ” to a question which has not been asked is
foolish, because what Chamberlain proposes may
be something to which Protectionists cannot pos-
sibly assent. Ex-Premier BARTON’sstatement, which
is on all-fours with the views of the Canadian
Government, is, on the whole, the most sensible
yet made; if Australia decides to give a tariff pre-
ference to Britain, it would best take the form of
an increase of duties on non-British imports. But
even his guarded assurance is a good deal
too.previous. It would be a foolish step—and a step
which Australia almost certainly would not take—
to concede such a tariff preference to Britain and
get nothing in return, whilst losing heavily by re-
prisals from the non-British countries, which are,
on the whole, our most valuable customers. As
for George Reid’s top-note of loyalty, it shows
sheer midsummer madness. A 50 per cent, re-
duction of the tariff on British goods would shut
out imports from other countries in very many
lines and lead to a drop of about 40 per cent, in
the present tariff revenue. Reid may hope that
that fall would be made up for by a huge
increase, in imports, and a consequent general
destruction of local industries and an utter
inability to pay for the extra imports except out
of loans. In either case it would be a per-
nicious thing for Australia—a loss of fully one-
third of the customs revenue, or an increase in
imports of about 65 per cent. It is probable
that, Chamberlain having now given himself a
free hand by resigning from the British Cabinet,
some definite proposals will come; when they do,
it will be time for Australia to speak. Mean-
while, the rush to accept proposals that haven’t
been .made, and'to approve cordially without any
definite knowledge as to what we are approving,
is a ridiculous process of signing a blank cheque.

Lunacy Reform.
At length some definite action is likely to be taken
in Victoria for improving the method of dealing
with the insane. Two recent cases—those of
Esther Layton, the St. Kilda hermit, and
Robert Parker, the patriot who called on the
Governor-General with a gun—have brought
home, even to the unwieldy mind of officialdom,
the necessity of providing a receiving-house where
persons with “peculiarities,” and nothing more,
may be observed. It is an insult to civilisation
that people of this class should be forced to herd
with hopeless maniacs. The removal of maniacs,
who are a menace to the public safety, to a
place of restraint, is as desirable as the in-
carceration of the dangerous criminal. In fact,
the difference between them is growing harder to
define every day. But to lock the harmless or
merely suspected lunatic away in an asylum
along with incurables, and compel him to associate
with the most serious cases, is simply doing all
that is possible to make his case hopeless. With-
out doubt there is a certain uncanniness about
even the mildest cases of insanity, and it is as
much to the advantage of the public as the patients
that they should be removed from association with
saner people. At present this removal must be to
the general asylum, where overcrowding renders
proper classification impossible, and the chance of
recovery is remote. There is a natural horror
about the phrase “going into the asylum,” and,
as a consequence, mental cases seldom receive
skilled advice until so far advanced that they
are either hopeless or at least difficult. The
matter has already been considered in Maori-
land, and institutions are to be established
where suspicious oases will he treated, and per-
haps, in many instances, prevented from develop-
ing into actual insanity. The asylums are also to
be cleared of children and aged persons, whose
removal will make room for more serious cases.
With a view to allaying the popular -prejudice]
“ mental hospital ” and “patient’’are to lie sub-
stituted for the more familiar “asylum” and
“lunatic,” which are words to be as little used as
possible. Victoria, which, in the management of
its lunatics, has drifted' back to the methods of
the Bedlams of a century ago, is now threatening
to do something of the same kind, though it is
bqst not to hope for too much. The dreadful
desire to save money is always getting in the road'
in Victoria. - - - -

ECKERSIEY & SONS, 214 Queen St, Melbourne, Vic.
South Kalgurli (W. A.) has just made the costly
experiment of sending 210 tons of ore to be treated at
Boulder No. 1 battery for the insignificant return of 13oz.,
equivalent to l'24dwt. per ton. The loss on this transac-
tion must be £150, and heaven only knows what it was
done for. The big heap of residues, which late manager
Isles always asserted were valueless after leaving the
Riecken vats, is to be re-treated by the ordinary cyanide
process. The mine is said to be absolutely depleted of
pay ore, but the main shaft is now being carried down
with the view of opening up deeper levels. Very little
work has been done on the Perseverance lode in the
sulphide zone, and the west portion of the property is
practically untouched below 300ft. The treatment plant
is to be brought up to date, so as to be in readiness when
the mine is again at a producing stage. As the Brown’s
furnaces and Riecken plant are to be scrapped, the
remodelled plant will be practically new.
* * * *
Kalgurli (W.A.) continues to give a good
account of itself both in monthly output and in
dividends. The 2s. 6d., payable October 6, is the
fourth this year, making a total of £60,000, or 50 per
cent, on the issued capital. Work is going on simul-
taneously at 10 levels, the deepest being 1050ft., so that
the monthly returns of ounce ore give a very fair idea of
the real average value of the mine. The ore reserves are
now a long way ahead of the treatment plant, which will
probably soon be extended. Three lodes are being
worked, the west, middle and east, the first-named being
the best and most uniform. All round Manager Ross has
done first-class work.
* * • •
Golden Horseshoe, Kalgoorlie (W. A.), is at last
to start erecting another 50-head of stamps in continua-
tion of the new 50-head mill. The original 60-head, which
were erected on the cap of the lode, and are consequently
not too safe, will be run as long as they will save gold.
The original Martin’s air compressor, which formerly gave
trouble, has lately been overhauled, and will again be put
in work. This will enable another 20 drills to be used, so
that development may be pushed ahead faster. Whether
No. 4 lode is in the Boulder, as alleged by manager
Hamilton, or in the Horseshoe, as stated by-manager
Sutherland, is a moot point. In any case, the Horseshoe
has large reserves of ore in sight, and a series of other
lodes to fall back upon, if, unfortunately, the rich No. 4
has deserted its ground. It is more than likely that
the lode has split, and that one leg is in each mine.
• * * •
Princess Royal, Norseman (W.A.), shareholders
seem to be anything but a happy family, judging from
recent rowdy meetings over the proposed purchase of the
Central blocks on the east. Considering that negotiations
for the purchase of these blocks were opened two years
ago, the directors can hardly be congratulated on their
rate of progress. The statement made by one of the
directors, that it had taken seven years to get down to
380ft., and that consequently it would take another seven
years to reach 760ft., is not very reassuring to holders of
scrip, and does not say much for the energy of directors.
It was practically admitted that the Board had been lying
low with the view of squeezing the neighboring Central,
and apparently this policy is to be prolonged. It would
be much more satisfactory to come to some amicable
arrangement, as, sooner or later, a new shaft will have to
be sunk near the Central boundary ; the reef is dipping
so flat that the present shaft can hardly serve another
level. Unless the reef dies out, which is very unlikely, it
must eventually make into the Central ground, and, if
such is once proved to be the case, the Central blocks as
a mine will be a far more valuable property than the
outcrop property, which, even according to Captain
Treloar’s own estimate, will lose the reef at an average
depth of 1000ft. The Sons of Gwalia and the Great
Fingall Consols are already working deep level blocks
acquired after the co.’s were originally floated, and, but
for these acquisitions, they would both be nearing the end
of their tether.
• • • •
Bellevue Prop., Mb. Sir Samuel (W.A.), is the
scene of great activity, as portions of the new 40-head
mill, which is to supersede the present treatment plant,
are now arriving.on the ground. The mine has recently
been placed in charge of H.’C. Vail, a’Murkan engineer,
and since his advent thing's have hummed. Previously the
new main shaft was progressing 3 to 4ft. a month, now it
is going at the rate of 30 to 40ft. The property is
traversed by a very wide lode, running in places up to
30 to 40ft., which has so far been producing about 1800
tons monthly of 17 to 18dwt. ore. The new mill is ex-
pected to treat 4500 tons at least for a yield of 3500oz.,
and, as the old mill has been more than paying its way, it
is anticipated that the co. will be on the div. list early
next year. This mine is one of the best outside the
Golden Mile.
• • • •
Ironsides, North Kalgurli (W. A.), into which
shareholders have been beguiled into paying 6d. calls for
the past year or two, has just cleared up 57 tons for ,
4oz. 7dwt., an average of per ton. And yet share-/
holders have again and again been told that the mine was £
looking well, and that ore was being bagged for treatment, i
The co. appears to be an absolute wild cat, which will
never give holders a run for their money. Hannan’s
Prop, shareholders also have long been gulling themselves
with the yarn that they would get the same lode in the
Queen of the West lease, and that it was of a payable
character. The result of this crushing should about open
their eyes.

The August yield of the W.A. mines tots up to
206,617oz., and brings the total of the first eight months
of the year to l,640,882oz., as against l,476,063oz. for the
same period of last year. This shows an improvement of
164,819oz. The gain is really far more, as quite a dozen
mines, including such large producers as G.B. Persever-
ance, Great Fingall Consols, Ivanhoe, Oroya-Brown Hill,
Sons of Gwalia, Cosmopolitan, etc., are now stating their
returns in fine gold worth £4 4s. lljd. per oz.
* * ; * *
Oroya-Brown Hill (W.A.) has very 1 - nearly
aompleted the extension of its treatment plant, and its
full 50-head mill should be runrting some time-.in October.
When in full swing the plant should treat about 7000 tons
monthly, and very materially increase'returhs'and profits.
The reserves of ore have been considerably augmented of
late, as the new underground boss, Williams, found that
the stopes above the fifth level of the Brown Hill had been
filled with pay ore, which is now being sent to the mill.
He also found new bodies of high grade ore at the same
level by drilling into the foot and hanging walls. On the
Oroya north block the winze below No. 10 level has gone
down all the way in 6oz. ore. Too much notice need not
be taken of this, as the winze follows the rich chute on its
dip, and practically scoops out all the rich ore. This was
done also in the Associated Northern.

Golden Ridge (W.A.), which was discarded by
John Bull as a wild cat after much money had been spent
in equipment and development, looks as if it would
shortly yield large returns to the local syndicate that re-
pegged it. To-date, mine has produced 7128oz. from'
6778 tons, the latest return being: 6230 z. from 465 tons.
From proceeds of gold won the syndicate purchased the
adjacent Waterfall property, which is now connected by
tramline, and very shortly 20 head of stamps will be
producing over lOOOoz. monthly. The chief difficulty is
scarcity of water.

Cumberland, Niagara (W.A.), which has been
in existence for over five years, is to be reconstructed
with 120,000 shares, or 20,000 more than previously.
Shareholders in the old co. will have the doubtful
privilege of buying one new share for each five old shares,
and will likewise get share for share in the newcO. fully
paid up. The co. owns 84 acres, and is equipped with a
20-head mill, which has crushed regularly since March,
1899. The average was originally 13dwt. from the plates,
but latterly has dwindled to 6Adwt.
* * * •
North Kalgurli (W.A.) is getting short of funds,
and all underground work has been stopped pending
starting of mill. If the mine cannot pay its way in
future, another reconstruction will be inevitable. The
co. appears safe for a year at least; but, as there will be
no funds for undertaking further development, it looks
as if, sooner or later, something unpleasant would happen.

Golden Pole’s, Davyhurst(W.A.), initial crushing
is a long way short of expectations ; 600 tons yielded only
11490 z., worth £4400, whereas it was predicted that a
monthly profit of £SOOO, equivalent to a div. of 6d. a
share, would be earned. The mill ran 21 days only, so
that with a full month’s run, the tonnage will be con-
siderably augmented. As the new plates would absorb
a fair quantity of gold it is premature to say what the
mine is really capable of.

Writes a Hobart pressman :
The late Nicholas J. Brown, Speaker of Tas. Assembly,
was conspicuously Tasmania’s most gentlemanly legisla-
tor. Politically, Brown’s qualities were such as can only
move a democrat’s derision ; but, personally, Brown was
a genial and estimable man, a perfect host, and at all
points a very amiable companion. As Speaker he was
suave and placid, with a pronounced Tory bent and a
habit of giving magnificently improper decisions
with a very magnanimous serenity He was of the
old school; and however little love is due to
the old school in . 1903, Nicholas Brown will be
mourned by many as an excellent fellow who
passed away over-early. Nicholas Brown was Hobart
agent and representative of the Lyell company. This
same company recently sacked Labor-man J. J. Long, on
the stated ground that the Lyell company would not
employ members of Parliament. Circumstances alter
c*ses.
N.S.W. Minister Kidd went up the Moun-
tains last Monday to “ formally open ” a motor
car service from Blackheath to Jenolan—to open
a sort of omnibus, in fact. One great reason why
N.S.W. never gets any decent local government,
any graduated land-tax, absentee-tax, closer settle-
ment law, Upper House reform, any State Land
Bank worth mentioning, why its finances are awful
and its debt ditto, is that Ministers haven’t time
to attend to big things—they have got to go and
“ formally open ” some darned bush omnibus or
other. And in course of time there has developed
the Minister who is only fit to ‘‘ formally open ”
the new omnibus, and the corresponding Parlia-
ment is developing. B. R. Wise is about the only
N.S.W. politician now who is above the omnibus-
opening level.
Attorney - General Wise introduces to
N.S.W. Council this week a Bill to change the
law position created by the “Taff Vale decision.”
The marrow of the Bill is that workers may
combine to ameliorate (1) their own conditions ,of
employment or (2) the conditions of others; and
in combination may refuse to work for or with
any person ; and ‘ ‘ may use any means which
are now or may be lawful to be used
by any combination of traders in pursuit
of gain, or by any other lawful means.” Further,
no union is liable for the actions of any
agent, if 3uch actions are performed contrary to
the instructions of the union and are repudiated
bona,fide by the union. And the Arbitration Act,
Sec. 7 (2) (a), is so amended as to make it plain
that a union registering as an industrial union is
thereby relievedx>f all pains and penalties except
such as are provided in the Industrial Arbitration
Act.
