At Poverty Point.
A defeated Parliamentary candidate who has any pre-
tensions as an orator is always certain of a position as a
“ door talker ” for a side show. A door man was wanted
the day after a recent election. The defeated candidate
grabbed the position in front of twenty applicants, and is
now vigorously engaged in drawing the attention of the
passer-by “to the greatest show on earth visible inside
for the small sum of sixpence.”
The Maoriland musicians who went to England saloon
and came back steerage, after drumming through the
Cold Country with dire financial results, knocked against
surprises everywhere. Big magnates, who seemed to look
upon them as wandering curiosities, asked if their
“colony” had sent any men to the Boer war, as Canada
had done. “ Why,” returned the flabbergasted minstrels,
“we sent thousands 1 ” “Good gracious 1” was the in-
variable reply, “we never heard of it.” Yet the poor,
simple Maorilanders thought that, after Seddon’s great
advertising freak, the whole world knew it, and that the
band would get tremendous receptions everywhere.
Its arrival, however, only sent people scurrying through
geographies to see where on earth Maoriland was, and if
it was really connected with Asia by a bridge.
“ F.”: Why doesn’t Ada Orossley give more “ sacred ’’
songs? A contralto voice should never try to frivol or
to be passionate ; it cannot pierce through the air as a
sword of poignant feeling, or run up the scale of emotion
on little wings of violet and red flame. Its metier is to
express a massive British grief with a suggestion of
Divine consolation—the sentiment of the widow who is
gluttonously sorrowful in her fine weeds and her great
house with the photograph of the deceased’s splendid
mausoleum over the mantel-shelf.

here.
“ Lion-tamer ” Pagel, who nightly monkeys with a lion
at Wirth’s Circus, can’t expect to be always merely
scratched on the forearm, as happened the other night.
Already, no doubt, some morbid people are following
him round in the expectation of seeing the lion bite off
his head.
Only once while in Melbourne did pianist Hambourg let
himself off the chain. This was when the softgocds
fraternity lured him round to its club and made him
listen to the tootlings of some of the dimmer musical
lights. Mark was afterwards taken off, and somebody
proposed his health. The lion joined in drinking to
himself, and did not attempt a reply, but impatiently
shook his mane and said, “Now for billiards.” Hambourg
is a fine billiardist, and would be still finer if his hair did
not get into his eyes.

Josephine Scantlebury writes: “ Comparison of the
voices and positions in the musical world of Clara Butt
and Ada Crossley is absurd. Clara Butt is over 6ft. high,
made in proportion—a tower of strength, and a voice of
such power that it appals you to listen. Clara Butt never
was and never will be an artiste. A voice of such sledge-
hammer magnitude must, of necessity, be rough, and
cannot be subdued to touch the heart. It is like a torrent
that sweeps all before it. I heard her in the Albert Hall
with full organ accompaniment, with forte stops all on ;
but above all rang out the trumpet notes, ‘ God who made
thee mighty, make thee mightier yet.’ The strain was
so great I felt thankful when it was over. Ada Crossley
is the most artistic singer I have ever heard. A rich and
velvet voice, full of pathos, whose sympathetic quality of
tone touches the heart and makes you feel ‘ a lump in the
throat.’ What strikes the hearer most is the evenness
and ease of the production, and the particular richness
of the middle and upper register and clearness of enunci-
ation. She is the sweetest singer of the day, and in her
subdued and sympathetic style has no equal.”


The drunkest train in all Maoriland, and
almost the drunkest in Australasia, is the one that
leaves Dunedin (M.L.) for Port Chalmers at 11.30
p.m. on Saturdays. Port Chalmers is a Pro-
hibition town now; the lumpers and such-like are
paid on Saturday, and they have the afternoon to
spend and the long, dry Presbyterian Sunday to
provide against. Dunedin hotels close at 10, but
the bar at the station remains open till the last
passenger train leaves, and then it takes six
policemen at least to shovel out the incapables
and load them into the train. Sometimes
the wrong incapable is thrown into the
wrong train, but it is necessary to clear
the place up, and the stray and unaccount-
able drunks are hurled in at random, and the
locomotive goes off breathing beer and vengeance.
When a busy and not very respectable seaport
has to concentrate its week’s drinking into Satur-
day evening the results are terrible. Port Chal-
mers is a dismal place on Sunday morning. The
heavy breathing of the drunks who are recovering
in bed sounds like a breeze shaking the bones on a
gallows, and the feet of the lost drunks —the ones
who were cleared up promiscuously when Dunedin
shut up for the night—are not beautiful on the
highways as they wander about asking the name
of the town and how they got there.
“L.W.”: “Should a widow marry?”
Certainly—if she can prove to an impartial
examination board that she can cook. If she can-
not cook—certainly not; the chances are ten to
one it was her bad cooking that killed the original
old man; in fact the cemeteries are full of the
bodies of men who were killed by their wives’
bad cooking. Probably the Other Place is full of
their souls. If a widow can cook she should be
made to re-enter the married state. For the busy ;
man the widow is invaluable. She conies to him
clothed in her former experience ; she is a skilled
hand and not a raw apprentice. She knows what
men require, and better still, what they like.
When he comes home on the rim of his hat, in-
stead of threatening to go home to ma, she bustles
round to get her lord and master fit for the office
next day. When a man marries a spinster it is
six months before he even dares to stay out late,
let alone come home on the rim of his hat. Then
he has to spend several years teaching her the
things she pretends she never even heard of, and
much valuable time is wasted bringing her to a
proper state of forgetfulness of things that it is
better she should disremember. All this is saved
by marrying a widow ; she starts off the same
mark with the man, so to speak.
An indignant Launceston man sends a few
facts anent the management of the smallpox out-
break in reply to B. (12/9/’03):
At the commencement of the smallpox outbreak the
Central Board of Health sent to Launceston its secretary,
a most estimable young man with no previous experience
whatever, and he, after being in the city for some days,
did absolutely nothing, and did it very well. The Central
Board exhibited every symptom of hysterical scare, and
issued fresh orders almost every day. Those objected to
by the citizens of Launceston were the ones which isolated
the city by ordering the placing of patrols at all outlets
and the prosecution of persons leaving unless vaccinated.
The Central Board also hysterically demanded the imme-
diate removal to the hospital of all patients, while the
hospital was not in a fit state to receive them, and the
wholesale vaccination of everybody while there was no
lymph with which to vaccinate them. Launceston’s
“ delirium” and “ insurrection ” consisted of a public
meeting protesting against the illegal isolation of
the city, and an impromptu procession emphasised
the protest by marching past the patrol at one of the
outlets of the city. Dr. M'Call, Chief Secretary, came to
Launceston as Chairman of the Central Board of Health,
and every measure he suggested was carried out, though
it must be conceded that our local officer of health did
not afford him that support which it is considered he
should have received. The first certain news of the out-
break was received on the 22nd June, and not until July
7th did Dr. M‘Call give authority for the erection of
additional wards at the hospital. Up to that date the
entire accommodation consisted of four small wards of
1300 cubic feet each, intended for the use of eight
patients, thus allowing 650 cubic feet of space for each,
and as it is generally conceded that small-pox patients
should have at least 1500 cubic feet each, it may be
imagined that the accommodation was not first-class.
The Central Board of Health had supreme control over
the hospital; the local Board had no authority at all, but
as time was passing Dr. M'Call, on his own initiative,
gave the local Board authority to construct a couple of
additional wards, of which the first was put up in a few
days, and the second only a short time afterwards, giving
accommodation for 16 additional patients. These wards
were erected by the local Board, and afterwards other
wards were added by the Central Board. That the
measures adopted by the local Board were effective is
proved’ by the fact that nothing was altered after the
appointment of Dr. Elkington, and that the epidemic
was practically “collared 4 ’ before the doctor’s arrival.
As to the decision of the Government not to charge the
cost of combating the epidemic to this year’s revenue, no
one condemns this senseless proceeding of “passing on”
extraordinary expenses more than I.
Thechurches have, for somereasonor other,
always opposed sanitary progress. They seem to
have acquired a taste for unclean methods.
Cremation, as opposed to the hideous practice of
planting dead bodies in and around the cities, has
receivedno encouragement from the churches. The
repulsive methods of administering the wine in
the Communion services have long been objected
to by the clean, decent party of civilisation. A
Colac doctor raised the question again at a Pres-
byterian meeting. He gave a case where 50 per-
sons had sipped from the same cup, and declared
that such a state of things was inimical to health,
and that the danger of contagion was very great.
Writing on this subject two years ago Dr. Gress-
well, the chairman of the Melbourne Board of
Health, said :
I do not suppose that any physician, any pathologist,
any bacteriologist, would venture to say that the drink-
ing of wine from a vessel from which another or other
persons had previously drunk (the vessel having been
merely wiped in the intervals while being passed from
one person to another) would not involve risk of con-
traction of infection of various sorts, such as diphtheria,
tuberculosis, &c. ; while repugnance to such pro-
cedure, if not the gravest apprehension of danger of
infection, would, I think, be at once aroused if such a
disease as cancer of the lips or mouth were borne in mind.
Years of Torture
MR. JAS. LANE, OF ROSE HILL, ORANGE, CURED OF CHRONIC SCIATICA.
AN OLD MAN’S STORY.
No farmer is better known in the Canoblas
District than Mr. James B. Lane, who lives at
Rose Hill, three miles from Orange. As his
wife says, “ they are as well known as the Town
Clockin Sydney.” Mr. Lane has been a martyr
for years to Sciatica, which no doctor has been
able to cure. Off and on, for thirty years, he
has suffered, experiencing that fearful grind-
ing, agonising pain, which all Sciatic and
Rheumatic patients know only too well. Mrs.
Lane, in a recent visit to the Specialists of
The Freeman and Wallace Institute, told
them of his marvellous recovery, through
wearing their ONE POUND ELECTRIC
“INVIGORATOR” BELT,’ and adopting
their Remedies. Here is her story
“You say your husband has suffered for
thirty years ? ” asked the Specialist.
“Yes. Off and on,” replied Mrs. Lane,
“he has suffered fearfully. Last winter, in
1902, he was confined to his bed; His right
foot and leg were swollen greatly. He was
never free from the pain, and never expected
to get well.”
“How long has he been under treatment?”
“He wrote for one of your Orte Pound
Electric 4 Invigorator ’ Belts about twelve
months ago, and took your medicines; He
improved from the start. ”
“And what is his condition now!”
“He is so well that he can go without the
stick he used for years. He Can now ride
round the paddocks for his coWs ; a thing he
could not do before. Anyone at Rose Hill
can prove this, and I think it all the more re-
markable because of his great age. Had he been
younger it would not have been so wonderful.”
“How old is he?”
“Seventy-three years old,” replied Mrs.
Lane, with pride, “but thanks to you and
your wonderful Belt, he is the picture of
health to-day. It is really a wonderful cure.
I am glad to be able to speak of it to you.”
“We are glad we have done him good.”
“Good is not the name for it. When I
think of how he suffered, and look at him now,
I can scarcely understand so great a change
for the better. I have brought the Belt to see
if it wants re-charging. Will there be any
price to pay? We do not understand it ex-
actly, but we have followed your instructions
as to its wear correctly.”
“There will be no charge whatever. We
will hand it to our professional electrician,
who supervises these matters. _ It will be
ready in a day or two. Call again and see us.
We shall be glad to see you at any time and
hear you tell such a pleasing story.”
“I think everybody should know of this
wonderful recovery,’’ said Mrs. Lane, as she
left the Consulting Room. “Persons suffering
from Sciatica might be. cured as well as my
husband. I am sure he is a living advertise-
ment to your wonderful skill, and to the great
powers contained in your Electric ‘ Invigo-
rator’ Belt. Is he to go on wearing it round
the waist as usual ? ”
“Yes ! if necessary. But if he is free from
all pain he need not wear it again.”
“ Very well, I’ll call for the Belt to-morrow,”
and with a smiling face, Mrs. Lane took her
departure.
This is Mrs. Lane’s story. Any sufferer should
w.'ite to the Specialists for full particulars. This is
only one case. The Specialists have thousands o
testimonies, and will mail a special pamphlet of
testimonies of cure free to any applicant.

“ The greater number of deaths occurred
among the children of the poorer classes living in
closely populated centres, but scurvy and rickets
were more common among the children of the
wealthy, who could afford to buy expensive pre-
pared foods, and used them because they were less
trouble.”—Dr. Argyle, in a lecture on the feeding
of infants, before the Aus. Healtn Society.
Casual philosophy :
Woman is like food—good and plain’s the safest. The
rich often tends to heartburn—so does any, if taken too
hurriedly.
Words are golden, so want careful weighing. The
Jourc6'Of your neighbor’s wealth may be still a secret,
but it isn’t necessarily a secret still.
You can’t judge a man by his wife. Yet every husband
it judged by his wife—it’s her privilege to do so.
The married man often carries the mark of it on his
face—especially if his wife wears metal hair-curlers.
If you see a man chewing cloves when leaving his club
It’s proof that his actions are subject to criticism—
probably by his wife.
It isn’t every husband that’s independent enough to
•sat onions for supper.
Some misfortunes bring their own remedy—if you’re
knocked down you’re generally knocked up.
A cat can see in the dark. A girl can’t—but she can
generally tell whether her young man has had a shave.
Love’s called a spoon, being supposed to stir to sweetness
the cup of life. There are mustard-spoons as well as tea-
spoons, though.
“Money makes the man”—a fair thing seeing that
man makes the money.
“ Truth is stranger than fiction ” —because it’s more
rare.
The horse is the symbol of truth—his ’ay is ’ay and his
neigh is neigh.

IMPERIAL.”
A well-known society dame entered the shop
of a Collins-st. optician, the other day, and desired
to have her sight tested —but no fault could be
detected with her vision. Not satisfied, she had
almost every pair of pince-nez in the place hauled
out, and tried them on—still no result. At last
the resourceful assistant produced a beautiful
gold-rimmed affair that got her in the right
spot, and she paid the handsome price without
a murmur. Next day a very ruffled and excited
old lady again entered the shop and pounced on
that assistant. ‘ What sort of goods do you sell,
young man ? ’ she commenced. ‘ The best, madam,
I assure you ! ’ 4 The best! Why the pince-nez
--
I bought yesterday just suited my sight beauti-
fully and I could see perfectly all the evening.
But this morning when I went to use them, both
of the glasses had dropped out and quite dis-
appeared. We couldn’t find them anywhere.’
‘Glasses ! ’ remarked the assistant, ‘ what glasses ?
The gold rims I sold you never had any glasses in
them ! ’

They take their love-making very seriously
in Russia. A gruesome telegram in the London
papers of July 27 tells how 33 young women of
the Don district, working in the fields, locked
themselves into a barn at night “ to secure them-
selves from the importunities of farm laborers ” ;
whereupon the men, resenting this unsportsman-
like conduct, set fire to the barn and burned the
whole lot to death !
A recent commission appointed to investi-
gate the cause of the prevalence of leprosy
amongst the Kaffirs in S’Africa found that the
disease was due to the mass of badly-cured fish
sent inland from Capetown, and consumed by the
Kaffir aforesaid. This finding is to some extent
endorsed by the investigation of Jonathan
Hutchinson in India. Eleven years ago a British
commission decided that there was no connection
between leprosy and fish-eating because many
patients denied ever having eaten fish. Against
this theory Mr. Hutchinson points to the record
of a small island called Salsette lying off Bombay,
It has a population of 50,000, mostly occupied in
fishing and fish-curing. All being direct
descendants of converts made by the Jesuits during
the Portuguese occupation, the inhabitants are all
lioni an Catholics, and observe fast days and eat
fish largely, as well as catch and cure it. And Mr.
Hutchinson found that during the last eight years
all the lepers in the Bombay Presidency came
from this one small patch !

In the first Assembly under Respon-
sible Government in S. Australia (elected in
1857), three men sat for Mount Barker. One
(F. Krichauff) still survives, another (John Dunn)
lived to be 95, and the third (W. Rogers, father-
in-law of Attorney-General Gordon) has just died
at the age of 85. An 80-year-old brother of
Mr. Rogers died the day he was buried.
Treasurer Butler (S.A.) worries still over
the financial outlook, although the harvest pros-
pects are excellent. He has an “undertaker”
aspect at all times, and is taller and thinner even
than his predecessor, Holder; and he appears to
have no relaxations. Holder preached now and
then, or unbent h’imself at a tea meeting, but
Butler is unvaryingly lugubrious. Still, his eyes
do gleam with a sad joy when you tell him that
another rich man has died, and remind him that
both the wealthiest South Australians are over
80 years of age.
“Top”: Truganina, recently mentioned
in The Bulletin as a variously-spelt name, was
an old-time aboriginal belle, and absolutely the
last of the Tasmanian race. She died in the early
70’s. In the 40’s, she was in Victoria for a time,
having been brought over with some of her friends
by Aborigines Protector Robinson to assist him
in civilising the local blacks. After a while,
Truganina and her party, wanting to return to the
land of their birth, wandered away to Western
Port, having some vague notion that they would
be able to cross Bass’ Straits. When near Cape
Paterson, they decoyed ashore a vessel engaged
lu ” he _ seal trade, and murdered the crew. For
that offence, two of the male blacks were hanged
in Melbourne gaol.
“H.” : The newspapers to hand fill out
our cabled information about Pius X. It is clear

Concerning a new Judge :
F. E. Chapman caHed to the Bar in 1872, has been ap
pointed Supreme Court Judge in Maoriland, in succession
to the very aged Justice Conolly. The new Judge is a son
2r-? J e , nr £, Sara , . Chapman, who was called to the Bar at
Middle Temple in 1840. In 1843, Pa Chapman was ap-
pointed to a Judgeship in Maoriland, which he relin-
quished in 1852 for the post of Colonial Secretary in Van
Diemen s Land. He lost that office owing to his sympa-
tny with the anti-transportation party. He returned to
where he refused a West Indian Governorship.
54 he moved to Victoria, and in ’55 became a member
legislative Council. He was Attorney-General in
the first and second O’Shanassy Ministries. In 1862-63
he was an Acting-Judge of the Supreme Court of Victoria
during the absence of Redmond Barry. In 1864 he was
appointed a Judge of the Supreme Court in Maoriland,
and lived in Dunedin until his death in December, 1881.
Mr. Chapman’s first wife, whom he married in 1840, was
a daughter of Mr. J. G. Brewer, barrister-at-law, and
afterwards a County Court Judge in Victoria. In 1865
Mrs. Chapman went to England, where Mr. F. R. Chap-
man was left for his legal education. The family took
passages in the ill-fated s.s. London, which foundered in
the Bay of Biscay in January, 1866.
Hall, M.L.A., of N.S.W. Labor party, the
youngest member of the Cornstalk Assembly, has
passed his final examination for the Bar. It is
pleasing to hear that no more Labor members are
pointing that way at present. The Bar is so
horribly overcrowded now that there isn’t a fair
living for half its members, nor an honest living
for one-third. And if it wasn’t for the old failures
dropping out—emigrating, turning cooks or bottle-
washers or kitchenmen, making for the Labor
Bureau, getting into gaol, or going to sleep out in
the Domain—things would be worse than they
are. Whenever The Bulletin hears of an
amateurish-looking burglary it wonders if some
lawyer has been driven to supplementing his
scanty earnings with a crowbar.
If ’Siah Symon goes out of the Senate the
vacancy should be filled by Kirkpatrick, who
stood a good seventh on the South Australian
poll. When Robert Reid was sent up in Victoria
the excuse was that if he wasn’t seventh he was,
anyhow, eighth ; and H. J. Saunders was chosen
m Westralia vice Ewing because he was the seventh
man. In South Australia, however, the political
claims of the seventh man (apart altogether from
his personal claims, which are great) are being
carefully suppressed in the interests of Louis Von
Doussa, a Conservative State member, who wasn’t
even a Federal candidate. Kirkpatrick’s offence
is simply that he is a Labor man. Von
Doussa, on the other hand, piloted through the
Assembly the Snow Tramway Bill, which has
been so huge a frost; and probably the Houses,
sitting together, will elect him as a reward for hig
great services in that connection,
Victorian Chief Secretary Murray aroused
himself from his customary lethargy when a depu-
tation from the Criminology Society waited on
him t’other day and made several sensible
remarks. He hit out at the barbarous solitary
confinement system, and declared that he was pre-
pared to recommend its abolition. “If our
Judges,” he said, “knew what solitary confine-
ment meant they would very seldom order it.”
Then he hinted that some of their Honors ought
to be treated to a personal experience of the lonely
cell which makes so many criminals and many
more idiots. Murray also said he was in favor of
imprisoning proved habitual criminals for life. It
was only fair to deprive a man of his liberty when
he abused it constantly.
That fine old Victorian gentleman,
Thomas Bent, didn’t allow even the opening
day _ of. the local Assembly to pass without
getting into conflict with a fellow member through
his studied politeness. Bromley was making
some remark about the Sandringham station*
which is being erected in Bent’s own electorate, at
the end of a hopelessly non-paying line, whilst all
the other works have been stopped by the firm
hand of Thomas. Thomas broke in with the
statement that Bromley was a fool. There is
hardly a sitting of the Victorian Assembly at
which Bent doesn’t call someone a fool; it’s his
way of replying to suggestions that he should
cover up his tracks more neatly after he has done
something that looks like a political job.

The Great Australian Cow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sweet singers of the Southern Seas
Which lave our Austral strand,
Bard-builders of bush elegies
By poet’s sleight-of-hand,
Just hitch your brumbies to the trees
And listen to the band !
That sun-scorched clime of drink and curse,
Yclept the “ Western Plain,”
We shall not here depict in verse
Whose metres mourn in pain :
The Shearer and the Shearer’s Horse
Must rest—as we’ll explain.
Too long this woolly pair and bold
Have occupied the stage —
The songs of blood and brawn and gold
Been quite the writing rage ;
With tramps and teamsters o’er-extolled
Pervading all the page.
And when some critics trembling tried
To pass a few remarks,
Like Roman lords the poets cried,
“ The envious dingo barks ! ”
And chucked the beggar overside
And fed him to the sharks.
The swag was by verse-heroes swagged
Across a dactyl West,
Until each trocheed corpse lay snagged
With it upon his chest;
Or, fresh from flooded river dragged,
Spondeed at length to rest.
The native Muse shall now entwine
Fresh clover for the brow,
And o’er a land of palm and pine,
From Townsville to Cape Howe,
Shall sing, through Summer days ashine,
The Great Australian Cow.
With inspiration from the North
(A new and special brew),
We fain would bud and blossom forth
In praises of the Moo ;
A lacteal lyre loud strike henceforth,
To song and subject new.
Grave Brahmin bards, well versed in milk,
This singing band will be —
The horse-rugg’d Muse prepared to bilk
Of many a future fee ;
For they can flatten out that ilk
On points and pedigree.
From Milky River shall the white,
Pure inspiration spring,
The milk-cans gleaming large and bright,
The leg-rope and the ring,—
Which keeps the calf in penance trite—
These be fit themes to sing.
Those dauntless souls who danger scorn
In valor at the bails,
At sunny eve and dewy morn
In filling milking pails;
They shal, in future song, adorn
Poetic New South._Wales.
Why should the Horse for ever hold
A verse monopoly,
Horse tales in divers ways be told
And favorites beaten be,
With not a rhymer to uphold
The Austral Cow—save me?
Why should the Swagman largely loom
And all the credit steal ?
Before the bad Bushranger’s tomb
All poetasters kneel ?
For Milkmen there is surely room —
The Cow must have a deal!
Sweet singers of this mortgaged land
(In pawn to many Jews)
Get grass ! or else, converted, stand
To wider bovine views.
Behold the meek and sleeky band,
New subject for your muse !
Horse poets have we plenty bred
And teamster bards a few,
And swaggie “ potes ” have painted red
The blanket which was blue ;
But not a bardlet yet has shed
His light upon the Moo.
In “ fancy’s realms ” then let her graze,
And on your altars vow
The future source of Austral lays,
And laurels for the brow
Shall be, through all your singing days,
Your own Australian cow.
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
The Recognition of the Native.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was morning. I was leaning over the white
railings of the wooden bridge that spans the creek,
smoking my pipe and watching a shoal of bream
swimming about in the clear water. A fish, I
reflected, always seems so much more at home in
the water than most of us are on land. So many
of us walk the earth as though we had not grown
accustomed to it. A fish is a perfect master of its
element. Poised between surface and bottom,
curving quickly or fanning the water in graceful
idleness, it moves with a flexibility of motion that
is perfect in its effortless ease. Now, look at the
fellow lurching down the road. He was born
and bred on the earth, like all of us, but
he is still a stranger to his native element. He
lumbers over it clumsily. If the soil were not
rock-ribbed he would sink through. ... I believe
he was drunk last night. ... He was—on closer
inspection I am sure of it. T
Presently he loafed up to me and asked if 1
lived in the township.
“ No,” I replied ; lam a stranger here.
“Seems to me as I am, too, mate, though I was
born here.”
Ilis teeth were irregular. Two, on the right
side of his mouth, were worn away by the pipe-
stem habitually held there. He was unshaven ;
his hair was long, black, straw-straight and the
tufts of it were ragged at the ends. Mis
moustache was like a housemaid s blacklead
brush. He chewed it at intervals.
I let him resume. “I haven’t been near the
place for nigh twenty year. Of course, I expected
to find changes, but' blow me if there s a soul in
the place that knows me.”
He spoke with so much regret in his tone that I
was interested, and inquired: “You left, then,
as a youth, I imagine ? ” . , ,
“ Yes, gettin’ on for bein’ a man. Ive humped
it a bit since, but I thought I should like to see
the township again, and ’ave a yarn with some of
my old mates, if there were any left.
“ And are there not any ? ” ,
“Well”—he took off his hat and scratched
his head, as an aid to reflection— Ive seen
several folks as I seem to know, but they don t
reckernise me. There was a woman uj> the road
as was cleanin’ her doorstep. I sez to er, Ain t
you Maggie Davis?’ but she guv a staie
at me. Rude ?my oath !—an’ then went in
an’ banged the door. Then there was a
man ridin’ a ’orse an’ leadin’ another, as 1
felt certain was a schoolfellow o mine.
*’ow are yer, Tom Perkins?’ I sez, but he only
sez, ‘Good day, mate’—civil enough, you under-
stand, but not reckernisin’ me—an rode on.
There was another woman, lead in two kicls to
school. So ’elp me never, I’ve kissed er ahun-
dred times when she were a gal. I stopped dead
in front of ’er, an’ dropped me swag, an sez
‘Mary ! ’ but she threw me a look fit to freeze up
an arteeshun bore, an’, afore I could say a word
to explain, hurried on. I might be a bloomin
Rip-Penny-Winkle, coinin’ ’ome after three years,
for all the reckernisin’l get.”
“Perhaps the hotelkeeper will know you, 1
suggested, and watched the effect of the remark.
“ No,” he said, with a laugh. “ Murphy would
’a’ reckernised me right enough, but e s dead, an
I don’t know the new bloke.
“ The policeman, of course, is new.
“Rather.” He laughed suggestively. Ibe
old one—Vs a sergeant now up Hamilton way, 1
think—would ’a reckernised me, I’ll take ray
bloomin’ oath, though we were never great pals,
’im an’ me. Through ’im I left the township—
leastways, it was through myself, you understand,
but me an’ ’im run up agen each other, an 1
cleared. Ah, them was lively times.
He was silent for awhile, and then, jerking his
head towards my canvas bag and camp-stool:
“Takin’ photos?”
“No,” I replied; “sketching.
“I’ll show you a pretty bit if you like, im
going along the creek to the cemetery. I want to
see where father and mother are buried. There s
a bend in the creek where we boys used to come
after ’possum and blaclcfish ; an’ there’s a big red
sandstone bank on t’other side where the swallows
used to build. Man, it is awful pretty, and Ive
often thought of it. If you want a good picture,
you can’t beat that spot. I’ve seen most o
Queensland, an’ a good part o’ the West, an New
South Wales since I left home, an’ have had a
look at some bits o’ scenery that folks would say
were fine—an’ so they were, I’m not denyin’ —but
somehow I always fancied they were nothing like
as pretty as that bend o’ the creek at home.
We were walking along the bank of the creek
as he spoke; for I liked his enthusiasm. I
wouldn’t mind givin’ a couple of bob for a photo
of that bend.” He looked at me as though wish-
ing me to understand that he was making a busi-
ness offer, and I had to assure him once more that
I was not a photographer. “Well, a picture o’
any sort would do,’’ he persisted. “ 111 give two
bob any day.”
He showed me the place. It certainly was a
beautiful corner, with its red sandstone bank
sloping down to a foreground of mossed rocks and
dainty ferns, shaded by silver gums most graceful
in form. I thanked him, and praised the place,
which pleased him. Then he went off to the
graveyard. ,
I stayed by the creek, sketching, reading,
dreaming, smoking, till the afternoon. T hen I
walked back to get a meal, when lo ! the first
thing I saw on approaching the hotel was my
friend of the morning coming out of it. He was
being shot beyond the bar door by the landlord,
who had one hand to his neck while the other
gripped the loose bulge of his trousers. He ca-tne
out with a great rush, arms snatching at vacancy,
legs sprawling east and west, eyes glaring, and
beer trickling down his chin. He and the land-
lord shouted together, the bar doors clattered as
they swung back, and in a moment the son of the
township was in the gutter.
He got up, swore, and shook his fist at the
hotel; after which he drew his torn shirt over his
uncovered mahogany chest and staggered down
the street. , . . , ,
A woman came out of a house behind me, ana
spoke to another woman who was walking along
with two children. Both women had witnessed
the unedifving incident. , , .
“Do you know who it is?” asked the woman
with the children. , , . . ..
“I do believe,” commenced the other, heavily
emphasising the “do.”
“Peter Callaghan come back, I m sure.
“Just what I was goin’ to say.”
“ The same old Peter ! ”
“ Same old Peter ! ”
A man rode along on a horse. Blest it ttiat
ain’t Peter Callaghan,” he said to the women.
We all watched him as he made his wav to the
bridge. Half-a-dozen men were by this time
sitting on the rail, smoking. Peter sparred up to
them. “Do ye reckernise me, ye dogs?” he cried;
“because if ye don’t” . He tapped one of
them on the chest, without any provocation.
“Same old Peter,” said the man on the horse.
The man who had been assailed sprang down
from the rail and grasped Peter. The wrestle
was only momentary. The returned native was
heaved into the air, over the railing, and into the
crcck.
Then the local constable, with whom the hotel-
keeper had communicated, came upon the scene.
Peter Callaghan scrambled up the bank a
drenched, miserable figure, but now compara-
tively sobered. ■, , _ „ ~
“Callaghan, my boy, Ive hearcj- of you, said
the policeman. . , „ .
“ Nobody reckernised me this mornm, he
gasped, while spitting the mud out of his mouth,
“but I meant this ere place to know I d come
back.” j .
“Is it a triumphant arch you wanted, with
‘ Welcome ’ on it in gold letters ? ” inquired the
constable. “Did you expect the brass band to
turn out to meet you?” Peter ignored the
sarcasm, and blew mud and grass out of his
nostrils. “I don’t want to lock you up, so the
best thing you can do is to clear out as soon as
you can. This place isn’t big enough for you,
Peter.”
“Well, all right, so long as they reckernise
me,” said the Native.

There is no doubt that if some beneficent being, with the power to grant our requests, were to
ask us to express our dearest wish and promise that it should be realised, nine out of every ten of us
would, without hesitation, elect to become millionaires. The other one-tenth, too, would be sorely
tempted to make a like choice, but would, probably, after severe mental conflict, conclude that a
million would be of little use without the capacity to enjoy the pleasures it would procure, and would
consequently decide that freedom from pain was the greatest boon they could ask. To the nine-tenths
nothing can be promised, but it is Within the power of a large proportion of the remaining tenth to
realise their desire, the sufferings of most of them being caused by 8

Gout
Neuralgia

Sciatica

and a radical cure in any of these cases can speedily be assured. One and all of the disorders specified
are but the effect of the failure of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER
bo efficiently perform their functions. These important organs, when acting healthily, deal with
and remove from the system, the poisons which inflict the pain and sickness.
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid
together with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce!
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either Weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proportion of
the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous, and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, G nit, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Blad^ dei Troubles, and Brights Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition
of the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and
let it stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust-like sediment, or if the
particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time
must be lost in adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but
more painful illness will result.
The Liver.— ln the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood
converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood*
gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble into
urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived* their
life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion
Biliousness, Anaemia, Sick Headache, and Blood Disorders.
The health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
trie kidneys to be affected, and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research directed specially to diseases of the Kidneys and
Liver was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure.
It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that it was necessary to find a curative agent which
would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver, these organs being so immediately associated
in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material, and after many disappointments, the medicine
which possessed the required action in the fullest degree was at length discovered. Warner’s Safe
Cure cures all diseases of the kidneys and liver, and, by restoring their activity, these vital
organs are enabled to rid the body, through the natural channels, of the urinary and biliary poisons
the presence of which, in the system are the cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Neuraigia, Lumbago, Back-
ache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders, Anaemia, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and General Debility. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all these disorders simply
by removing the cause of the disorder. This is the reason why cures affected by Warner’s Safe Cure
are nermanent cures. [qgj
A Voice from the City.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On western plain and eastern hill*
Where once my fancy ranged,
The station-hands are riding still
And they are little changed.
But I have lost in London gloom
The glory of the day—
The grand perfume of wattle bloom
Is faint and far away.
Brown faces under broad-brimmed hats,
The grip of wiry hands,
The gallops on the frosty flats
Seem dreams of other lands.
The camp-fire, and the stars that blaze
Above the mystic plain,
Are but the thoughts of vanished days
That never come again.
The evening star I seldom view
That led me on to roam ;
I never see the morning star
That used to draw me home.
But I have often longed for day
To hide the few I see,
Because they only point and say
Most bitter things to me.
I Wear my life on pavement stones
That drag me ever down—
A paltry slave to little things
By custom chained to town*
I’ve lost the strength to strike alone,
The heart to do and dare;
I mind the days I’d roll my swag
And tramp to God-knows-Where.
When I should wait I wander out,
When I should go I bide ;
I scarcely dare to think about
The days when I could ride.
I would not mount before his eyes,
Straight bushrnan gaunt and tan—
I mind the day when I stood up
And fought him like a man.
I mind the time when I was shy
To meet the brown bush girls;
I’ve lunched with lords since then, and I
Have been at home with .earls.
I learned to smile, and learned to-bow,
And lie to ladies gay,
But to a gaunt bush-woman now
I’d not know what to say.
And if I sought her hard, bare home,
From scenes of show and sham,
I’d sit all ill at ease and feel
The poor, weak thing I am.
I could not meet her hopeless ey< s
That look me through and through—
The haggard woman of the past,
Who once thought I was true.
But nought on earth can last for aye,
And, wild with care and pain,
Some day by chance I’ll break away
And seek the bush again.
And find awhile from bitter tears
The rest the bush can bring.
And hear, perhaps, with truer ears
The songs it has to sing.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
By Rail and Semaphore.
MR. JONES AT MARAETARA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Old Man read the telegram, and turned to
♦he Traffic Clerk in amazement.
‘ The Station-master,' Maraetara, has been
quite incapacitated by a badly-loaded truck of
firewood collapsing on him ; I never heard of such
a thing! ”
“ They do collapse sometimes, sir ! ”
He wants the Relieving Officer sent up at
once. Whom shall we send ? ”
Jones, I suppose ; he’s the best fighter on the
section.”
“The best fighter on the section ! What the
devil has that to do with running the station ? Is
this section a prize-ring, sir ? ” growled the new
Traffic Manager, with some asperity.
“Well, sir, the S.M. Maraetara is always a
fighting man ; the present officer in charge is
quite a pugilist. You see, sir, it’s a timber-
station, and the timber getters are very rough.
They never argue—they fight. At the end of the
month, when they are paid, two doctors and a
dispenser go up there for a week, and make
money.”
“Good heavens!—what savages ! ”
“They certainly are very rough, sir.”
“And this man Jones,” continued the Traffic
Manager, “is he a professional pug—does he
receive anything extra from the department for
being the boss bruiser of the section ? ”
“No ; but he’s a very good fighter, and many
of the professionals hereabouts decline to take him
on.”
“ H’m; to-day’s the 9th—he’ll probably arrive in
time for the tail-end of it. Perhaps you had
better notify Mr. Jones to stand by in readiness
to go to Maraetara.”
* ♦ * *
At 12.45 p m. the express steamed out of the
wilderness of fern trees, rimus and towering pines
into an open space of country where the bush was
visible only on three sides, while on the other the
view was bounded by a low line of hills with a
panorama of stream and lakelet lying between.
1 lie chrome-yellow railway buildings stood out in
bold relief in the foreground, and When the train
stopped Jones found himself at Maraetara.
Jhe scared-looking cadet, who shrank when
Jones spoke to him, led the way into the office,
and said the S.M. was in bed behind the letter-
rack and battery cupboard. Jones walked round,
and found a weird apparition gazing meditatively
at the fire with one eye, the bedclothes tucked
lghtly round his chin.
The apparition consisted mostly of sticking-
plaster, and had a large lump of steak fastened
over the right eye. It might have been a truck
tnat feu upon him, but he looked much more like
a man who had been fighting with three timber-
getters.
-The apparition explained that the truck of fire-
wood had. broken him all up.
A*’? you sure it wasn’t a bull kicked you ? ”
Said Mr. Jones, with a winning, affable expres-
sion.
The S.M. glared savagely out of the only eye he
could see with, and Mr. Jones went off to get the
express away.
* ♦ * *
Out in the crisp sunshine the bees were hunl-
ming; there was a scent of foliage, and a drowsy
stillness gave an atmosphere of heavenly calm to
the scene of frequent fights. Mr. Jones felt dis-
appointed So far, he hadn’t seen one warlike
individual. He began to think that Maraetara’s
reputation was bogus.
The landlord of the Railway Hotel was a mar-
vellously quiet man, and the few fellows lounging
around seemed anything but pugilistically in-
clined. After eating, Jones went to the smoking
room, and held a pleasant conversation with a
man in a blue shirt on the subject of timber-
getting.
The man in the blue shirt admitted screw-jacks
Were useful, but said they took up too much time
when Totara Jack was around. Totara Jack, he
explained, could shift any log, if there was some-
thing to plant his feet against, in which case he
just curled himself up between the log and the
stump of rock, straightened himself out, and then
either the log shifted or the other thing left the
ground.
Totara Jack, it was mentioned, was still in the
township, not having been able to tear himself
away from the charms of a woman who was badly
m love with him.
Mr. Jones determined to see this remarkable
man before he left Maraetara, and arranged for an
introduction. Then they had drinks, and Jones
went back to the station and received a bull-pup
valued at £65, consigned to the Hon. J. D. Break-
water, Rutaniwha. This valuable animal he
hooked by his chain to a bent nail in the wall.
A ballast train came in, and when the ganger
had got his time-table one of the men asked for
the afternoon off. The ganger was willing, and
the acting S.M. didn’t care a hang; so the train
went off, and the surface-man remained explaining
to J ones that he had reason to believe his wife
was receiving the attentions of a skunk known as
iotara Jack. He announced his intention of
wringing Jack’s neck.
“ But I thought Totara Jack was an abnormally
strong man—a dangerous man, in fact?”
“All skite ! He’s too muscle-bound to scrap.
111 fight him with one hand ; and I’ll out his
throat when I catch him talking to my missus
this afternoon ! ” And he ground his teeth with
rage.
“Well, if you’re going throat-cutting you don’t
leave this office.”
The surface-man breathed hard.
“I’ll be calm, boss; I will be calm!” he
repeated vehemently; “but, ’struth! I’ll kill Totara
Jack all the same. I’ll kill him calmly. I won’t
let my passions rise. ”
“ Shut up ! ” yelled Mr. Jones.
The man looked at the clock, and dived out of
the office. Hardly had he gone when a tall, hand-
some woman appeared at the parcel window and,
leering at the Relieving Officer, asked if the ballast
train had gone.
Being assured it had, she beamed on Jones, and
after ten minutes’ friendly conversation went off.
Mr. Jones watched her through the office
Window ; she was certainly a splendid animal,
and her regal walk fascinated him so that he
watched her till she disappeared among the
clematis-covered trees.
* * * *
The droning peace of the afternoon entered
Jones’s soul; he sat in front of the fire and read
a magazine. The little cadet was having an
afternoon off, the first since he joined the service,
for the S.M. sweated him dreadfully.
Suddenly the ears of Jones were offended by
horrible threats and blasphemy, shouted in tones
that travelled like the screech of a bull-Yankee
engine.
He got up hastily and through the window saw
the husband bounding towards the office at a
wonderful speed, and, yards behind, the biggest
man Mr. Jones had ever seen rushed along in
pursuit. The tall, handsome wife of the fettler
brought up the rear.
Mr. Jones chuckled.
The husband bounded into the office and begged
incoherently for protection.
“ Good Lord! I thought you’d have had his neck
wrung by this time ; but buck-up, man ! you and
I can surely lay him out; if we can’t we’ll get
Reid, the porter, to help us. and the three of us
ought to be able to eat him.”
. The husband responded to this stirring exhorta-
tion by scrambling into the battery cupboard
and lying down on top of the 70 odd jars of
rotting copper and zinc and putrid water. That
sort of electric battery doesn’t smell too nice at
any time, but when it’s disturbed !
Mr. Jones didn’t mind fighting a man a little
bigger than himself, but Totara Jack was not to
be thought of. So he locked the door and awaited
Jack at the parcel window.
‘“’Day, mister! ’’said Jack, civilly enough.
“’Day !” said Jones, shifting his cigar with his
left hand and knocking the ash off it with the
ruler in his right hand.
. “Pass out the bloke inside; I want to tear him
in three pieces,” said Jack in a confidential tone
of voice.
“ What for ? ” said Jones.
“ He’s chucked aspersions on his wife’s charac-
ter, a.n’ I won’t have it,” fiercely.
“ What are you to his wife—a relation ? ”
“Naw, but I’m not goiu’ to ’low that white-
livered sneak to take away her character.*’
“You’re quite right!” said Jones; “but I
don’t think I d better let you have him; you
might kill him and get yourself and the lady into
trouble.”
Look here, mister. Don’t you try to go for
to stop me. I’m goin’ to choke him with his own
words ; so just you pass him out, or I’ll come in
an’ take him. T can pull this yer buildin’ down
if I wants to—d’yer understand ”
‘Well, you’re not coming in here without a
fight, anyhow !”said Jones, savagely.
“Well, mister, if it’s fight yer lookin’ fer,
you’ve come to the right place to find it.”
Well, you’ll only get yourself into trouble,
and that’s what I want to keep you out of.**
“Your troubles about me !” said Totara Jack,
commencing to climb in through the parcel window
in a cold blooded, matter-of-fact way.
In just as cold-blooded and matter-of-fact
a manner Jones smote him across the head with
the black ruler, and he rolled off the parcel counter
on to the floor of the shelter-shed with his coun-
tenance badly broken.
He looked at Jones with a gloomy eye, while he
wiped the blood with his sleeve.
“That Was a, real ‘ wattle-snoozer,’ all right,
Mister! ” he said, in a tone that seemed to Jones
quite friendly ; “ but it’s no good ; I’m cornin’ in
fur that cove right now.”
The woman said nothing, only stood in a bent
position, with her hands between her knees,
intoxicated with delight at the sight of battle.
Totara Jack came with a rush, and Jones
swung up the heavy ruler, but Jack put his
hand On the counter and lightly vaulted in, feet
first. Before the charge of these mighty clay-
stampers Jones recoiled, and Jack landed on his
feet inside the office. But, before he could regain
his balance, Jones had seized him round the
knees and thrown him down on top of the bull-
pup, and that noble animal had bitten him twicb
before he had time to swear. As he sprang to his
feet he met the ruler once more, but Jones
missed the point for which lie had aimed, and
Totara Jack was saved from annihilation. He
staggered to his feet, and as he did so the bulldog
bit him again.
With something between a shriek and a shout
he kicked the dog heavily in the ribs and upset
him, and at the same moment he took a double
turn of the chain round his hand, and with one
mighty wrench tore the hook from the wall and
again dodged the black ruler. Then he assumed
the offensive. He swung the struggling bulldog
at the end of the chain, and lashed at Jones with
the animal.
Against this astounding weapon the black ruler
was useless. Jones side-stepped and ducked as he
had never done before, but it was all in vain. The
south end of the dog crashed down on his face and
chest, and sent him to the boards with a bang.
But he was up again in a flash, and once more
seizing Totara Jack by the knees tried to fling him
on to the stove.
Jack missed it, but the dog didn’t; though it
could have made no difference to him—if he wasn’t
dead he ought to have been. Jones rushed in to
finish thefight with thornier, butthedog smote him
heavily in the ribs and knocked him down, and
the boss fighter of the section realised he had met
a tougher man than ever before.
He tried to close with the giant once more, but
the long fellow shortened his stroke, and with a
blow over the head laid Jones right out.
Next minute a series of swears and howls,
accompanied by the crashing of much glass,
scared the bruised S.M. next door and sent his
wife into hysterics. Even Potter Reid at the far-
end of the platform, cleaning the main line points,
heard the crash of the battery as the intruder
dragged the injured husband out and spilt the
battery soup over the floor.
He banged the injured one with the dead dog,
and the husband rolled round the floor trying to
escape the blows, and was nearly drowned in the
soup. A playful mood came over the victorious
timber-getter, and finding three quart-bottles of
assorted inks he amused himself coloring the
injured husband in lurid half-tones. Then he
poured some over the face of Jones, and that
slogged-out individual opened his eyes, looked at
the dog, and tried to collect his thoughts.
Jack’s novel weapon tickled his fancy and he
regarded it critically as it made a dull, meaty
impact with the husband. That injured individual
was absolutely the noisiest man who was ever
walloped with the remains of a £65 bull dog, and
his yells roused Jones, who, with blood in his
eyes, suddenly scrambled to his feet, and to
Totara Jack’s amazement, attacked him at long
range with every portable thing he could lay
hands upon. The black ruler bounced off the
timber-getter’s head; ink bottles followed in
quick and unerring succession, and when Totara
Jack rushed at him Jones promptly fled round
the office and threw the furniture behind him.
Jack came down badly over the long, three-
legged stool which Jones tipped over right in
front of him.
Round and round the ofßce they went. He hit
Jones between the shoulders with the dog, and
Jones retaliated with the P 26 book in the eye.
The P 26 book was large, and the corners sharp,
and Jack dropped the dead pup and gripped his
eye with both hands. Like a flash Jones turned
and banged him heavily, knocking him against
the wall (the woman’s face, framed in hair like
burnished copper, now beamed at him through
the parcel window), but he was afraid to give
Jack a chance to get to close quarters. The
timber-getter renewed the chase, and the other
did more excellent foot-work. Reid, the rat-like
porter, scrambled in through the window, and
caught the giant round the legs. Jack tried to
slay him with one blow, but missed, and Jones
hit him 16 times almost simultaneously.
Suddenly Jack bounded about three feet into
the air and shook Reid off—that ferocious little
man had bitten him on the leg. Reid fled out of
the window again. The injured husband had
disappeared long since through the same orifice.
The <£G5 bulldog belonging to the Hon. J. D.
Breakwater was quite dead. The tall woman
cavorted up and down the platform in ecstasy.
Inside, Jones and the timber-Goliath again hustled
round the room at great speed.
Then a weak man of no account came and ended
the battle. The incapacitated S.M. crawled to
the window, looked round desperately for a gun,
or a brick, or some other weapon, then in despera-
tion took the steak off his eye and threw it feebly
at Jones’s enemy. Jack trod on the greasy sub-
stance, slid wildly across the room and came down
heavily. When he recovered a second or two
later, Jones was standing over him with a kettle
of boiling water, which the thoughtful porter had
handed in through the window.
“If you move,” said Jones, coldly, “I’ll spill
this on you.”
Jack said he was a peaceful man, and all he
wanted was complete rest. He even offered to
pay for the bulldog.
C. A. JEFFRIES.

“ Ami.” : Dan Barry made two great coups as a juvenile
newspaper man. He got out an extraordinary in Hobart
about the Russian bombardment of Adelaide, and another
ia Adelaide about the Russian bombardment of Hobart.
Also a Melbourne sensation, in a Sunday paper called
Gossir, about Ballarat caving in and entombing 400
people.
“Nar-Rooni” writes: The Australian girl may not be
able to capture London crowds by her singing, but she
seems to be fully able to capture the London press by
shaking the smallest grain of salt on its tail. “ M.A.P.”
of July 18 has a par which describes Miss Kate Rooney as
being “ a grand horsewoman, an excellent shot, a
champion swimmer, a splendid all-round athlete, and she
can throw a boomerang like a blackfellow.” Now I
have the pleasure of knowing Miss Rooney intimately,
and I can guarantee that she is a fairly good swimmer ;
but she had no athletic training, she never fired off any-
thing more deadly than a “ hunger,” and she can’t throw
a boomerang. I would be sorry to know a woman who
attempted such a thing. It wouldn’t be safe. The lovely
sex should practise first with a brick at a fowl. *
Hamlet” : “ L. 8.,” on Australian drama, among other
things, said that Australian managers would only be
willing to pay £5OO or £lOOO for a play equal to “ If I
Were King” and other such stuff if it were offered them
by an Australian. Would they? If a manager won’tread
your plays how is he to know whether they are good or
not? How often do our leading managers produce an
unknown play by an unheard-of author? Most of the
plays we see are those that have run successfully in Eng-
land and America. Our managers, as a rule, watch
London managers experimenting with new plays, and
those that are successful in England they buy up, and
produce on their London reputations.' Our leading
managers are doing practically nothing for our
Australian playwrights. The limited stock of
Australian drama we have is, for the greater part*
melodrama. Years ago The Bulletin stepped into
a long vacant position and assisted Australian litera-
ture; now the struggling Australian drama is
waiting and watching for a wealthy manager to feed it,
encourage it, and raise its tone. Despite "‘A Tired Aus-
tralian,” there is material around us to build comedies
upon—dramas, too, and, maybe, operas. Alfred Dampier,
who has done more for Australian drama than any other
manager, in an interview in Adelaide some months ago,
expressed the opinion that there was a great future ahead
of Australian drama, and a growing demand in England
for things typically Australian. If it were in Dampier’s
power, he would pay big prices for Australian workman-
ship of this class, but it is not. Still, he is doing his level
best for the cause, which is 100 per cent, more than our
bigger managers are doing.
Elizabeth Howard, from 40 to 30 years ago the greatest
of “circus queens,” and by many old hands reckoned the
most daring of all sawdust-performers, died a few weeks
back in New Orleans, where for years past she had been
a street paper-seller. She is said to have been the first
“star,” male or female, to tumble through paper hoops
while her horse was at full gallop.
Charles Cartwright, the actor with the mighty metallic
voice (of whom a Melbourne penwoman once wrote that
“he hasn’t a hair on his body”), is said to give a very
pathetic performance of Daniel Peggotty in the new
dramatisation of “David Copperfield.” Miss Agnes
Thomas plays the old maiden aunt, Betsy Trotwood, in
the same show. Time was when Agnes Thomas played
young girls in farcical comedy for the Firm, or smart
heroines in Bland Holt’s dramas. She came out to
Australia with Dion Boucicault the elder, and used to
capture the heart of Titheradge in “ The Shaughraun”
19 years ago, or thereabouts.
John Coleman, an older actor than Johnny “ Scare-
crow” Coleman, has been writing a book about the late
Charles Reade, in which appears a cold-blooded confession
of “ British fair-play ” as practised by one of the most
serious-minded of English dramatists and novelists.
Reade told Coleman the story of how he came to adapt
» The Ladies’ Battle ” from the French. He was in Paris
when the original was first played, and he stole it right
away. “No British brigands were present, so it occurred
to me as an inspiration to play the bandit myself.
Obtaining a copy of the play, I made way back with my
booty, and immediately set to work on the adaptation. I
am ashamed to say I never thought of paying Scribe or
LegouvA or even asking their permission to do the piece.
It was so customary then to steal from our neighbors.”
But when his own novel, “ It’s Never Too Late To Mend,’
was being pirated for stage purposes by the hack authors
of obscure theatres, Reade rushed to the law-courts and
started litigation, which ultimately settled the law of
dramatic copyright in his own country.
The last Charing Cross Hospital Gazette (London)
states that Margaret Howard, aged 76, a vagrant recently
treated there for an injured shoulder, claims to have
been once a leading lady in Henry Irving’s dramatic
company. It is believed that she is a well-known actress,
under a disguised name. Evidently a lady of education,
she was able to repeat long sketches of her theatrical
characters.
“ B.”: “Are You a Mason?” before it reached the
English stage, was a German farce. Traces of its Teutonic
origin are writ large upon the piece, for the German is as
good at writing farces as an elephant is at playing the
violin, or Bent at walking the tight-rope.
Labouchere was one of the members of the House of
Commons who proposed to bring stage children under
the Factories Act— i.e., abolish them altogether from the
profesh. Ultimately the minimum age of the stage kiddie
was fixed at 10' years, which is four years more than the
age of Ellen Terry when she played a fairy in “ Mid-
summer Night’s Dream.” A London paper, commenting
on Labouchere’s speech, hoped that his wife had some-
thing to say about it after he got home. Mrs. Labby—
nie Henrietta Hodson—started her stage career as a mere
baby, like Mrs. Kendal and most other actresses born of
theatrical parents.
A clairvoyant gave exhibitions of future-reading capaci-
ties in the Mechanics’ Institute of an up-country town, a few
weeks back, and answered queries with the usual facility
and accuracy. Amongst the members of the local Ranger
detachment were Corporal O’Toole and his hated rival for
military promotion, a wizened warrior named Smith.
O’Brien, who loved neither of them, wrote out a question,
“ When will O’Toole become a general?” and signed it
“Smith.” Question was read out from the stage,
but never answered. There was a roar like that of an
infuriated bull, a mad charge, a shout of “ What
the devil d’ye mane by axing that question, ye
crawlin’ cur?” then biff, biff, clench, roll, and a
wriggling mass of humanity surged towards the door, the
combatants locked in the centre of the knot. Ten
respectable citizens tore the farm-burners apart just in
time to save the empire from being torn to pieces
